The RED PAGE
THE NEMESIS ON NATIONS
IN analysing the causes of the decline
and fall of the nations of the ancient
world, W. Romaine Paterson, in The
Nemesis of Natiotis (Dent, per Syd-
ney Book Club) justly lays emphasis
upon the basis of wholesale slavery upon
which their world-wide splendor was un-
doubtedly reared ; but the author seems
to have minimised several other con-
tributing factors, as is apparent when we
consider the disintegration of medfieval and
modern States, into whose composition
slavery -did not largely enter. However, the
author promises us another voluipe, wherein
he purposes tracing the transformation of the
world's social basis from slavery to serfdom,
and from serfdom to poverty ; so that, with
this wide definition -of slavery, he • may
yet demonstrate the universality* of his
thesis. But nations, like individuals, ripe
and rot; like all other living organ-
isms, they have their allotted span, of
existence : they are born and grow
old and die. In the case of the individual
medical science c m postpone that death ;
and in the case of nations statesmanship can
for a few centuries hold at bay their inevi-
table end ; but the cause of decay in both
instances is not exterior ; it is due to changes
within the organism that are the direct con-
sequence of the length of the period of its
existence. When a nation, or a man, grows
old, each loses not only the power, but the
will, to combat the seeds of decay within.
And each, when the end comes of old age, is
usually glad to die.
Still, in his survey of the decline of ancient
nations, Paterson has graphically shown one
cancer that attacked them all, and, helped
by other circumstances, at the appointed
time destroyed them. In attempting to
p<mi-tray the vital world that lies behind
Oriental and classical scholarship, the' 1
writer tells us he determined, as far as
possible, to see that world not so much from
the top as from underneath—and the roots
of that beautiful garden drew nourishment
from a vast and -hideous substratum of
slavery. And in each of his four examples
of ancient States, Hindustan, Babylon,
Greece and Rome, he finds in their social
basis “ a fearful monotony ” —the omni-
present slave. For reasons, the author has
omitted Egypt from his survey. He there-
fore begins with the entry into India through
the Khyber Pass of a white Aryan race.
Even to-day in the Himalayan valleys we find
tribes speaking Aryan dialects and possessing
white skins, ruddy complexions, and often flaxen
hair. There is the clearest evidence that the
people who became Indians were proud of their
racial color, which, however, was gradually lost
under the sun of the tropics In the Yedie
hymns, which chronicle their victory, there is
constant scornful reference to “ the dark race,”
and Indra, the Aryan sky god, is described as
fighting against that race on behalf of his “ white
friends.”
The author finds an economic justification
for slavery at the period when civilisation
was just beginning. It was necessary for
men to unite in order to cope with nature
and clear the earth for a human settlement.
Thus freedom —the freedom of the wandering
savage or the wild beast—was restricted,
and social subordination necessary. But
subordination would be followed by insubor-
dination, and there began that conflict of
wills of which history is the mere record.
Slavery was the first rude discipline in that
combined labor which became necessary if man
were to be capable of holding the place which he
had already won in the world. It is really doubt-
ful whether the foundations of industry could
have been laid in any other way... . Given
immense hordes oscillating over the earth's
surface, plundering like animals, and already
the slaves of instincts not yet human, we may
well ask bow order was to be brought out of such
a chaos. Civilisation begins with the crack of
the slave whip. It is the first frantic effort of
the race to organise itself.
Beneath the splendor of Babylon and
Greece and Rome, the author affords us vivid
glimpses of the horror of that universal ser-
vitude. He shows us how great wars were
undertaken solely for the purpose of gather-
ing in the hordes of slaves necessary to
build those titanic cities of the past; how
the trade in slaves was approved and regu-
lated by the State, how their growing
numbers periodically threatened the exist-
ence of government, how the minority of
drones that were their masters instituted the
most elaborate precautions against a combina-
tion of their laborers, and how, at last, these
millionaire slave-owners met the fate of all
social parasites. Behind the glitter of Greece
was a dark and abiding background of hate
and suffering. It was in the hells of her
slave-worked silver mines, and in the torture
dungeons of her law courts that we find the
foundations of that brief splendor. Pater-
son’s account of the methods of working and
the extent and the richness of the silver mines
at Laurion is highly interesting ; for Athens
owed, not merely her commercial but her
political greatness to these mines, where the
chain-gang toiled, lived their brief lives of
misery, and died. The silver was so pure
that it became famous, and other Greek
States bought it for their own coinage.
Athens became the silver market of the
eastern Mediterranean. Behind the glorious
facade of Athens we discover a va,st in-
dustrial tyranny, beneath the Panthenon
workshops full of manacled slaves. Paterson
concludes that the culture of Athens was
bought at too high a price.
The programme of making wars mere
slave-catching expeditions was carried out
in her turn by Rome. First she ravaged
Italy, making slaves of the vanquished
peoples of her own stock; and then she
reached out to enslave the world. Every
new conquest opened up a new human
market. Slave - merchants followed the
Roman armies, and sales on the battlefield
were superintended by the State.
Often, indeed, the chief interest in a war
centred upon the kind of slaves who would be
brought to Rome. Thus we find Cicero complain-
ing that the slaves whom Caesar would lead cap-
tive from Britain would be illiterate;
&
So the author draws his terrible moral.
The economic‘basis of all ancient States was
the slave-market. Slaves formed the wealth
Of nations and in turn their nemesis. Given
a great and continuous supply of slaves, the
capitalist carried on his work uncontrolled.
In ancient States there was not even a pre-
tence of a genuine equation between labor
and capital. And here Paterson tardily ad-
mits the collusion of other destructive forces
—especially that decay in the character of a
people that comes when a State grows old.
We have seen that communities started their
corporate life with a great store of energy, which
expressed itself in a vital religion, a vital agri-
culture, a vital art, arid, above all, in a vital mili-
tary activity. . . . Meanwhile, as wealth flowed
in, a struggle,for its equal distribution began
within the State’s own borders, and it became
articulate in frequent and violent oscillations of
the body politic. The national self-consciousness
was. now permanently divided, or it . was united
only in face of an invader. ... The community
had become lop-sided; power and property
had fallen into the hands of the- more energetic
citizens, while hunger and misery became the
hereditary burden of the masses. But both of
those opposed factors produced the same result,
for luxury undermined the virility of the rich, and
poverty undermined the strength and character
of the poor, and slavery broke the spirit Of the
working class. .. . The wealth that had poured
in began to pour out in the purchase of super-
fluities, and native industry became unproduc-
tive. .. . There comes a day when the State finds
itself moving on an inclined plane. Sometimes,
as in the case of Greece, the descent was
rapid and violent. Sometimes, as in the
cases of Babylon and Rome, it was prolonged,
and many centuries heard the rumblings of
their dilapidation. It is extremely remarkable
that almost every State appears to record uncon-
sciously these symptoms of its own decline, as, for
instance, in inferior and stagnant art and litera-
ture, and above all in the loss of its religion. . ..
It is wrong to suppose that mere political develop-
ment can save a State. One of the lessons of
history is that great social abuses go on accumu-
lating while men talk politics. ... A fighting
force, however efficient, if it be detached from
the nation, is no proper bulwark. The victories
of Stilicho and of Actius, however brilliant, did
not save a people who were content to fight by
proxy,,and had begun to mutilate themselves in
order to escape military service. Further, as a
disintegrating influence we should observe the
tendency of property, and especially of property
in land, to" become vested in a few families. . . .
In contrast with all other things, wealth gravi-
tates upwards. ... A wholly hostile relation sub-
sisted between capital and labor, and this
permanent deadlock involved economic sterility.
There was an utter absence of that co-operation
without which a State remains an artificial and
highly dangerous organisation. . . . Accurate lists
of kings, archoris, and strategoi, consuls, pro-
consuls and tribunes, will never enable us to see
the unrest of those vanished States. Even their
art, their literature, and their religion are lame
guides, because modes of thought and expression
change and beliefs die. But labor lives. ... It is
the continuity of human work which binds ages
together. . . . If we study ancient nations from
within, we discover a society governed by intimi-
dation. Their combined rivalries, like the rival-
ries of'modern States, pressed most heavily on the
poorest class, and involved an immense but futile
activity.
From such a valuable an.d thoughtful work
as this, those who are concerned about the
future of Australia may deduce many an in-
structive generalisation affecting the health
of nations. It is from a study of the past
that we have the surest hope of predicting
the future.
We look with interest for the announce-
ment of the companion volume, dealing with
modern States, promised us by the author.
♦

the R./\., with a large portrait group. Among
other commissions, and lucrative black-and-white
work, he is now engaged upon the portrait of a
daughter of Lord Lansdowne.
ANOTHER AUSTRALIAN KIPLING
“ It breathes the freedom of Colonial life in the
open, and some of the London reviewers have
gone so far as to describe Mr. Eden as the ‘ Aus-
tralian Kipling.’”—Extract from publisher’s
letter to the unprotected reviewer.
With this stimulating letter of introduc-
tion we receive Mr. Guy Eden, and his
Bush Ballads (Sisley’s, -London) with unkind
suspicion. And a reading of his verses
enables us to qualify the publisher’s phrase.
Guy Eden is not an Australian Kipling ;
he is a cockney Paterson. Eden writes—
in Paterson-and-water—of “The Old Grey
Mare,” and “Biralla Station Ball,” and
“The Man who came to Burrambeit,” and
“The Cove that Held the Ace,” and “The
Cove as Wears a Beard,” and there are odes
to Carbine and to Trumper ; and it is all
nicely rhymed, and swingingly stanzaed and
long drawn out and innocuous. The yam of
“The Old Grey Mare” is told in twenty-six
verses, disfigured by bathos such as this i-f-
But away in the corner old Billy,
Forgetting all else that was there,
Was quietly standing, and sobbing
With his arms round the neck of his mare.
While in a threnody over King Billy, Eden
opines that :
Surely some day when the story of life is told,
Angels will weep o’er the last of his race !
Most- of the yarns begin in the old time-
honored way.
Ridden a bit! I should think I had. ...
■ But there was a time—don’t mind me, sir,
I’m talkin’ of long ago.
And many of them end with the. same time-
honored bathos ;
I’m getting old and weary, and the end is near, I
know.
Will my darling never call me ? Hark, that music
soft and low.
Can it be? Oh God, it is, for now she calls me,
and I go
To meet her where the stars are softly shining!
Occasionally Eden’s humor mitigates his
weariness, and in “Sydney Harbor” and
“A Bush Conversation ” he just manages
to touch the real thing. But on the
whole, his book is a poor echo of a phase Of
Australian verse into which, by this time,
the white ants have got. In fairness to Guy
Eden, we append liis best effort :
Norman Carter’s realistic portrait of the ex-
Lord Mayor of Sydney in his robes is a highly
creditable piece of work. The portrait of Alderman
Allen Taylor is an excellent one; but for future
mayors the City Surveyor, or some other high
municipal official, should design a pair of
mediaeval and picturesque boots to complete the
costume. Beneath the gorgeous gewgaws of office
that adorn the brilliant-hued robe appears a pair
of boots, and the mayoral boots are just boots.
It seems a pity that convention should compel a
shrewd and likable personality such as Mr.
Taylor’s to go down to posterity in a form of
garment that modern civilisation uses only when
going for its bath. However, the personality is
there.
In a shilling book, Dr. Joe Cassidy (George
Robertson), Cecil Bramley, which is evidently the
pseudonym of a Melbourne medical man, depicts'
the career of a doctor from his student days in a
London hospital to suburban practise in Mel-
bourne, and ultimately to his installation in
Collins-street as a successful surgeon. To literal
turethe yarn modestly makes no pretences ; but
as an interesting and convincing story of the
medical profession it will be read with pleasure
by, at least, the members of that profession.
“ The editors will not derive any pecuniary re-
ward from the enterprise,” dolefully remark the
editors of The Trident, a little trilingual monthly
magazine, unillustrated, sponsored by T. C.
Lothian, Melb., price Id. In this “journal of
modern languages and literature,” there are book-
reviews and articles in English, French and
German. We fear that the editors have some
prescience.
A BUSH CONVERSATION.
Jim lightly rests upon his axe
As close at hand he hears a sound.
Then mopping up his heated brow.
With languid gaze he tjirns around ;
’Twas Tim, the stockman, standing by,
“ Good morning,” grunts old Jim to Tim ;
The other slowly shifts his pipe.
And grunts, in answer, “Mornin’, Jim.”
Then Tim, the stockman, looks around,
And gazes long on tree and sky,
Jim, likewise, throws his eyes aloft,
And gently heaves a tired sigh ;
Tim pauses in his wayward gaze,
And rests his eyes upon one spot—
“ It’s bloomin’ ’ot,” he says to Jim ;
Bays Jim, in answer,. “Bloomin’ ’ot !”
A magpie chatters in a tree',
A horse-fly comes to buzz .around,
Tim’s mare just whisks an angry tail,
The horse-fly sinks upon the ground ;
“ Woa, mare! ” says Tim, and then to Jim,
11 The fly’s a worrit, Jim,” he said.
Jim gurgles back a slow reply,
“It won’t no more—it’s bloomin’ dead ! ”
The sun climbs slowly up the sky, .
As Tim sits there and idly waits ;
Jim once more heaves a gentle sigh,
And thoughcfully expectorates;
Tim gently prods the sleepy mare,
Who vaguely wonders what is wrong ;
“ Bo long,” comes floating on the air.
An echo answers back, “ So long !”
GOBRT AND NEW YORK
Maxim Gorki, whom pious New York hounded
out of America on the grounds of alleged immo-
rality, gets his revenge on that moral community
in the Monthly Ilcneio. His article, “New York :
the City of the Yellow Devil,” is a most masterly
indictment of American civilisation, presented with
the sure literary touch of genius. “ One realises,”
he says of monstrous New York, where freedom is
lacking, and houses grow high because land is
dear and artistic taste low, “ that in these great
gaols dwarfed people live dull lives. No flowers
nor children are seen at the windows. From afar,
the city seems a great man with uneven black
teeth. It exhales clouds-of smoke, and appears
like a giant suffering from obesity. On entering,
you feel that you have chanced into a belly of
stone and iron, into a stomach which has en-
gnlphed millions, and which crunches and digests
them. The street is a greasy, I’avenous throat;
therein, somewhere deep down, float the town’s
working food-scraps—living people. Every-
where—over head, under foot, on a level—lives
and roars sinister iron, triumphing in its victory.
Evoked into life by the power of gold, inspired
thereby, it envelops man in its close meshes,
stuns him, drains blood and marrow, devours
muscles and nerves, grows arid expands, spread-
ing its chains even wider, reposing on silent stone.
Locomotives arid cars crawl like great worms;
motor horns screech like fat ducks; electric
wires wail grimly. The suffocating atmosphere
is permeated as a sponge with moisture,
with thousands of roaring noises. Packed in this
dirty city, grimed" with the smoke of factories,
man is imprisoned as in a gaol between high walls
covered with soot. He shudders apprehensively,
exhales foul odors in one’s face ; he has been
poisoned, is suffering and moaning. . . . Men’s
faces are immovably calm—it must be that none
feel the misery of being slaves to life, the aliment
of the city monster. With sad self-conceit they
consider themselves masters of their fate.”
Gorki notes that inner liberty, freedom of soul,
does not gleam from their eyes. In each coun-
tenance it is only the teeth that he sees. Their’s
is but “the freedom of blind instruments in the
hands of the Yellow Devil—gold.” Never before
has mankind seemed to him so insignificant, so
subjugated by life. All the people are silent ;
they have becorrie used to this violence without
object—accustomed to think that this represents
the object. Within the houses of the City of
the Yeliow Devil people quietly endure every-
thing that kills the man within themand turns them
into beasts. For the East Side, the compost-pit
of the city, Gorki has only a shuddering horror.
“ In these streets, packed with people as a sack
with grain, children seek eagerly in the garbage-
pails which stand upon the footway for rotten
vegetables, and devour them, mildew arid all, on
the spot, amid the acrid dust and exhalations....
At one in the morning, at two, and later, they
are still wallowing in filth, these 'wretched
blossoms of destitution, living reproaches to the
voracity of the wealthy.... Dirt is elemental. It
has pervaded everything; wails of houses; glass
of windows, the clothes of the people, the pores
of their bodies, their brains, desires, thoughts.. ..
They were as disease-creating microbes in the
city’s vitals, and the day will dawn when they
will poison it with the same virus upon which it
now nourishes them so liberally,”
By now, New York will perhaps be sorry that
it was so unkind to Gorki.
Readers of Jack London’s Before Adam, of
which, in its serial form, particulars have been
given on this page, will be interested to learn that
there is a remarkable superficial resemblance
between that story and The Story of Ah, by,
Stanley Waterloo, which was published in 1897.
Charged with plagiarism by Mr. Waterloo,
London practically admitted that he got his
fundamental idea from The Story of Ab. “If
you return to the proposition of similarity of
treatment, (he says), let me tell you that your
story and mine are as far apart as the poles in
treatment, point of view, grip, etc. Why, I wrote
my story as a reply to yours, because yours was
unscientific. You crammed the evolution of a
thousand generations into one generation—a thing
at which I revolted from the time I first read your
story. This situation strikes me as very funny.
Here I fall out with you because of your un-
scientific treatment of the primitive world, and
therefore write a reply, and then you say that in
six weeks I took all I knew from you.” Where-
upon The Bookman (American) comments that this
doctrine, if pushed to its logical conclusion, would
produce disastrous results. Any novelist who
chose to think the latest story by Mr. Howells, or
Mr. Kipling, or Jack London himself, unscientific,
would be free to adopt the plot, correct the errors,
and republish the amended story to his own
greater profit and glory.
Recently we put upon this page the name of 0.
Henry, as one of the smartest modern short-story
writers. Into that select category must now be
admitted the name of Leonard Merrick, author of
a series of delightful Whispers about Women.
(Unwin, per Sydney Book Club).- Merrick has the
knack of the short story, the allusiveness, the
humor, the “kick” in the denouement. By a
conscientious study of Henry and Merrick, the
local fiction-writer might improve his art, and the
local fiction-reader is promised good-entertain-
ment.
A travel-book, pleasant to read and well illus-
trated, is supplied by Beatrice Grimshaw. In
From, Fiji to the Cannibal Islands (Geo. Bell per
Geo. Robertson) the authoress, whose portrait,
quite in the manner of our local poets, serves as
a frontispiece, gossips interestingly of certain
little-known islands of the Pacific. Upon the
larger questions of polities and Imperial strategy
Miss Grimshaw has nothing illuminative to say ;
but her travel impressions are noted with an ex-
perienced and picturesque pen. Particularly in-
teresting is her description of Norfolk Island,
which lies so near our doors, and is-so little visited
by the Australian. The photographs add mater-
ially to the value of the book.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oblation.
All day the rain had teemed upon the earth,
Had poured in runnels down the glassy gums,
Or spouted arrows at the black cascades,
That beat defiance on their stormy drums.
But now the sun, post-hasting to the west,
Let loose a sudden gleam of wat’ry light,
As if that he relented to have borne
The world, so long, his grudge of jealous spite.
The broken track grew visible; its pools
Flamed ochre, like the wide-set hooded eyes
The rnopoke turns upon a traveller
Whom Night, descending, captures by surprise.
Then great Orion gave his massy belt
To girdle back the curtains for the Moon ;
And hidden angels, of their own accord,
Spread budding stars to glow around hershoon.
At last she came ; the proud, white-hornbd gem
Upon her forehead to the zenith raised,
As though to find the shy Endymion
For whom, on earth, her barren passion blazed.
The sun sank flaring; like a treasure-ship
Which some fierce brood of Adriatic slaves,
First having plundered of its wine and gold,
Lets loose in long red smoke upon the waves.
Anori there was no single sign to show
That it had ever been—unless it were
The giddy vortex in the ether made
By the displacement of so grand a sphere.
And Venus, with her sad autumnal smile,
Gazed thro’ the twisted windows of the trees,
Where leafy ladies chanted silver songs
In chorus to the thrush’s melodies.
B it mighty Mars, aloof and obdurate,
Pluckt at his harness and his sword undrawn,
While underneath his scarlet morion
His eyes absorbed such peacefulness with scorn.
And so, with all the great Hellenic gods
Arrayed on high, I lit a glowing jet
Of Eucalyptus boughs to give them word
That on this earth the gods are worshipped yet.
N.s.w.
HUGH McCRAE.
My Mate.
In dreams we meet, though dreams be few
Wherein your voice is clear ;
Awake, I go companionless
Down crowded roads in loneliness
Austere;
And yet, have I not longed for you,
My dear ?
My best can never quite repay
The craftsman’s full demands ;
I miss my utmost by as much
As means to me the inspiring touch
Of hands
Whose dreamland spell the workaday
Withstands.
What summit might not I attain
Upon illusion’s hill,
By your unaltered faith upheld,
By your unchanged belief impelled,
Until
Came conquest—might that dream we twain
Fulfil !
Alone, my makeshift best I try—
What matter, since I know
It serves its turn, and it is well
If in the market-place ’twill sell,
Although
Such guerdon mocks the dream that I
F orego.
In dreams we meet, though dreams be few
Wherein your voice is clear,
And still I go companionless
Down crowded roads in loneliness
Austere;
But oh, do I not long for you,
My dear !
Vic.
KETTLEDRUM.
The Reformer.
A shriek stabbed sharply into Slumber’s breast,
Her tender lips fell dully fro u my eyes ;
And half-awake, and fractionally dressed,
I soothed a man who vented horrid cr.es.
“I dreamed a dream,” hesiid, with eyes a-stare
“ A busy town I found myself within,
And to a multitude assembled time
I spoke in warning thunders of their sin.
“ At first T started on the traps that lie
To snare the godless where the glasses clink,
But like to thunder rose an angry cry :
‘ Within this city there are none that drink ’!
“Of dances, then, and theatres I spoke,
Wherein is evil for all such as seek ;
But on my warnings swift denial broke :
‘ We do not know the things of which you spa ik.’
“Then straight my tongue t',e sin of bitt'ng
spurned.
Upon this dreadful vice I placed my curse ;
The quick denial still the crowd returne 1 :
‘ There is no bettor here—all things are woV ,e.’
“ The great crowd glared at me, a ghastly gr.n :
I felt the limelight from my life hid gone.
It was a nightmare city, with no sin,
And I had nothing left to lecture on !
“It made me shriek.” And, laughing, forth I
went.
He called me back e’er I had fairly gone :
“Young man,” he cried, “ what sin is your intent
Roam.tig at night with such scant clothing on ? ”
N.N.W.

1/ ow to A ddre.ss Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the ot/icial
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, ivhere practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches

THURSDAY, JUNE 20, 1907.
HOW AUSTRALIA CAN BEAT JAPAN
AT SEA.
To attempt the discussion of the Australian naval
question with the average person is a painful pro-
cess. Not long since the writer observed casually
to an acquaintance that it was high time Aus-
tralia set about building a navy of its own. The
man so addressed simply glared in horror at the
suggestion. “Where’s Australia going to get
several millions to throw away on a navy ? ” he
demanded. Yet the cash laid out on the navy
need not be “thrown away” in any sense. It
appears to be the accepted theory of the vast
majority that the money so expended is to be
shovelled straight out of Australia—is, as far
as the Commonwealth is concerned, to be
money exported which we will never
see again. This idea comes from the
supposition that the battleships are to be
“imported ” (made in England, Germany, Japan
or Timbuctoo) and smuggled through the Customs
with faked invoices. And it is an idea that
makes every good Australian feel tired —very
tired. For it is quite possible to spend
£50,000,000 on a navy without the money being
“ thrown away ” in any sense whatsoever. To build
the Australian navy in Australia, from Australian
material, and by the hands of Australian work-
men, would mean keeping the money expended in
the country. It would certainly lead to great
industrial activity, besides aiding in the develop-
ment of “ our great natural resources.” Skilled
mechanics would be attracted to these shores from
other parts of the world, and that would lead to
increase of population, and mean more men
behind Australian-made rifles when the Monkey
or some other Asiatic or miscellaneous enemy
appears on the horizon. Also when we can build
battleships we will be quite capable of building
cargo and passenger steamers to carry Australian
produce to foreign lands, and that in itself would
be a profitable industry. So much for the
“ thrown away” superstition.
* * * #
Another point that the naval sceptic delights
to harp on is that, because Britain has been 300
years getting its navy to its present state, Aus-
tralia will necessarily take 300 years to raise a
decent fighting force, and consequently it is
utterly useless to make a start. This 300 years
appears to be a favorite period, though some talk
of 500 years, and some date from Alfred the
Great, and some from the year when the first
ironclad appeared on the waters. But in either
c ise the story ought to be in a lunatic asylum,
and the narrative should Le consigned to a home
of aged and decrepit myths. It implies, in the
first place, that we have no naval traditions as
Britain has, and therefore have got to start from
the bottom of the ladder—a long, long ladder
with its bottom step a great way down. This is
absurdly unhistorical. Australians helped to
budd the British navy from the time of the first
galley to the time of the ironclad; and Aus-
tralians fought in every British naval battle
from the time of Alfred the Great down
to Britain’s last great sea effort at Trafal-
gar. That is to say, our ancestors, who • are
dead, fought there just as much as did
J. Bull’s ancestors, who are also dead. Neither
set of ancestors could be any deader than they
are. And, with naval traditions as good as those
of Britain, there is no reason why we should not
do just as well as Father Bull on the sea.
* * * *
And the talk of the great period required to
create a navy is childish folly. Our father, Bull
—:the marine Bull —has built in 18 years the
greatest navy the world ever saw. More than
, that, he has built all of the said navy that possesses
any real fighting value in 10 years. Everything
dating back before 1889 is old junk now, and
everything dating before 1897 is middle-aged
junk. It is the same with Germany, and the
same also with Japan. And as time and decay
still go on, it follows that it will be in the future
as it was in the past, and that the ships launched
yesterday will almost certainly be obsolescent
in 10 or 12 years, and just about obsolete
in 17; they will be old junk by 1924,
and fairly middle-aged junk by 1920. So we
come to this point, that if Australia begins build-
ing Dreadnoughts, or improved Dreadnoughts,
and first-class cruisers and torpedo-boats to-day,
and keeps on steadily at the work, it will almost
certainly have, about 1920, if peace lasts so long,
the most up-to-date fleet, and, with good manage-
ment, the most efficient fleet on all the seas. That
fleet will be of better quality than the navies of
Britain, France, Germany, Russia, the United
States, or Japan. It will be the one navy in
’'existence with no tail to it, and containing not a
single ship over, twelve years old. In 1918 or
1920 the issue of a great naval battle will almost
certainly be decided by the vessels that are built
between now and the hour of the scrimmage.
Those that date back to last year and earlier
years will cost about as much for up-
keep as the newer vessels, and they will
require about as many officers and men, but they
will only be qualified to scramble on the outskirts
of the trouble, and tackle the old or middle-aged
vessels of the enemy. They won’t decide the
issue, or do anything very serious toward decid-
ing it. Granting that skill and valor are equal,
and that the fleet with a tail of antiquated ships
isn’t more than twice the strength of the fleet
with no tail, there are great possibilities for the
fleet that has no tail. And if we set to work in
real earnest now , the Australian fleet is likely,
10 or 12 years hence, to be the only strong navy on
the waters that really answers that description.
The point to which it is essential to draw the
attention of the helpless, the unpatriotic and the
despairing, is that THERE IS NO REASON
WHY AUSTRALIA, BY THE YEAR 1920,
IF PEACE LASTS SO LONG, SHOULD NOT
HAVE A NAVY WHICH CAN BEAT THAT
OF JAPAN SINGLE-HANDED. It is easier
for us to have that navy by 1920 than for Japan to
have it, always provided we can get a dozen or so
precious years of peace, and provided that we
make good use of the time. In the first place,
Australia has almost exactly the same revenue as
Japan, and, in proportion to its wealth, it has a
vastly larger reserve of taxing power. (Also, by
the way, it has a vastly greater reserve of taxing
power than Britain possesses ; this country is just
as rich per head as Britain, but Federal, State
and municipal taxes in Australia are about £1
per inhabitant per annum less than national
and local government taxes in the United
Kingdom.) Consequently it should be easily
possible for us, during the next handful of years,
to build ship for ship—first-class ironclads, cruisers
and torpedo boats—with the Jap. Then we are
free from a huge burden which the Monkey has to
carry. If the battle can be put off till 1920, by that
time the present Jap fleet will be practically out
of consideration —it will be obsolete or obsolescent,
and of little account ; yet it is costing Japan
£2,500,000 annually for maintenance. The fact that
we have no old fleet, which will be a mere tail in
1920, to support means that, if we can have peace
till then, we will have the money that these old
ships cost Japan for upkeep to spend on the con-
struction of new vessels. Consequently we could
build much more than ship for ship with the Jap.
The great point is that, if the war can only be
postponed till 1920, the Jap’s long start won’t
count at all, and the existing Jap fleet won’t come
into consideration except as a burden. It will
cost a lot of money between now and 1920, and by
1920 it will be just about dead. Therefore in
saying that THERE IS NO REASON WHY
AUSTRALIA BY THE YEAR 1920, IF
PEACE LASTS SO LONG, SHOULD NOT
HAVE A NAVY WHICH CAN BEAT
THAT OF JAPxIN SINGLE-HANDED, this
paper speaks on the strength of facts and circum-
stances that are as capable of demonstration as
the multiplication table. As for finding the 36,000
men to manage such a fleet as that of Japan,
Australia will have men enough and to spare if
it only develops its manufactures by a sane Pro-
tectionist tariff, breaks up its great estates to
make room for small settlers, and encourages
desirable immigration. The men can be got,
and the money is here already—more than enough
of it.
• * * *
Yet, so far, Australia has done nothing. It is
retarded by many different kinds of people.
There are the wormy little State politicians like
Carruthers, who object to the building of even
so elementary an item of defence as the Northern
Territory railway unless it can be done without
N.S.W. paying any of the cost; and the horde
of people—the Staples and Hodges and Huie
and Adams class of Secessionists who write to the
papers, and the Secesh papers that they write to
—that are so busy inventing mean slanders
against the Australian Government that they
haven’t time to think of Australia. There are
also the crazy “loyalists,” who have a floating
impression that it is revolutionary not to depend
on the Ma Country for everything, and that to
build a fleet strong enough to help the Ma Land
is something like insurrection. There is a frenzied
little Sydney mob which says that we should have
no fleet lest, if we have one, some of the ships
may be built in Melbourne. There are the people
who don’t want to pay for defence. There
is a crowd of well-meaning fools who talk
of asking the Hague Convention to guarantee
the safety and integrity of Australia, regard-
less of the fact that the Hague Convention
itself hasn’t a regiment or a ship to defend us
with ; that even if the countries it represents
cared to guarantee our security, that under.
taking would be worth no more than the solemn
guarantees given to Poland, Denmark, Turkey,
and at some time or another to almost every
other country; and that, in any case, it is
a cowardly thing to ask other countries, which
have no interest in our affairs, to fight our battles
while we loaf. Further, there are confused tacti-
cians, like Willie Kelly of the Motor Car, with
crude ideas about the uselessness of defensive
fleets, and wild ideas, derived from some Ameri-
can author, that the only successful thing in war
is to go out and look for your enemy as the
Spanish Armada and the fleet of Rozhdestvensky
did ; and who somehow hold that an Australian
fleet couldn’t go out, though a British one can.
There are silly, despairing people who hold that
unless we can at once have a fleet which can face
a first-class Power, we shouldn't begin to build
any fleet, just as they might say that unless we
can build a whole house on the instant, we
shouldn’t lay the first brick. Also there are the
multitudinous people who reckon that as nothing
has yet happened, nothing will ever happen,
quite forgetting that everything happens for the
first time some day. And there are very many
people who hold that the fleet should be built some
day, j ust as the Sydney-North Shore bridge has been
going to be built some day for over 40 years. And
among all these people the precious months and
years are slipping away; and the evil day is
coming nearer without Australia being any more
ready to meet it when it comes.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Can a Secessionist be a Christian?
Within the last few months an excited but busi-
ness-like woman has been asking whether it is
possible for an actress to be a Christian, and she
has let down her hair, and lighted up her eye like
a burning coal, and answered her own question
with energy in the negative. Now The Bulletin
asks the much more important question which
appears at the head of this item, and after serious
consideration it is inclined to the belief that, as
regards at least the kind of secesh person who
clamors in public, he can't. The secesh push which
declares itself openly in the press is only a very
small band, but it is vicious and vociferous enough
for a very large one. And on strict theological
grounds it is damned ; also there is nothing about
it to even faintly suggest why it shouldn’t be. It
starts out by being a huge, coarse, malignant liar
of vast proportions. Ananias has been held up to
obloquy for close on 1900 years, the charge being
that he departed from the truth once ; a single
letter in the Sydney Telegraph or S.M.
Herald or Evening News from one of the hand-
ful of prominent seceshers can, for straight-out
lying, beat the combined known records of
Ananias, Sapphira, and the Leper three
times over. This sort of thing must get in the
way of Christianity. Then the secesh person is a
wholesale and malignant slanderer. His lies are
not harmless or humorous ; they are virulent and
groundless charges of robbery, breach of faith,
and all manner of things, levelled wholesale at all
manner of persons. He is a stirrer-up of strife
among brethren. Also he is an unpatriotic Stink-
ing Fish, and an evil-minded cad who drags the
good name of Australia in the gutter of calumny
by describing it as if it were a huge Fagin
which lived only for the spoliation and de-
struction of the Ma State. An individual
who wantonly assails the, honor of his mother-land
is the same kind of ruffian who would call his
mother a harlot if he thought he could make 2d.
out of the transaction. These things all make it
difficult for the loud-mouthed secesh agitator to
be much of a Christian. Mostly, too, he is an
enemy of his own State, and the people of his
own State, as well as of the rest of Australia. He
is, in the main, a grasping person whose real
objection to Federation is that the Federal tariff
does a little to encourage N.S.W. industry, and
to find work and wages for the N.S.W. people,
and thereby does a little damage to his sordid
business of discouraging N.S.W. industry and
starving out N.S.W. workmen to make room for
imports. It is always difficult for an importer to
be a Christian. It is his business to object to the
native industry which supplies work and wages
for the Australian father, and food for the
Australian wife and kid, and which provides the
country with population, and makes it prosperous
and strong to resist attack; for all these good
things mean a lessened demand for his imports.
Therefore, he is the public enemy by profession
and occupation. And when the importer class
turns secessionist and anti-Australian, it is difficult
for it to be even a fragment of a Christian. And
the secesh person is sour and cantankerous, in
addition to his other sins. He is grudging and
mean, and is always trying to make new seces-
sionists by alleging that some other State may
possibly get 2d. ahead of “our” State. In many
cases he clamors for secesh on the ground that, if
N.S.W. doesn’t break away from the Union, it
will have to pay something towards the cost of
protecting the Northern Territory (our open back
door) against Asiatic invasion. In that case he
ranks as a sordid traitor—the worst, meanest, and
lowest traitor in all history ; one who would sell
our country, our civilisation, and our race to
Asiatic semi-barbarism in order to save his own
pocket the price of a few cigars. That kind of
secessionist ranks with the person who would give
his daughter in marriage to a gorilla to save the
price of the trousseau that might be required if
she married a human being. If such a secessionist
can be a Christian, he must needs do it with a
dead lift, and the chances are that the lift would
necessitate the employment of a crane. Alto-
gether, The Bulletin has an idea that it would
like to meet with a Christian secessionist, just to
see how he did it, and what he was like when he
had done it.
The Scream of one Adams.
Just at the moment the depressed and anxious
Secesh person who is keeping up the chief corres-
pondence in the daily papers is one J. L. Adams.
He isn’t quite the magnificent liar that some of
his species are, but he is a sorrowful and heart-
broken inhabitant, with a purely parish view of
things. He feels that under Federation N.S.W.
is at the mercy of the Commonwealth, just as the
other States are, and just as Nottinghamshire or
Berkshire is at the mercy of England, and just as
Parramatta is at the mercy of N.S.W., and Cook
Ward is at the mercy of the Corporation of
Sydney. J. L. Adams expends columns of space
over arguments which only amount to a protest
against everything that has been done in the way
of promoting order ever since the anarchical
monkey left his tree-top and began to make his
first attempt at social organisation. And the rest
of the vast waste and wilderness of words and
Secesh argument, with which J. L. Adams fills
columns of daily paper space, is devoted to showing
how much money N.S.W. might save if it broke
away from the Commonwealth. (It is amazing
how these little sedition-mongers talk about
imitating J eff Davis and his Confederacy, and
rushing into a policy which might cost £10,000,000
in money and thousands of lives, and how they
cavort round as if treason and breakage of the
Constitution wei’e a little tippety-tappety game
of ping-pong.) Adams’ figures are vastly exag-
gerated, bu t still the fact remains that N.S.W.
would save a little immediate cash by breaking
away from the Commonwealth. It would save
the cost of its share in the construction of an Aus-
tralian navy, and it would avoid its share of the
responsibility of garrisoning Australia’s most vul-
nerable point, the Northern Territory, in the in-
terests of the White Australia policy. If a great
Asiatic invasion comes this way—as it almost cer-
tainly will some day, when Britain is fully occu-
pied elsewhere—the Adams policy would avoid
the expenditure of some money, by taking no
notice till the trouble reached N.S.W. itself.
Then, of course, it would have to take notice. ,1.
L. Adams is firmly (and quite correctly) assured
that some outlay could be avoided in the imme-
diate present by letting Australia slide. In the
same way, supposing N.S.W. could secede from
Australia, and save money thereby, the inland
parts of N.S.W. could save some more money by
seceding from N.S.W., on the ground that they
are under no more obligation to pay for a mili-
tary force that is mainly required to defend the
exposed localities of Sydney and Newcastle from
attack than N.S.W. is required to defend the
Northern Territory from attack. In like
manner the inland counties of England have every
right to secede, on the ground that they would
thereby save their contribution to the cost of the
British fleet; they never see the fleet, and, being
inland localities, they don’t want a fleet. And if
Britain were half as grossly selfish an institution
as J. L. Adams, it would expressly intimate
to all whom it might concern that it would never,
under any circumstances, pay anythingtowards the
cost of defending N.S.W. against foreign attack,
N.S.W. being a great deal further from Eng-
land than the Northern Territory is from N.S.W.
J. L. Adams deserves a monument all to himself,
He is parochialism incarnate. He has plainly
never heard of Australia'. He has apparently
never heard of patriotism or national spirit. If
the British empire were made up of individuals
like him it would be split into 100,000 fragments,
each of the less exposed fragments busy saving 2d.
by refusing to contribute to the defence of the
more exposed fragments, and each in turn losing
its life and its carefully hoarded 2d. after its more
exposed neighbors went down. J. L. Adams’
ideal State is one in which Penrith wouldn’t worry
about the defence of Sydney, so long as the enemy
wasn’t in sight of Penrith itself; and Katoomba
wouldn’t begin to think of defence till the Asiatic
hordes had occupied Penrith ; and Bathurst, when
it heard that the invaders had burned Katoomba,
would merely remark that they were still a iong
way off, and some streak of luck might turn
up. It is difficult to understand how persons like
Adams are possible in this allegedly enlightened
age, but they are not only possible—they seem to
exist to the number of some dozens at least.
Carruthers, for example, isjust like J. L. Adams.
Food, Folly and a Rebuke.
At a mayoral banquet in Sydney, last week, the
good food was spoilt by a display of doubtful
taste and'some folly. There were two Admirals
present—the State Governor of N.S.W. and the
Admiral in charge of the Australian station—and
certain anti-Australians thought it a good oppor-
tunity to parade their horror at the thought of an
Australian navy. It was in doubtful taste, seeing
that neither Admiral is supposed to have any
“ politics ” in public. And as an effort to
curry favor with the British guests it was
foolish, being based on a complete misunder-
standing of the British attitude on this
point. Is is by no means the case that
the British people are with the local anti-
Australians in their opposition to an Australian
navy. The most influential of the London news-
papers agree absolutely with the Australian atti-
tude as expressed in The Bulletin, and so do the
most important of the ‘‘Service” journals, and
very many of the chief public men. The anti-
Australian who goes abroad to cry “Stinking
Fish ” about his own country usually does so with
an idea that, by making an offensive display of
himself, he will please the “ British aristocracy ” ;
his conduct only excites contemptuous surprise
in those who notice it at all. Something of
the same feeling fills the average Briton when
he hears an Australian arguing fiercely that
Australia is too poor a sort of place to have even
the beginnings of a fleet of its own, and that Aus-
tralians are too helpless and cowardly a people to
do anything of value for their own defence. Mr.
Pilcher, in parading his poor opinion of Aus-
tralia on this naval question before the Governor
and Admiral, invited a snub, and got it. Admiral
Fawkes, in a speech which will be read
with pleasure throughout Australia, deliberately
“ dissociated himself from those who thought an
Australian navy impossible. He believed Anglo-
Saxons would come out right in anything.” The
rebuke was polite but severe. Mr. Pilcher was,
in effect, publicly asked by the British Admiral
to think a little better of his own country, and
not to seek to curry favor with Britain by throw-
ing dirt and contumely at a community that is
mainly of British descent.
■Wanted, Another 80,000 Minimum.
When the N.S.W. State Government takes its
bogus referendum, professedly to find out whether
the people of N.S.W. are satisfied with the pro-
posal to have the capital of Australia beside the
magnificent Snowy River on Monaro, or whether
they would prefer to have it in a comparatively
dry spot where all the trade will go to the already
congested port of Sydney, it will, if it is logical,
require that at least 160,000 votes shall be cast
against the Monaro site before the existence
of any popular dissatisfaction is recognised.
Joseph’s present disturbance arises out of the
proviso that the capital must be in N.S.W. The
chance to insert that proviso arose solely through
the failure of the first Constitution Bill to become
law in N.S.W. And it didn’t fail on its merits,
for there was a majority in favor of the Bill; it
failed solely because the N.S.W. Parliament loaded
the dice by declaring that unless the majority in
favor of the measure numbered at least 80,000 votes
(only men had votes then) its decision should be of
no account and should be annulled. But for that
loading of the dice there would have been no
clause stating that the capital must be in N.S.W ~
and the present row couldn’t have happened.
Now consider the new position as compared with
the old one. In the first case the I ederationists,
whatever their majority, were disqualified unless
they could raise 80,000 votes; if there had
been 79,999 votes against provincialism and only
one for it, provincialism would have triumphed
all the same. In the present case the provincial-
ists claim, as an additional concession, that the
N.S.W. State Legislature should have the right
to choose the capital site, and in pursuance of
that claim, they make the astonishingly cheeky
proposal that a referendum shall he taken
to say whether in N. S. Wales more votes


can be got in favor of Dalgety than in
favor of all other possible sites put together.
And by way of going one better than their old
80,000 minimum swindle, they propose that unless
Dalgety gets more votes than- all other possible
sites put together it shall be rejected. All the
advocates of all the other sites will naturally vote
against Dalgety, just as all the advocates of all
other sites would vote against Yass or Lyndhurst
or any place; and on that basis, and by that
disreputable fraud, there will be a majority against
Dalgety just as there would be against any other
site if put to a similar faked test. The political
welshers who loaded the dice against Federation
nearly 10 years ago are thus just the same old politi-
cal welshers. It is up to the provincialists to show
that they believe in the fairness of their own
policy of 10 years ago by applying it to themselves.
On that previous occasion it was held that unless
80,000 male voters declared their dissatisfaction
with the existing order of things no dissatis-
faction would be recognised as existent. Now,
to make things level, it should be held
that unless 160,000 male and female voters
express their dissatisfaction with the exist-
ing condition of things—said condition being
that the Australian Parliament has decided that
a site by the Snowy River is the best one for a
Federal capital—-no one shall be officially aware
that there is any objection to Dalgety at all. Of
course, as the anti-Federalists of the Carruthers
Ministry sit in the place where the ballot can be
officially faked, and the ballot-box officially
stuffed, there is not the least probability of any
such fair deal as this taking place. Still it is dis-
tinctly the turn of the anti-Federalists to struggle
against an 80,000 minimum (which would be a
160,000 minimum under the new conditions), and
they would get a quite rare and unusual reputa-
tion for honesty by doing as herein suggested.
The Australian Mail Contract.
The Australian Government can be credited with
a rare degree of patience in its negotiations
with the unreliable English syndicate which
tendered for the Australian mail contract.
In the first instance the Government made
what appeared to be an excellent bargain.
It was, therefore, generally abused by the
Opposition and the Opposition newspapers for
daring to do anything which might advantage Aus-
tralia. That was the first phase of the matter. This
storm of abuse had hardly subsided when the
syndicate which had tendered began to show the
cloven hoof which the “Private Enterprise
syndicate usually has concealed in its boot, and
to uncoil from beneath its spotless frock coat a
curiously spiked tail. Possibly the syndicate,
having read the comments of the Opposition
papers in Australia, and noticed their bitterly
unpatriotic and anti-Australian attitude, calcu-
lated that it would be a good thing to squeeze
the Government a little further, as help
could be reckoned on from the Opposition in
any step which meant detriment to Australia.
Anyhow, the syndicate began to go outside its
signed bargain in search of further concessions.
Its new proposals to the Australian Government
practically meant that if the new line were to be
a success the contractors should enjoy all the
profits, but that if it were to be a failure Aus-
tralia would have to step in and shoulder all the
loss. That was a game of “ heads you win, tails
I lose,” which Prime Minister Deakin wasn’t
willing to play on behalf of Australia with any
syndicate. Then the mail service promoters
began to talk of extra guarantees. Failing to
get them from the Australian Government (which
in fairness could not give the successful tenderer
any special new concessions, to the possible detri-
ment of other unsuccessful tenderers), it has been
seeking them from the States, and, at latest, Vic-
toria was willing to give the syndicate special help
in return for special concessions for Victorian
producers. But obviously the Australian Govern-
ment has no right to sanction that. Under a
Federal contract, all the States must have fully
equal rights and privileges. There is, of course,
nothing actually illegal in the arrangement which
the Victorian State Government offers. Anybody
—a State Government, or a bank, or a private
individual, or a Rechabite lodge, or the Shah of
Persia—has a right to lend its money or its
guarantee to the syndicate. And when Victoria
asks, in return for its big cash guarantee, the first
claim upon 1200 tons of cargo space on each boat
for the carriage of its products it commits no
breach of any Federal arrangement; the Federal
arrangement is for a swift fleet of mail-ships, and
the distribution of cargo space isn’t mentioned in
the bargain. Moreover, Victoria offers to any and
every other State that likes to join it in the
guarantee a share of the advantages for which it has
stipulated. But despite all these points the arrange-
ment is wholly undesirable. Prime Minister
Deakin should hold the syndicate firmly to its
bargain ; if the bargain is not carried out, the
delay and loss occasioned will have to be entered
as a debit—as the price paid for experience as to
the ways of Private Enterprise syndicates,
especially foreign syndicates. Then negotiations
on a surer basis should be opened with Australian
firms, or the Government should decide to itself
undertake the work of organising a European
mail service. There is no reason for pessimism if
the syndicate fails to carry out its bargain. Aus-
tralia is big enough and strong enough to face the
situation.
The Australian Fleet.
“ Amphibious ” writes as follows :
With all the prestige of your enthusiastic endorsement,
you publish a letter from Dr. R. Arthur
The Bulletin interrupts here to say something
about the enthusiastic endorsement. Looking at
it now, it is quite prepared to admit that its re-
mark might be misleading ; but when it “recom-
mended ” Dr. Arthur's letter “ to the considera-
tion of all Australians,” it didn’t intend to endorse
his scoff at Captain Creswell, or his idea of
training our sailors in old ships and postponing
the creation of a real fleet for 10 years. But it
did intend to express its whole-souled admiration
for almost the only politician we have who
preaches at all times the need for population and
the urgent necessity for a real defence system.
After this interruption, “ Amphibious ” may
resume.
——containing the following :
“ I am weary alike of W. H. Kelly and Captain
Creswell. The problem of Australian Defence contains
more things than either of them dream of. . . . As for
Creswell and his toy flotilla, the thing is either a joke
or an imbecility. Any compliance with this scheme
would mean sheer waste of money, and would delude the
easily-cajoled Australian public into an utterly unfounded
sense of security.... What then is to stand between us
and the might of the Power whose navy will have un-
disputed control Of the Pacific ? Frankly, Ido not know,
although I can say without hesitation that it is not
Captain Creswell’s mosquito crafts.”
I am certain that Captain Creswell does not yield even
to Dr. Arthur in devotion to Australia and regard for
her welfare. He may be credited with some smattering
of professional knowledge, and particularly in a question
which he has made his close life study for many years,
and assuredly he would not be capable of either a “ joke ”
or an “ imbecility ” in a matter of such huge responsi-
bility as the initiation of a naval policy for Australia.
I think Dr. Arthur and your readers generally may
take it as certain that every possible line of advance in
naval development had been carefully examined before
the present one of a torpedo flotilla defence to be built
in Australia was adopted. As to any idea that the tor-
pedo defence would suffice in such a contingency as Dr.
Arthur sets forth —invading attack in force from a power
outside Europe—Dr. Arthur may be certain that neither
Captain Creswell nor any other person with ordinary
brains has any such illusion.
The difference between* Dr. Arthur’s and Captain
Creswell’s ideas would, therefore, be narrowed down to
the years before the arrival of the danger belt in this cen-
tury. Dr. Arthur advocates the borrowing of old war-
ships now, and a loan of £10,000,,000, and the purchase of
a fleet eight or 10 years hence. A little closer look into
this idea would have changed Dr. Arthur’s view. The
“cast-off” ship, the vague, indefinite training of men
for an equally vague and indefinite purpose, is waste.
That poor farce lias been played before. We remember
the Wolverine. Such vessels, too, are perfect sinks, down
which to pour Expenses for repairs. The years of pre-
paration passed in such increasingly old crocks would be
spiritless to our sailors, but this is too obvious to require
words. For all this time, and for the purchase of the
£10,000,000 fleet, we should be dependent on Great Britain
across the globe. If the £10,000,000 fleet were damaged
seriously, our dockyards would be unable to deal with or
rebuild it. To cable for a fresh fleet and for another
£10,000,000 would require a high rate of interest.
Now, against this, Captain Oresswell’s proposals are
to build in Australia —and many leading firms have al-
ready expressed willingness to do so—the vessels to
meet the definite requirements laid down. To build and
commission these vessels will cost n.i more, probably less,
than it would to keep six old cruisers in repair and
commission, and think what else is gained? ADVANCE
—ever advance in naval progress and real efficiency.
Advance with the building and the class of vessel we re-
quire. We shall gain experience and responsibility
of the Government and the country in dealing directly
with naval matters, the experience of a personnel—
not of shadowy-purpOsed Naval Brigades or Re-
serves, but of a Government in authority and men
experienced in actual work, and in measuring their
means to accomplish it —the most valuable of all experi-
ence. And all working up to what? Why, when the
time comes for a big national effort, it will not be repre-
sented by a £10,000,000 cheque cabled to London for a
fleet, but £10,000,000 to be expended on the spot in works
and yards, fed from our iron and steel works, and our
coal mines. And what does that £10,000,000 expended
here mean ? Not alone the self-contained power, the pro-
ducing power which is worth many times 10 millions, but
it means many thousands of men at work in yards and
works, requiring many thousands in the country to feed
them. And which country of the two —Dr. Arthur’s or
Captain Crkswell’s— would be in a better position to
raise a loan ? A country with power in itself or one with
power to borrow only ?
Dr. Arthur really thus seems to have cheerfully waded
in to bludgeon his best friend —one who, I can say with
confidence, is perhaps the most earnest believer in Dr.
Arthur’s ideas (I know this for a fact) —who regards
population as the basis of defence. On the question of
the present power and efficiency of the toy fleet, i.e., its
direct return for expenditure, you may have every confi-
dence, notwithstanding opponents of the Kelly type,
who expect a destroyer to fight a Dreadnought in broad
daylight, and condemn it if unable. As to the indirect
return —the building, and population drawing, the iron
and steel encouragement, are plain to all.
Just by way of postscript, The Bulletin fancies
that “Amphibious” misunderstands Dr. Arthur
on one point. There was no mention in his letter
of borrowing the £10,000,000 to create an Aus-
tralian fleet. The remark that we must, if
necessary, submit to be “taxed to the last
penny ” rather suggested creating the necessary
fund out of taxation.
Undesirable Visitors.
One of the saddest of recent cable items is the
announcement that Keir Hardie, “ leader of the
Independent Labor party in the British Parlia-
ment,” is about to visit Australia, The state-
ment is added that the object of Mr. Hardie’s
visit is to study Australian legislation; but this
does not materially detract from the gravity of
the situation. All the visiting Labor leaders
come with the same yarn ; but they have hardly
got both feet on the wharf before they begin
lecturing instead of learning. Australia, and
especially the Labor party of Australia, is getting
very tired of these visitors. They are as
great a nuisance as the London alderman
and the Manchester manufacturer, who, when
they come to these parts, give rise to the legends
concerning the bolting of capital and the horror
of the foreign investor. Australia has had three
visiting Labor men lately— Tom Mann, Ramsay
Macdonald, and Ben Tillett. Keir Hardie
makes a good fourth. Mann is with us yet—to
our sorrow. Of the four, Tillett, Mann, and
Hardie are all of the one class. They are windy,
frothy, and shallow irreconcilables, whose stock-
in-trade is to call upon the workers to arise and
break their chains, and to take some undefined
weapon and smite somebody, not precisely
specified, with an indefinite amount of smite.
The bewildered listener, even with the most
fervent desire to smite, can gather nothing to
guide him from the Mann and Tillett 1 ravings.
It would, at least, be a little to the purpose
if, for example, Sam Hordern or Rupert
Clarke were pointed out, and the exact
nature of the proposed injury set forth. Simi-
larly, instead of vague talk of revolution which
leads to nothing, it would be interesting to hear
these beer-froth orators get down to details, and
make arrangements for enrolling, arming, and
drilling their forces, and fix a date for the out-
break, and mention where timber and other
material for barricades could be procured. The
Mann, Tillett, and Hardie type of agitator is
common in England, where such vaporings are
taken less notice of than the capture of a 201 b.
salmon. All the bubbly oratory of these men
hasn’t had the slightest tangible effect upon the
condition of the British workers. Mann and Co.
haven’t succeeded in loosening a single rivet of the
“chains.” They haven’t even agreed amongst
themselves which rivet to attack first. The present
British Parliament contains 50 Labor members —
out of a total of nearly 700; and these 50 are divided
into three parties. Keir Hardie is the leader
of one of the small parties; and yet, when he gets
here, he will probably try to teach the Australian
Federal Labor party. That party, which is a
splendid example of discipline and solidarity,
knows more than Keir Hardie’s crowd has ever
dreamt of. All that men like Tillett, Mann,
and Hardie do is to bring labor politics into con-
tempt, and to serve as useful arguments in defence
of Toryism. Intellectually they are miles below
Watson, Hughes and Price ; and altogether they
are a great weariness. Also they are a pest.
The Bulletin therefore moves that Keir Hardie
be asked, before he lands, to write 50 words in the
Polish language, and, if he fails to do so, that he
be sent back in the ship that brought him.
Carruthers and his Insane Three-Cornered
Bridge.
The N.S.W. Premier and his wonderful tri-
angular, three-jointed North Sydney bridge
scheme are still heard from at intervals. In a
sense the North Shore bridge is like the Sydney
city railway station. The city station was talked
of by futile State politicians for 40 years. It was
agreed on all sides that it was an absurdity to have
the railway terminus of a great city in a remote
suburb ; but after two generations of silly argu-
ment as to whether the city station should be on the
east or the west side of George-street, everybody
was as tired as if he had walked 27 miles. So
when a reckless politician suddenly built the new
station on the old remote spot, there was very
little complaint. Everybody was too stunned to
complain much. Similarly the North Shore
bridge has been used by politicians for advertis-
ing purposes till everybody is so dead tired of the
very name of it, that anything is liable to happen.
There is only one possible site for the bridge, but
that site has been mentioned to death during 40
years of repetition, and N.S.W., from sheer
hatred of its futile State politicians, would almost
welcome a proposal to build a bridge over Behring
Strait for a change. And here Joe Carruthers
comes in. He proposes to construct a bridge via
Goat Island and Balmain. It would be almost
twice as long as the direct bridge—33ooft. instead
of 1800 ft. It would start from a remote and out-
of-the-way corner of Sydney. It would miss all
the populous part of North Sydney, and arrive in
the midst of the bush at the edge of nowhere.
Incidentally it would strike a corner of Balmain
far away from the centre of population. It is the
last possible fool and circumbendibus in the way
of a proposal. But Goat Island is Federal pro-
perty, and the new idea enables Joseph to be
nasty by demanding that the Australian Govern-
ment should surrender Goat Island, and to
profess that the Australian Government pre-
vents the building of the wonderful bridge.
Probably the thing that would make Joseph the
maddest man on earth would be the passing of a
Federal Act which provided that the bridge might
be built, and that, as soon as it was completed and
the trains had started to run over it, Goat Island
would be handed over to the N.S.W. State
Government. Then Carruthers wouldn’t build
his loony .bridge, but he would no longer be able
to profess that it was the Australian Government
which stood in the way. At least, he would pro-
bably go on professing, but it would be very hard
to persuade people to believe him. In the way of
professing, it is hard to say where Joseph would
stop.

Fines.
“ Y” writes :
Many who thought Truck Acts and other workshop and
factory laws had compelled employers to pay employees
the full wages earned, were astonished at the recent
revelations in N.S.W. Arbitration Court. One after
another, employers admitted to deducting from the wages
earned sums for thread, needles, and other requisites to
the business, and numerous sums denominated “fines.”
The practice of “ fining ” should in particular be brought
under law. It is a widespread, and enormous, and
grotesque iniquity. The late sensationally-murdered
Whiteley, of London, ran up a code of 159 rules, all
worth 2d. or more in fines, and, of course, the fines were
imposed by a court which heard only one side of
the case, and from which there was no appeal.
Some offences : “ Lounging about in an unbusiness-like
manner, 3d.” ; “Not introducing at least two articles to
each customer, 2d.”; “Unnecessary talking, 6d.”;
“Losing copy of rules, 2d.” Other Captains of Industry,
as the big drapers like to be called, run up codes of 160
and more “ fine ” offences. Employers often aggregate a
substantial sum from this device.
“ Cleaning, as well as rent,” says a writer in the New
Review, “ is sometimes met by a weekly charge of 2d. or
3d. for c eaning the workroom.” The writer goes on to
say that one employer pays Iss. a week for cleaning the
premises, and deducts, in all, 30s. a week from his em-
ployees to cover the cost! The man who makes his staff
pay his charwoman is a genius in the way of meanness.
Miss Clementina Black, in “ Sweated Industry ” (Duck-
worth and Co., 1907), gives records for three successive
weeks in an English stay-factory, the wages, deductions
and receipts running thus :
(1) Nominal Wage .. 9/8} .. 8/- .. 10/2}
Deductions .. 1/4* .. ~/9} .. 1/6
Wage Received .. 8/41 .. 7/2} .. 8/B,}
(2) Nominal Wage .. 9/2} .. 8/6 .. 8/4
Deductions .. 2/2 .. 1/7 .. 1/11
Wage Received .. 7/0} .. 6/11 .. 6/5
(3) Nominal Wage .. 5/3} .. 5/2 .. 6/5
Deductions .. 1/4* .. 1/9 .. 1/9
Wage Received .. 3/11} .. 3/5 .. 3/8
It does not satisfy the claims of humanity or justice to
hand a hook of the code of fines to an applicant
for work, and thereby obtain his or her consent to the
“ bargain.” As there is no impartial tribunal to try the
accused, and no tribunal of any sort to which, without
loss of billet, he can appeal, the system is iniquitous from
one end to the other. It is forced on the employee who,
in an overcrowded market, recognises that if he does not
accept he must starve. To raise wages is a futile
thing while the greedy employer simply deducts
the increase, calling his deductions fines for the
breach of some items in an ingeniously con-
cocted code. “Young lady with soiled collar” was
a 3d. fine under Whiteley. “ Young lady with soiled
cuff,” another 3d. “ Young lady appearing untidy,”
another 3d. Miss Clementina Black mentions the case
of a retail drapery and fancy goods foreman, whose busi-
ness it was to collect fines, and who was required to make
up a fixed sum of money from this source every week.
That foreman was himself a married man with a family,
and knew he would lose his employment if the sum were
not forthcoming. Girls nominally earning 11s. were by
this foreman reduced regularly by fines of one sort and
another by 4s. per week. Australia has already acclima-
tised this robbery and drapery swindle, and if the law
does not suppress it, there is nothing to prevent the
worst abuses of other countries creeping in.

The “S.M. Herald” Speaks Out.
Sydney Morning Herald gladdened Australians
last week with a leader that was “straight
Federal.” The small cantankerousness of Joseph
seemed to have got on the Herald’s nerves, and
it broke out: —
Is N.S.W. desirous of assuming the long-discarded
mantle of South Carolina? Let those who think of
forcing her into that unenviable position read the early'
history of the American Federation, and what came out of a
small tariff matter and a high sense of the dignity of a
sovereign State. But wherein do our grievances lie ? If
the Federal capital site still remains State territory on
whom rests the responsibility? Partly on the State
Government and partly on those whom we ourselves have
sent to represent us in the Federal Legislature. If the
administration of the Customs-house or of the Post and
Telegraph Department does not come up to expectations,
new administrators may be needed, or the clothing of ex-
isting officials with more discretionary power may be all
that is necessary. At any rate, interstate Freetrade,
which Federation has given us, amply compensates us for
Customs pinpricks, which should be removable, and
cheaper telegrams and uniform postal rates and usages
go far as a set-off against those petty irritations which it
should be the authorities’ most earnest endeavor to put
an end to. If we compare the administration of these
two great departments of Federal activity with that of
the Lands Department, solely controlled by'the State, we
do not believe that anyone will be found to urge that the
Commonwealth comes unfavorably out of the com-
parison.
Which last is very nasty indeed to Joseph. The
Herald takes breath and goes on ;
The Union has already accomplished one of the great
objects of its creation. It has awakened in us a sense of
our national responsibilities. Had it not been for Federa-
tion, the pressing danger of the unoccupied North would
never have come home to the people as it has now done.
South Australia might have gone on muddling till her
financial position became so strained as to be unbearable,
but the people of Australia would not even then have
realised that this was a problem in which their national
existence was involved. Had Federation done no more
than to arouse interest in this subject, the price we have
paid would not have been too great.
Had anti-Federalists been able to point to Federal
extravagance in the past, they might have made out a
more plausible case. But they cannot do so. Although
the Commonwealth might easily have incurred debt, it
has not done so.
They say that Macquarie-street doctors had a
busy morning administering restoratives to aged
members of the Cabbage Tree Mob, who had that
bombshell served up to them at breakfast.

vitality.,

health ?

A Ballad of Perfect Curse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A vision there is abides with me—a vision of
scorching verse,
Whose every line shall faultless be —the essence
of Perfect Curse !
An hour will come—an hour sublime —when I,
with an inkpot vast,
Shall fashion a grim and mordant rhyme, whose
words shall rend and blast!
Red thoughts shall drip from the speeding pen—
they’ll be written in god-like ink ;
They shall reach the minds of careless men—they
shall force the world to think !
It haunts me aye, that vision of bliss—o vision
of joy perverse,
When I shall fashion with words that hiss the
Ballad of Perfect Curse !
The looms of thought a song shall weave—an
anthem fierce and grand,
Whose flashing lines like swords shall cleave the
shams that infest the land !
The roaring flails of rhyme shall beat, like
hammers of massy steel,
When at last I fix in a song complete the thoughts
that I sometimes feel!
The thoughts that come when the blatant world
lies hushed in cow-like sleep—
Like thunderbolts they shall be hurled, and the
souls of men shall leap !
Aye, the earth shall start like a guilty thing—
shall drop like a toad its purse
When the shattering stanzas roll and ring of the
Ballad of Perfect Curse!
The palsied creeds shall wither away —shall pass
from the sight of man ;
And kings shall rush in a Judgment Dray, while
he shall drive who can !
No more to cash the knee shall bend—no more
shall white men creep
Like prostrate worms to their journey’s end-
yea, men shall be more than sheep !
The New Republic’s flag shall fly—new eras men
shall see.
When my metred blast goes roaring by like the
blast of artillery !
0 song unsung ! O god-like thought that my in-
most soul doth nurse,
That by my pen may yet be wrought the Ballad
of Perfect Curse!
Volcanic thoughts sweep through the night—red
tides of lava gleam;
They pour their blaze of crimson light athwart
my burning dream !
The pregnant lines take shining form, the long
stern metres swing—
I hear thy voice, incarnate storm, for ever
thundering !
Like vivid lightnings through my soul the jagged
curses flash —
The blazing tides transmuted roll in rhymes, 0
God of Cash !
I see pale Cant—it scuttles by, a dead thing in a
Hearse,
And fetiches piled mountain high—slain by my
Perfect Curse !
A vision abides for aye with me —His a vision of
burning verse,
Whose clarion lines shall perfect be —the essence of
Faultless Curse!
An Hour will come—an Hour sublime vjhen I,
with an ink-pot vast,
Shall fashion a most infuriate rhyme whose words
shall blight and blast!
Red thoughts shall drip from the speeding pen, and
I’ll boil down hell for ink —
I shall write an epistle to careless men that shall
force the world to Think !
It haunts me, that dream of joy and light (0 world
where good dreams are scarce !),
That the gods have appointed me to write the Song
of the Perfect Curse.

In Westralia the nefarious truck system—the
long-since-abolished “tommy shop” method of
the bad old days in England—still exists in places,
naked, glaring, blatant, and unashamed. The
great insolent Timber Combine practises it for all
it is worth. One of the demands made by the
men is that they should be allowed to put up their
own houses at their own expense, instead of
being compelled to live in any kind of cottage
the Combine likes to put up, and to pay
any rent the Combine likes to charge. To this,
manager Teesdale Smith returned a flat negative,
and said :
We will erect, as we have hitherto done, decent
cottages where necessary, and the men will pay a fair
rental.
Teesdale Smith and the Combine deciding, of
course, what is a “fair rental.” And to another
demand made by the men—that they might be
allowed to run their own co-operative stores
instead of being compelled to buy their food at
the Combine’s “tommy-shop” at any prices the
Combine cares to ask for any kind of food the
Combine thinks fit to supply—nigger-driver Tees-
dale Smith thus replied
I said we would certainly agree to let the employees
run any stores they liked provided they would give us
AN EQUIVALENT OF THE STORE PROFITS IN A REDUCTION OF
WAGES, WHICH I ESTIMATED AT ABOUT 6d. PER DAY PER MAN,
MAKING THEIR MINIMUM 6s. 9d. INSTEAD OF 7s. 3d.
The nominal wages are pitiful enough, and yet it
is practically admitted by Teesdale Smith that
the men are diddled out of 6d. a day of their
scanty pay at the Combine’s “ tommy shop.”
And Smith hasn’t the moral sense to be ashamed
of this.
