“Jimmie Pannikin”: Here are my answers to
Quans” relevant questions (8., 30/5/’O7), the
irrelevant and irreverent ones do not concern the
present administrative heads :
(1) I do know some N.S.W. public school inspectors
who look upon this New Syllabus as tommy-rot; but Xdo
not know any inspectors in whose districts no attempt is
being made to introduce it. Even the most antiquated
and ought-to-be-superannuated fossil of an inspector is
sure to have some live teachers who keep in touch with
modern developments.
(2) There be some folk who regard every innovation as
an improvement; there be others who like to shuffle alone
in the old ruts of a rusty past.
(?) Superficial people think that “ dambi-extra drorin ”
and blasticene puddling,” and a few other frilly fads,
are the very soul of the New Syllabus. I have defined the
t ® everal tlmes for Bulletin borrowers and readers.
(4) I do not know any inspectors who were appointed
because they were prominent members of a teachers’
orgamsation. An organisation must be a limp, lukewarm
affair, if the promotion of some of its leaders can bust it
up.
(5) I do not know of any who were appointed because
it wasn t considered safe for children to be taught by
men with such violent tempers ; but I do know of two
able men who were not made inspectors, because thev
were both a bit hot-headed. J
(6) I do know quite a dozen out of the three dozen or
so who would be considered good teachers under the New
Syllabus.
C) I do not know many who made their schools
a haven, a sort of second home, a place of light and
love, a place of dear delights, to be remembered after with
warm gratitude in the strife and stress of maturer years ”
Some there were who believed in light and color, and
warmth and humor, as foundations for sure sympathy and
beneficial understanding. In the era before the New
Syllabus some of us got great kudos for our work in this
direction by the two present heads of the Department
Of course, there will always be educational icebergs, who
u'!f- Ve at a sc . h . ool should be like a prison, bare, stern
chilling, unenticing, uncompromising, frowning for-
bidding. They ought to be sent rabbit-trapping.
(8 and last) I do know teachers who assembled school
half-an-hour before the prescribed time, and dismissed it
an hour after the right time, and who were accused by
parents of harassing their pupils for their own ends an
inspectorship. Some hold sheaves of grateful lettersfrom
pupils who were successful in after life ; splendid presents
from thankful poor parents ; valuable gifts from the
townsfolk amongst whom they labored. I know one
who gave Saturday lessons from 9 am. to 12.30 p m for
15 years in the country and the city, free, gratis’ and
°J. n gthing’. He had no unwilling pupils and no dis-
satisfied parents.
In conclusion, I desire to say that much of my small
measure of success is due to two teachers (now dead)
who taught in historic old Parramatta, and who were not
afraid to give extra tuition, free of charge, to the ambiti-
ous sons and daughters of poor folk, like my own folk
These two teachers were not clock-watchers and time-
killers ; they were workers. God be good to them !
Occupiers of N.S.W. State lands, newly-arrived
immigrants and others, are hereby urged to study
a recent decision of the Gunnedah Land Board re
one Hogarth. Hogarth purchased the holding
of Michael Edmonds on the strength of a “ final
certificate, ” which had been granted to Michael
by the President of the Land Board himself.
Aftei the purchase, some doubts were raised as to
the vendor’s bonafides, and the transaction was
reviewed by the Land Board. Michael Edmonds
who was the respondent, objected, through his
counsel, on the ground that he had been given a
final certificate for his land, and if final didn’t
mean final, he asked what on earth did final
mean ? Counsel on the other side argued that it
was all very fine to say final was final, but what
about section this and section that, and Act No
546 and clause 3472. They went at the question for
five days, and in the end the Board decided that a
final certificate was not necessarily final, and the
holder of such a certificate was liable to have the
question of his title re-opened just as much as if
he held the most temporary certificate ever in-
y®n*ed - luckily for Hogarth, who had bought
the land the Board decided that Edmonds was
in bona-fide possession, and give him a still more
final certificate to pass on to Hogarth, but
whether this makes him any the more secure,
nobody knows. Hogarth doesn’t know for one!
Ihe decision has seriously alarmed a multitude of
the aged cocky who has been sitting in his humpy
with the final certificate under the hearth stone
and it has also terrified a cloud of mortgagees who
have lent money on these final certificates. There
is some talk of taking the case to the Land
Appeal Court. Anyway the State might be
asked to create a new title, and call it the
Absolutely Totally-Last, or the Really-the-Very-
End, or the Dead Finish, or something like that *
so that the cocky could be assured that there is a
dead stop somewhere.

“ The Gunyah ” writes :
Our dear Ma State has evidently set a bad example in
its continual growl re the hardships that the different
States have had to put up with since Federation. The
snarl has at last spread to Victoria, and is with reference
to the Commonwealth Patent Office library. Sec 85 of
the Australian Constitution Act provides that all State
property, used exclusively in connection with any trans-
ferred Department, shall become vested in and under the
Patent Offt® Con “wealth. When the different State
1 atent Offices were brought under the Commonwealth,
the library of each office became vested in the Australian
Government. As each library contained a vast mass of
the same publications, and the Commonwealth didn’t re-
quire the six of them, it decided to take over the Victorian
one—partly because Victoria was the possessor of two
sets of the same publications, one in the Free Public
library and the other in the State Patent Office; and also,
incidentally, to save a N.S.W. growl.
The Commonwealth has been in possession since 1904,
and has spent hundreds of pounds in binding, etc. But
now the Vic. Government can’t see why N.S.W. should
have all the growl to itself, so it has set up in opposition,
the first injustice to meet the eye being the Patent Office
library, which it wants back. Tommy Bent lit the fuse
just previous to leaving for Fogland, and left Davies to
be on hand when the explosion took place.
Meanwhile Victoria has a duplicate library which it
doesn’t use. The books are at the Free Public Library,
stowed away down in the vaults, and covered with the
dirt and dust of ages.
Victorian Government is under the impression that
the disputed library contains only valuable law books,
and no one seems desirous of removing the scales from its
eyes. If it were to carry its ridiculous point, which it
never will, and the vans were backed up outside the
Crown Law Offices, and 17,000 vols. containing nought
but dry-as-dust English specifications were emptied into
the street, then Bent would awake to the faot that the
library is of no interest to him, that he didn’t know
what he was growling about, and that the books’ proper
place is in the Patent Office. Dear old Ma State, you
have a lot to answer for.
What with petty State jealousies and “ left-behind ”
State servants pulling the strings, the life of the Common-
wealth official is fast becoming a curse. Betwixt his
duty to all of Australia and his struggle against State
bogies, he generally retires at night with a prayer that
in the morning he’ll wake up and find himself dead.
• a * )oU k , U P the Australian Government to protect
its officers, either by hiring someone to club the growlers
to death, or else by sprinkling insecticide around the
haunts of a lot of State Premiers.
A little while ago this paper reported the case of
the Mayor of Warrnambool (Vic.), who advised
his Council not to enforce the Pure Food Act,
because it was “inquisitorial.” The Board of
Health took the matter up, and the Warrnambool
Council retreated, in a violent hurry, from its
untenable position. Yet, a few days ago, an
almost similar scene was enacted in the Shire of
Mortlake, which adjoins that of Warrnambool.
The Board of Health wrote asking this Council
whether it had, during the year 1906, complied
with the Pure Food Act by submitting for analysis
at least three samples, per 1000 of population, of
food or drugs. To this polite, business-like, and
sensible inquiry was given the following reply
Councillor Dowling thought it an extraordinary
request. The Act never contemplated such a thing. It
■would, take away all power of legislation. He moved
that it lie on the table till next meeting. Seconded bv
Councillor Cumming.
Out of the 11 councillors composing this shire
council, eight are sheep squatters, and all
seem to be enthusiastic law-breakers and
advocates of dirty and adulterated food. The
consternation which the Pure Food Act is causing
amongst Victorian shire councillors is as
amusing as it is surprising. The Act is a simple
one ; and, to the ordinary person, it seems very
useful and exceedingly necessary. But the average
councillor views any interference with adultera-
tion as a sort of infernal machine, or a spring-
gun, or a mad dog, or a wild Red Indian of the
reddest character.
The S. M. Herald’s special correspondent with
the Governor-General's party in the Northern
Territory made some interesting remarks :
South of the Adelaide River the line enters a hilly
mineral belt. Hungry-looking hills, showing mostly
slate and sandstone, appear from the train, and have
been minutely prospected. A good deal of gold has been
won at various shows, and several promising copper and
tin deposits have been opened up but now the field is
almost entirely in the hands of Chinese. In fact, eight
men on the Elinor mine, near Pine Creek, are the only
whites working, if you except half-a-dozen at the Govern-
ment bore. The history of white effort on the field is a
dismal record of trusting London shareholders, of incom-
petent managers, and of thousands spent in elaborate
surface working, with reefs at depths in no wise entering
intait. From the train the Faded Lily is pointed out
at Brock’s Creek. Here over £40,000 was squandered
before 220 ft. had been sunk, and before the trust
of shareholders was shaken ; while at every point ex-
pensive machinery rusting to ruin may be seen at tne
mouths of pitifully shallow shafts. Even if he had any
capital left when he got there, no manager ever saw fit to
go below the water level, and 260 ft. is the greatest depth
reached. In nearly every case the shows were abandoned
while the shafts were still in payable ore bodies, and the
managers returned to England, vigorously denouncing
the field as unprofitable, and characterising any sug-
gestion of going below water level as absurd. Thus the
field fell into the hands of Chinese, hundreds of whom
with all their primitive methods and decidedly bad
mining, are making a good living.
In other words, it wasn’t the inability of the
white miner to work in the climate—as the pro-
Rigger party alleges but the crass incompetence
(or worse) of the imported “expert” (somebody
just like Mr. Johnson) that handed over the
mining industry of the Territory to the Chow.
Now, having made their navy quite familiar with
Australia, our devoted friends in Japan propose
to send ‘one of their greatest military leaders”
on a, ‘ holiday tour through Maoriland and Aus-
tralia about the middle of 1908,” and he will be
accompanied by “ two other officers whose achieve-
ments have made their names famous.” Doubtless
Australia will show them what maps it has, pre-
sent them with a list of its troops, explain that it
nasn t either a small-arms or an ammunition
factory, and point out that in the recent Easter
manoeuvres the broken sea-line from Botany Bay
to the Hawkesbury was manfully held by 1100
garrison troops, of whom less than 500 were
infantry. Then Premier Carruthers— if he hap-
pensto be Premier just then—wiU proudly point
•out that he has shifted the Mint, which should be
away at an inland capital, to a nice handy place
overlooking the harbor, and that if he can oblige
in any other way he’ll be so delighted. Then the
Monkey that squeezed the Bear will no doubt
smile, and put a few more things down in the
inevitable note-book, and remark that he really
■couldn t ask Australia to be more obliging than it
iias been. °

Marie Hall (whose first recital befell at
Sydney Town Hall last Saturday) charms rather
than stirs. It is questionable whether her body
will ever gain the strength which is necessary to
one who would be among the half-dozen on that top
where it is said that there is always room—and so
there is, if |you shove the other person off.
She will never—in this writer’s opinion—
stand with the first-rate masculine player. At
the same time, there may be a very high
place for her on the feminine side of musi-
cal expression. The feminine in art is as neces-
sary as the masculine, and the feminine seems
to be Marie Hall’s province. It is one
whose bounds will be enlarged by the experience
of life. She will gain a larger command of
emotion and of the dramatic sense. An example
is furnished by the allegro energico of the Max
Bruche Concerto. It would have been quite satis-
factory if there had been more of the energico.
The adagio was exquisite. Others might read it
differently, but this was a true, legitimate reading,
full of expression and tenderness, perfectly played,
and to be judged as a beautiful individual in-
terpretation. Like the Max Bruche allegro,
that which opens the Mendelssohn Concerto had
everything except molto appassionata to commend
it. The andante lacked shading, and was rather
cold. Miss Hall’s playing gained so much as the
evening went on, that it seems more than pro-
bable this movement might have been very much
more impressive if it had been played later. The
artist appeared to distinctly better effect in the
second part of the programme. The various items
on the list did not include any which enabled the
fragile little lady’s audience to estimate whether
she possesses any great gift or acquirement of
technique. They were all within the capacity of
any violinist who claims to be called an artist. Of
the two concertos the Max Bruche furnishes the
more severe test, but both are in the repertoire of
most competen t soloists. It is to Miss Hall’s credit,
and helps to define her artistic level, that she
relied for effect in her first recital upon interpre-
tation, and not on what is called virtuosity. But
her powers in that direction may be taken for
granted, since she has achieved acknowledged
success, amongst other things in the Ernst Con-
certo, which is so seldom heard, less on account of
its musical value than on account of its tremen-
dous difficulty. Miss Lonie Basche plays with
very great charm and sincerity, and wears a very
pretty frock.
ifc * * *
Society responded well to the call of
Albani at Sydney Town Hall on Monday night.
It was a readily-satisfied gathering, and the
Canadian singer of the past, with reminiscences of
royalty to support her, frisked about the platform
in her skittish little way to her heart’s content,
and was liberally rewarded with flowers and
encores on account of what she has been. It is
hard sometimes to enthuse over the musical
memories of Our mothers, and that is how we feel
in regard to Albani. Of her once glorious voice
there remains but a residue, and it is rather a
painful struggle for her to, produce it. She is,
however, respectably supported, vocally and in-
strumentally. Contralto Mildred Jones has capa-
cious and resonant lower notes, William Green is
a lusty tenor, Haydn Wood is a fiddler of a mild
Paganinian order, and our own Myrtle Meggy is
a facile and determined pianist. The last was
listened to with great interest. She has not
belied the hopfes of those who said she would do
good work.
** * *
The community is truly grateful that
Carreno is coming back to Sydney to give
three more recitals. They are to take place on
Wednesday (26th), the afternoon of next day. and
the evening of Saturday (29th). As a player who
covers the whole acreage of piano composition,
Carreno has never had her equal in Australia.
That is the verdict of those who claim to be ex-
pert judges, as well as of the public generally.
Carreno can make anyone like any piece of music,
whether it be a Beethoven Sonata or a Liszt
Rhapsodie. • Her skill is simply magical. She
promises to give us three great concerts, with
Benno, her manager, at a second piano.
** * *
“ Romeo and Juliet ” —“ Music by Chas.
Gounod, to the book of the immortal William Shak-
speare,” as the advertisement rather quaintly puts
it, as if it were a modern musical comedy, and the
dignity of the “authors” had to be acknowledged
—was staged by the Musgrove Grand Opera Co.,
at Sydney Royal, on Saturday. The opera falls
very closely within the lines of the Immortal
Bill’s specifications. Romeo makes love bravura ;
Juliet responds coloratura; then everybody is
killed. There are probably morals in the
story. Romeo was a roaming sort of lover,
and was just as prepared to wade through
blood for another girl before he met Juliet,
if that other girl had been such a forward
minx as Juliet was. The opera takes no notice of
the morals, but provides a very full supply of
shakes and trills, and other musical passementerie,
and the result is picturesque. Few composers
can put the spangles on a theme as artfully as
Gounod.
* * *
Mr. Musgrove’s artists gain in public favor
as their season progresses. “ Romeo and Juliet ”
on Saturday was in most respects a satisfying
performance. Here and there the heel of the
Teuton pressed heavily on the prostrate English
language, but that was a detail lost sight of in a
general high standard of merit. Miss Mary
Stoller, as Juliet, had the slight, girlish figure
for the part, and her voice was equal to the
music, though it was a little hard and un-
sympathetic. Richard Oeser had the gallant
bearing for Romeo, and his voice, too, was
satisfactory though not brilliant. Fritz Rupp
and Hans Mohwinkel sang well; and Emil
Greder gave distinction to the small part of the
Friar. Greder is, all round, the best artist of the
company. Summed up, the presentation of
“Romeo and Juliet ” satisfied the eye, and did
not offend it with a 20-stone Romeo, or an
ancient mass of avoirdupois trying to be Juliet;
and the ear was well enough pleased.
To-night “ The Flying Dutchman ” —a
simple little excursion into gloom, suggesting a
visit to the coal cellar on a winter night, which
Wagner ran off in one of his less involved
paroxysms—-will be staged. It should suit this
co., which is strongest in the basso section. The
last production in Sydney, with Pringle in the
name part, is still a satisfactory artistic memory.
* * *
“ The Spring Chicken ” at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, is getting bigger houses than it deserves,
which is probably why it is getting them. A
popular delight of the moment is to guess what
expurgated naughtinesses came in at particular
points, when the Chicken was a French frivol,
and the guessing is fairly easy ; for the average
human has a fine capacity for guessing what
wickedness other people would be capable of in a
given set of circumstances.

“The Squatter’s Daughter,” which still
hangs out its colored poster and its garish lamp at
Sydney Cri., has entered on yet another week of
the strenuous life that is led on the up-country
station of melodrama. The sheep still shed their
wool, the hero his gorgeous platitudes, and the
young person, who is a wife “in the sight of
Heaven,” her angry tears, to a running fusillade of
firearms. High morality, indiscriminate carnage,
and the reek of black powder dominate the
atmosphere.
** * *
Everyone knows the story of the “Colleen
Bawn,” as Dion Boucicault’s play has it: the
Irish aristocrat who has married a peasant girl
whom he dare not present to his mother; the
mother’s anguished efforts to get him to marry
his heiress cousin, and so save the ancestral home
from being sold up; the attempted murder of
the peasant wife by a hunchback foster-brother of
the aristocrat ; and the reunion of the latter with
the girl of his heart who has the brogue. The
real story, as it was enacted in real life in Ireland
less than eighty years ago, held two tragedies—
the murder of the pretty peasant wife, Colleen
Bawn, and the subsequent hanging of her hus-
band. But no audience in Australia would
tolerate the (stage) murder of the Colleen Bawn,
and as far as this writer knows, there hasn’t been
a hanging on the Sydney stage since 1886, when
Newton Griffiths was suspended by the neck at
the Gaiety and afterwards went round, mad, with
the rope in his pocket.
* * » •
Charles Holloway’s dramatic co. is playing
“The Colleen Bawn” to good houses at Sydney
Palace. Pretty little Beatrice makes a cuddle-
some Colleen, but her tongue rebels at the brogue.
The boy of the cast is John P. O’Neill, as Myles-
na-Coppaleen. His brogue, his songs, his sly-dog
humor, his humorous raggedness and artfulness
keep the audience amused all the evening.
Robert Inman and Godfrey Cass, as Hardress
Cregan and Kyrle Daly, act as gentlemen would
act under the circumstances in which it pleased
Fate and the light on Muekross Head to
call them. Mr. Jefferson Taite, as a pettifogging
attorney, pettifogs with success, though how an
astute attorney can continue to desire to marry a
lady who replies to his matrimonial proposals
with such outcries as “ Impudent Hound,” only a
dramatist knows. James West, as Danny Mann,
the hunchback, deserves mention next to the
O’Neill’s thirsty character. Hesford’s Father
Tom is a lovable old cleric, and Misses Rosa
Conroy and Mabel Russell are hereby congratu-
lated on their truly pretty playing of Mrs.
Cregan and Ann Shute. Next Saturday the
co. will revive what it calls the best play
in its category—“ Two Little Vagabonds”—in-
troducing the small “h’’-less vagabond with the
“ corf.”
** * *
West’s Pictures at Sydney Lyceum Hall
are enriched by a lot of photographs taken when
the Canadian, S’African, and Australasian
Premiers paid a visit to Portsmouth. To do the
visitors honor, the Admiral planned a fine, sham
piece of naval display, showing battle, murder,
and the extinction of life. Torpedoes, bluejackets,
and Dreadnoughts rather lend themselves to
dramatic effects.
** # *
Carlton, a new streak of humor, has
brightened the Sydney Tivoli bill since Saturday.
With a cheerful patter-patter, this leanest man of
the moment bustles through a programme of not
very difficult card-tricks ; but he doesn’t give his
audience time to reflect upon the quality of that
part of his show. He is a cheerful mummer, who
gets on good terms with his people, and that
means a popular success. Otherwise, the Tivoli
is offering a good and fairly-varied bill.

Melbourne’s new Biiou Theatre —entirely
new as regards the interior fittings—opened its
doors on Saturday, notwithstanding a prohibition
order from the local Board of Health. The cause
of this official unpleasantness was not obvious to
the audience. The Bijou stands where it did, the
entrances and exits remain as before, plus im-
provements, which had presumably been sanc-
tioned by the authorities who made trouble at the
eleventh hour, and the auditorium is sweet and
clean and beautiful. Lined with mirrors on the
ground floor and d.c., the house strikes one as
offering less opportunity for the aggregation of
dirt, dust, and insect life than any of the larger
Melbourne theatres. The effect is gladsome.
Even when the lights are lowered, and the in-
augural bellowdrama demands notice, the sur-
rounding brightness can be felt.
* » * *
The management of Messrs. Sullivan,
Harriss and Woods, with Mr. Tittell Brune
figuring as lessee, is also new to Melbourne. lo
some extent, the bellowdrama, “For Her Child-
ren’s Sake,’-’ is new likewise. It was compiled by
the author of “The Fatal Wedding,” and, in its
infantile interest, the present shocker compares
favorably with its forerunner. A pore sick
mother has two precocious kiddies, who
hold a meeting of youngsters at the house
of their grandfather, a dreary old vicar.
The reverend parent has driven his daughter
from home by forbidding her to marry her
•much-maligned young man. She doesn’t marry
him. She goes abroad and marries two other
fellows, one of whom —the father of her two
children aforesaid—breaks his neck during a 10
years’ interval between the first and second acts.
He dies of doing his duty as a trapezist m a
circus. Subsequently the widow marries the boss
of the circus, a violent autocrat who compels Ins
wife to go riding in the arena under threat of
converting her offspring into acrobats if she
shirks the contract. Poor mother is understood

ASTHMA CAN BE CURED.
A Noted Physician will Peove this to
SUFFEKEKS IN AUSTRALASIA.
The astonishing statement that asthma can be
cured, coming from so well-known authority as
Dr. Rudolph Schiffmann, will be of interest to
asthma sufferers. The experience of most
asthmatics has been that little, if any, relief has
been afforded them by the methods heretofore
employed ; and, in fact, the disease has up to now
been regarded as incurable. This noted physician
has, however, after a lifelong study of asthma and
kindred diseases, discovered a remedy which not
only instantly relieves the severest cases of
asthma and bronchitis, but has actually cured
thousands whose cases had been pronounced in-
curable. SocompleteisDr.Schiffmann’s confidence
in his remedy that he requests this paper to
announce that he offers to send a liberal sample
package of “Schiffmann’s Asthma Cure ” free of
charge to all persons sending him their name and
address, plainly written on a post-card, within the
next four days.
He believes that an actual test will be the most
convincing, and in fact the only way to overcome'
the natural prejudice of thousands of asthmatics
who have heretofore sought relief in vain.
Although most chemists in Australasia have sold
his Asthma Cure ever since it was introduced to
the public, still the Doctor fears there are some
persons who have never heard of it, and it is with
a view to reaching these that he makes this
liberal offer.
An opportunity to test, without costs, a remedy
so celebrated and promising so much certainly
should be eagerly grasped by every sufferer.
Address Dr. R. Schiffmann’s Depot, Nos. 5
and 7 Barrack-street, Sydney.*

piness, through the murderous agency of her
darling cheeild, is probably quite new. All the
other ideas in “For Her Children’s Sake” are
as old as “The Fatal Wedding,” i.e. , both
dramas have an amazing foolishness which differs
essentially from that of “ The Worst Woman in
London,” and similar Andersonian productions.
On Saturday, the somewhat brilliant success of
the kiddy element in the show was counteracted
by the dull superfluity of talk, which lasted until
11.40 p.m. Forty-five minutes of desultory dia-
logue could have been spared from the four acts.
When the performance is divested of its padding,
and the adult players have learned to moderate
their tones and subdue their tempestuous style,
the result will be n.t.b.

E. B. Russell and vociferous Alf Booth-
man were the principal offenders on Saturday.
Charles Blake and Johnson Weir did fairly well,
and Miss Helen Burdette, although she over-
acted all the time, more or less, showed a lot of
versatility and emotional power as the harassed
young woman. Miss Helen Fergus played a
genial German character part with exceptional
naturalness, and the children, one and all, were
excellent. The dancing schools of Melbourne,
founded long ago by Mrs. Green and the Misses
Hyams, have proved wonderful nurseries for stage
talent.
** * *
“The Squaw Man,” revived at Her
Majesty’s (Melbourne) on Saturday, has more
variety of action than one finds in any ordinary
melodrama, where a dejected father hugs his first-
born to his manly breast. There are moments in
every act save the first when truth seems stranger
than fiction, but these are not so numerous as the
moments when fiction seems less convincing than
truth. And, unfortunately, none of the incidents
which appeal to a journalist’s highest moral feel-
ings are quite so impressive as the lurid, sinful
drunk of Cash Hawkins. The characters im me-
diately connected with the Love and Marriage
question cannot get a good grip of the audience’s
sympathy. Diana, at the outset, is evidently dis-
posed to lead the good young man astray, and,
seeing that good young men are scarce, Diana’s
allurements are reprehensible. He (the virtuous
James) stoops to an alliance that no sincerely
White Australian canapproveof. His wife, Nat-U-
Rich, has a tender, loving heart, but she is only a
squaw—“just squaw”—consequently her suicide
goes for very little. The same may be said of her
father’s silent grief. The old Chief is a noble
savage and a loyal father-in-law to the hero, but
the people of this Christian country have no tears
to spare for a war-painted gentleman m a blanket
who erects a fence of feathers around the back of
his head. Some changes in the cast of “ The
Squaw Man,” since last year, are of less import-
ance than a noticeable improvement in the acting
of Miss Humphrey. That is to say, her acting is
not so theatrical as before, and her beauty seems
less self-conscious. Why are Les Belles Ameri-
caines so obtrusively charming as a rule ?
** * *
With an eagerness that borders on anxiety,
Melbourne is waiting for “The Blue Moon ” to
rise at the Princess’s next Saturday (22nd). A
new musical comedy is a thrilling event in
stage-struck circles, even when it means another
repetition of an old, familiar co. “The Blue
Moon” will introduce a new company of the
latest possible construction. Ten principal names
are advertised, and only two of them —Celia
Ghiloni and P. Bathurst—-are to memory dear.
And none of them, seemingly, was imported
from outside of Australasia, the principal soprano
being Miss Amy Murphy, late of Maoriland. It
is a notable sign of the times that J. C. William-
son is about to produce a new musical piece under
conditions which have happened only once in a
Blue Moon.
** * *
For 12 nights only Bland Holt will pro-
vide his Melbourne Royal public with a revival
of the lively drama of the Bad Blind Man and
the Simple Millionaire who abide “In London
Town.” The Blind Man (Styan) differs from
most members of his melodrama class in not being
an object for sympathy. He’s the wickedest old
citizen that ever felt his way with a walking
stick. He bullies the hapless maiden whom he
has adopted for his daughter, and he bangs his
stick against the crutch of a gentle lame boy.
He’s a terror to the virtuous, is this afflicted evil-
doer, until he gets himself knocked down a steep
flight of garret stairs by a rebellious subject,
and the sound of his falling body is the
last that is heard of him. The millionaire,
on the other hand, is an amiable, long-
suffering idiot. In the first act he accepts
the Blind Man’s daughter as his own flesh and
blood, and seats her in the lap of luxury where
the other girl ought to be. The misunderstanding
re the daughters is instigated by a shiny-hatted
villain (Albert Norman) for matrimonial purposes
or something, but his base designs are thwarted
by Bland Holt, the comic hero of this amazing
tale. As a matter of fact, Bland removes the
polished villain by pushing him off a house-top.
It is a lawless way of bringing things to a happy
conclusion, yet it passes without comment from
the police. Some five-and-twenty characters and
ten scenes are employed in the production of
“In London Town.” Maxwell (as the guileless
millionaire), Miss Ross, Styan, Albert Norman
and Miss Ireland (the bogus daughter) undertake
the heavy parts, whilst Bland and Mrs. Holt,
Brown, and several other people supply the rorty
Cockney relief, which helps considerably to make
the success of an unreasonable drama. The plot
is the weakest part of the entertainment.

“ 0.K.” writes:—
Prior to the re-appearance of “ Mother Goose ” last week,
it was reported in Melbourne that the panto companj’ had
lost about half-a-dozen of the original performers, includ-
ing Phydora. There were only three changes in the cast—
Mabelle Morgan for Florence Young, Shortland for Sam
Gale, and Florence Quinn for Celia Ghiloni. Pressy
Preston and Olive Morrell are understood to be leaving
shortly, but the retirement of Miss Morrell is not likely
to weaken the show, anyhow. As a matter of fact, it
hasn’t been weakened to an appreciable degree thus far.
The new fairy, who has little to do, is a very passable
substitute for Miss Ghiloni, whilst Mabelle Morgan, in
the principal boy part, now reveals a fresh young singing
voice, with some sweet top-notes, in addition to
a tall figure, which looks finer than before, though
not so large. Young man Shortland, too, is a
good substitute for his predecessor. “ Mother
Goose” promises to keep its popularity throughout
a six months’ tour. Phydora will stay with it all the
time, and if there ever was much fault to find with
that luck-bringing comedian, the reasons are no longer
apparent. He and Harry Shine have bettered what they
did at first, singly or together. Their grotesque duet-
singing in the airship is a finished work of harmony. It
sounds accidental—the harmony—but it happens every
night, as the result of half a year’s practice. Even a
panto, improves with age, if the mummers regard it
seriously, and when panto, is their special line of business
they take it as seriously as an amateur Hamlet takes his
Immortal Bard.

** * *
Zangwill’s “Mary Ann,” at Adelaide
Royal, proved to be like all his books, varying
quickly from real art to the foolishly farcical.
There was a soda-water syphon that stood and
gazed at the audience for two acts. It was quite
certain to squirt somebody; why didn’t it begin ?
As things turned out, it was waiting for a parson,
and, when he came on the scene, it squirted him
in a way that made up for lost time. Ten
minutes could with advantage be cut out of each
of the last two acts. Also Zangwill never can, or
could, resist a play on words. But the piece may
be forgiven, because it affords Miss Tittellßrune
a chance to do her very best work outside
of “ Sunday.” The co. is ending its season with
revivals: Leah of the cabbage farm, and Dorothy
of the temper that was warm enough even for
Queen Elizabeth. There is a cheerful, hard-
working humorist named Denis Carney at the
Tivoli; the Hippodrome has sunk from the horse-
drama to “East Lynne’’and the like; and the
“ All Blacks ” crowd has been giving its strictly-
moral family fiddling entertainment at the Town
Hall. Mrs. Brough has been in Adelaide during
the week ; but, alas! only on business, and that
not stage-business.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Hamer”: A horse went mad in a subdued township
in Victoria recently, and created more excitement than
the average hamlet generally experiences in a generation.
A mad horse is about as exciting a calamity as can arrive,
barring perhaps an earthquake or a drunken Jap. In
India, where the mad horse is fairly common, the civilised
native prince loves to set him to light a tiger in a circus.
Mostly the “ patient, plodding stead ” wins the battle,
and that is a fair criterion of the particular Sheol he is
capable of creating when he starts to eat a small town on
a summer afternoon.
“ Hiphen Alfred Sutro’s new play, “John Glayde’s
Honor,” is, I believe, shortly to be transplanted to Aus-
tralia. I have seen it here—George Alexander up. It is
many furlongs behind his “Walls of Jericho,” but still is
distances in front of the usual problem play. John
Glayde is an American by nationality, and by persuasion
a millionaire. In making his money, he neglects his
wife. She, being an American, naturally goes to Paris,
where she has an affaire with an artist. John Glayde
arrives suddenly, sees how the land lies, and makes
remarks of a more or less personal nature. His wife,
fearing a sticky death for her lover at the hands of her
husband, puts her arms round his (the husband’s) neck,
and pulls his leg (it isn’t a contortionist turn). She
swears there is nothing in the rodent he has sniffed, that
she loves him only, and will never, never look at anyone
else, &c., and that she only wants to go out and
see if the trams are still running, and will return
in a few minutes for ever and ever, amen.
John, who, for a Yankee bullionist that scalps the
’Merican public for its last dollar, seems to be a simple
sorter soul, gives her a torrid kiss and his blessing, and
lets her depart. As the most ingenuous debutante in the
ten-and-sixpenny stalls has already foreseen, she goes
straight to where her lover is waiting with a fleet motor-
car to convey her to the place where the husbands cease
from troubling and the millionaires are at rest.
Before they can get started, however, John arrives,
having been made wise by his sleuth-hound secretary.
He, as an interested party, has quite a deal to say on the
subject. Had it been melodrama, he would have shot
the villain, his wife and himself. As it is refined drama,
he merely wishes the villain luck, and tells him to keep
the goods, which cannot be returned on appro., or words
to that effect. Our old friend Henrietta Watson figures
prominently in the cast.
“ Snell ” : “ The Virginian ” failed to catch on in Mel-
bourne, where “The Squaw Man” was a big success.
This is not surprising, for the latter, despite its faults
and its slap-dash blending of the Family Herald with
“Deadwood Dick,” had a coherent story, and was a fairly
good and craftsmanlike drama of its kind, whereas “ The
Virginian ” is a mere string of incidents, and remains
quite as chaotic as the jumble novel from which it was
constructed. The love affairs of the Virginian and the
school-marm from Vermont don’t get into grips till the
last act, and the whole show is lingering, disjointed, and
unsatisfactory. All this troubles the reader of the novel,
too. By the way, the story of the mixed babies was told
in Australia of a Gippsland party long before the
American took it up ; and the yarn of Emily the hen, the
success of which, the author declares, almost induced
him to turn comic writer, is merely one of the commonest
comic properties which earlier and better humorists have
used again and again.
“ The Spring 1 Chicken ” gives indication that in its
original French it was something broader and more
precipitous than it is now. The action of the typical
Palais Royal farce —and “ The Spring Chicken ” is just a
Palais Royal farce bowdlerised and set to music—revolves
with damnable iteration around the administering, at
awkward moments, of “Elixirs of Life” or “Buckup
Drops ”to the various members of the cast. Indiscrimi-
nate hocussing, taken in conjunction with a night scene,
during which all hands burst in and out of each other’s
bedrooms in different stages of unclothedness, gives scope
for some humor—of a kind. The spectacle of the
p.yjamaed hero speeding across the bedroom furniture a
short length ahead of the stout and despicable husband
in a low-comedy nightshirt, with the remainder of the
company a lap or so behind, is one a Palais Royal audi-
ence never tires of. Only a faint flavor of all this is left
in “ The Spring Chicken.” The hocussing is a mere affair
of homoeopathy, so to speak ; and certainly in the play’s
country of origin that studio scene was not played in a
studio.

Britain’s tender and trucklesome regard for the feelings
of its Brown Ally has broken out again. The Lord
Chamberlain, a few weeks ago, vetoed Gilbert and
Sullivan’s opera, “ The Mikado,” on the ground of its
“ buffoonery in certain parts,” as though the ornate joke
were not consistently extravagant throughout. “The
Mikado ”is 22 years old. Its “ buffoonery ” was not less of
a libel on the Government of Japan in i. 885 than it is to-
day, but in ’B5 the libel didn’t matter. The Brown Man
was then merely the monkey that scratched its head
whilst John Bull played the organ. Now we have the
monkey turning the organ handle whilst J.B. scratches
his head. Therefore, further performances of “ The
Mikado ” are prohibited in Britain, which will probably
be the cue for a glorious revival of the work in
America. The dishonesty of the Lord Chamberlain’s
statement of his reason for suppressing the opera was
lately emphasised by a supplementary order forbidding
any performance of any of the music by regimental bands
or on ships of war. W. S. Gilbert, in a scornful letter to
the London Daily Telegraph, remarks that there can’t
have been any objectionable buffooning in Sullivan’s
music. Music doesn’t scoff at anybody. But Gilbert had
better lie low these times, and not draw attention to
himself. If the Japs demand his arrest for writing “ The
Mikado,” the new and humble Bull will assuredly run
him in, even if he has to do it under a dubious charge of
disorderly conduct and tearin’ the Jap emperor’s uniform.
“Hamer”: The Sunday Sun published last week a
photo, of “0. M. Berkeley, the new English actor who
will appear with the Human Hearts Co.” Isn’t this
carrying the “ English ” business too far? Berkeley has
been known, playing under that name too, for years in
Australia, and, to my best belief, he is an Australian by
birth. Some day an Australian audience, with a patriotic
desire to see what its own actors can do, will turn round
and ask the people who bring these “ English companies ”
to Australian theatres for an Australian show. And then
the managements, as they confess that all the people except
the stiff leading lady and the disappointing leading man
are native-born Australians, will look surprisingly small.
From an athletic point of view, the most important
artist with Musgrove’s grand opera co. is Rufus Ferguson,
who is in the chorus, and also does small parts. Rufus is
indeed versatile. He is an ex-champion amateur track
and cross-country ped, cyclist and gymnast; he wore a kilt
with the Vic. Scotty regiment, and has won prizes at
rifle shooting and at aquatic carnivals. On the all-round
ticket, Ferguson takes a lot of beating, especially with
his sweet baritone thrown in.
“G. Elburg”: I think that the title of Zangwill’s play
should be “Merely Mary’s Million.” Even the hero’s
respect for the girl whose soul struggles with beetles
in the kitchen dates only from the moment when
he becomes aware of her dazzling legacy. He is little
better than the fawning hash-house lady. Just previous
to the arrival of the news, he curtly orders the girl-
drudge to put a shine on his shoes, but when, as the
possessor of a pile, Mary Ann commences to brush his
coat, he says, in a shocked voice, “You mustn’t do that
now! ” The transformation, inside six years, of an
illiterate slavey, with a rustic accent that would turn the
edge of an axe, into a polished society Miss with musical
accomplishments, is too tough for any digestion. Alto-
together, “ Merely, etc.” is mostly tripe, but as the
average audience relishes tripe, and always asks for more
tripe, I suppose tripe will continue to loom largely on the
theatre bill of fare.
“K.” : Albani’s skittish mode of exit from the platform is
a truly amazing reminiscence of the singer’s bygone youth-
fulness. History records that the Albani frisk set her
Australian audiences laughing, nine years ago, when she
still had a little of her old voice left. This time the frisk
is pathetic—painful rather than funnj'. Seemingly the
matured matron has no other way of quitting the stage.
She runs off as though she had sportively flung a ball of
wool at the audience and were pretending to be afraid of
a return throw. The act of giddy girlishness is performed
even under the most inappropriate conditions. If
Albani has just completed an “Ave Maria,” she scuttles
away from public view as gaily as she flees from “ Within
a Mile of Edinboro’ Toon,” or “ Oomin’ Through the
Rye.” It seems unkind to allude to the airs and graces
of Albani in her advanced autumnal days, but a public
that never knew her when she was a Queen of Song, can
nowadays hear and see nothing in her save a handsomely-
dressed matron who makes a great to-do about a warble
that has lost its beauty. One must smile, or sneer, or
feel sorry for the lady, so it is best to shut one’s eyes and
mourn for the departed Voice. Some of the listeners
at the first of Albani’s concerts in Melbourne, last week,
didn’t need to shut their eyes. They were invitees from
the local Blind Asylum.
“Snell”: I see a London manager has been disputing
the right of theatre-goers to hiss performers or per-
formances that displease them. Defendant contended
that the right to applaud implied the right to hiss, but
the manager would not have it, and the person who made
the goose noise was fined. In Australia you never hear
the faintest hissing in a theatre in our time ; but there
was a period when things were otherwise. And lam far
from sure that managers are wise in rigorously repressing
the hiss. If people are displeased with an important item
in a performance, they sit it out with painful patience,
then go home and warn their friends to keep away, and
consistently remain away themselves for the balance of
the season. The manager isn’t certain what it is that has
emptied his house, and very often he would be glad to
know. And it would pay him to know, whether he was
glad or otherwise.
Even if “ Modern Milo ” Montague really rode through
Coventry 7 in her stage lack of costume (as alleged in the
yarn referred to in a recent Bulletin), she was not the
first to imitate Lady Godiva in the old town. One of the
letters of William Godwin, father of Shelley’s second wife,
mentions “ Coventry Fair, with a procession of all the
trades, with a female representative of Lady Godiva at
their head, dressed in a close dress to represent naked-
ness.” This was in 1797.
“ Rhaupo ” : The entertainers who periodically inflict
thousands of feet of Hograph pictures and middle-aged
female balladists on N.S.W. provincial towns are striking
opposition from the colored curse. Two of Bruce Smith’s
proteges, who formerly sold scented soap and cheap

“Flinderino ”: “.Let me say a word in favor of musi-
cal (?) competitions. It is the custom for the Melbourne
music-shops to give a donation to the prize funds of these
competitions, and in return for this donation the music-
shop people are allowed the privilege of selecting the set
songs and goanna items. Now, Messrs. Rail and Tando,
of Oollins-street, have in stock 25 doz. copies of “Good
Night, Queen Anne, Good Night,” which they bought
cheap at a sale in London 20 years ago ; they have had
them stowed away on the top shelf ever since. A cheque
for the prize fund is sent along to the officials of the
North-West-by-East Melbourne competitions, and “ Good
Night, Queen Anne, Good Night,” is chosen for the bar-
rowtone solo. Messrs. Rail and Tando are the only people
who have, this song in stock, so the competitors must buy
their copies there, and pay 2s. a piece for music not worth
2d. In this way the music shops are able to profitably
dispose of what would otherwise be dead and useless
lumber, and they don’t thereby make the competitions
any worse than they naturally are.
Maoriland’s most amazing’ showmanyis Felix Tanner,
whose endeavors in the cause of quaint uselessness have
l>een highly successful. Felix first burst into.popularity
as a public entertainer at Waihi during the gold boom.
A miner looked into the whare of Felix, gave a cold
shudder, and cut the rope by which Felix was hanging to
the tie-beam. Felix opened his eyes and smiled. He was
just experimenting, he said. After that he used to hang
himself more regularly, with the same success, and
before payable audiences of miners. To demonstrate that
his stomach was as weird an article as his neck, Felix
suddenly refused all food. He persisted in this refusal for
a month, after which he was satisfied that a man was
much better without tucker. He has put up a long
series of fasts since then. Six years ago Tanner
threatened to go round the world in a sort of barrel, and
immediately produced a. monstrosity funnier than
the Pah’f’l. After he had collected several shillings from
people who came to view and retired to laugh,
he went to sea, intending to return to Maoriland in about
16 years via the Arctic Ocean. Felix was away nearly a
whole day, but his boat remained longer. Subsequent
barrels were built and swamped, Felix undergoing show
fasts to obtain the lucre for each successive craft. A
fortnight ago Felix went on a coastal voyage in
the latest barrel, with five companions and three days’
tucker. Possibly Tanner concluded that his mates we: e
professional fasters too. Anyhow, Tanner and his Ark
were seven days on the trip, and the marvel is that they
weren’t longer. Maoriland is now waiting to see Felix
Sixpence break out in a fresh place. He never fails to
do that.
In a poor quarter of Hobart stands a queer-looking
building, time-worn and tottering. A stranger, gazing
thoughtfully at the ancient structure and the huge
wooden props which prevent its collapse, mistook it for a
factory, and expressed surprise that the authorities
allowed it to remain standing. When told that the build-
ing was the Hobart Theatre Royal, he sank down on the
footpath, and was carried, gibbering, to the nearest pub,
where they saved his reason with brandy. No one seems
to know exactly when this theatre was built, but a close
inspection of the original structure—now thickly covered
with patches of wood, iron, tin, etc.—reveals huge blocks
of rough-hewn masonry, which would suggest a con-
nection with the Stone Age. The interior is fearfully and
wonderfully designed, so as to utilise every square inch of
space. Years ago Bland Holt staged one of his mammoth
dramas at this place, but the narrow stage ruined the
piece and nearly killed the hero. The scene depicted a
prison, into which the hero had been cast by the villain.
The lights were turned down, and while you counted
three the bright morning changed to the darkest hour
of night. The hero then escaped, rushed on the stage
through the gloom, yelled “Yah ! I’m free !” and fell
headlong into the stalls. It is said this caused Bland H.
to swear never to stage another piece at Hobart without
taking measurements of the drama to see how it and the
stage would fit.

Pictorial post-cards bearing the inscription “ Greetings
from Australia,” and the portraits of Gertie Millar. Marie
Studholme, and Zena Dare, are now filling the windows
of Sydney stationers’ shops. This latest phase of the im-
porting mania is calculated to make the recipients of the
cards at the other end of the world think that Australia
doesn’t possess three good-looking girls in its total
female population of two millions or thereabouts. Why
put a group of English actresses on what purports to be
an Australian post-card ?
“Falk”: “Why Valkyrie for Walkiire”? (B. 23/5/’07.)
Because Valkyrie is a common English word, in use before
Wagner was born, and stands for thelady whom Germans
call a “ Walkiire.” Our knowledge of her was got from
the Icelandic, and the name, of course, also. And it
would be more appropriate than the High Dutch form,
even if it couldn’t claim priority ; for never a bit of High
Dutch are we (non nobis, Domine !), but a good deal of
Scandinavian. So for general purposes we say “ Valkyrie,”
and keep the German form for use when there may be
doubt as to whether it’s the Wagnerian or some other
version of the story that we are talking of. Anyhow, it’s
a sinful thing to urge the Australian to curl his tongue
round a German ii, when there is any fair way out of it.
and “ Valkeerer ” might well be unintelligible to both
parties.
Australia has one bank, anyhow, to which
Australia is home and the rest of the world isn’t.
In the official shareholders’ list of the Bank of
Adelaide, when a shareholder doesn’t live in
Australia he is simply described as of “Parts
beyond the Seas.”
The cheerful manner in which Abo. writers
hand round the statement “You’re Another” is,
no doubt, because they observe their snakes and
vanishing frogs under different, if not indifferent,
conditions. They should remember that the
ideal for all mental effort is acquired by the aid
of the right allowance of D.C.L. Whisky, the
beverage of the brainy, and an infallible cure fcr
snakebite.*
Things must be pretty bad in the cow-
yards when such an old crusted Conservative
print as the Warrnambool Standard, about the
leading paper in the Victorian Western District,
says the Presbyterian parson was right, and the
child-labor practice “is assuming proportions
that ought to be instantly checked.” In fact,
almost everybody is beginning to say that the
cheap child-slave must be taken out of the cow-
yard. There are not quite so many yet who say
that the child-slave would probably never have
been thrown into the cowyard if rents and free-
holds had not been run up so outrageously. Now
the cow-raiser has got into the child-slave habit,
and it will take a rigid law to break him of it.
And the chances are that in the process he will
often get broken himself, with the law pushing
him one way and the landlord pulling him the
other. So the landlord will have to be attended
to as well as the child-slave.
THE MIKADO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As a sop to Japan, the British Lord Chamberlain has
withdrawn his official license for stage performances of
“ The Mikado,” and has forbidden the music of the opera
to British military and naval bands.
O, Lord Chamberlain,
Must we mourn in vain
Vanished friends who sang before us—
Katisha, Pooh Bah and chorus?
Can’t they sing again?
0, Lord Chamberlain !
“ Mighty is Japan,”
Cries the Englishman.
People now are changing places ;
We must cringe to colored races.
Great is Hindustan !
Mighty is Japan 1
Truckle to the Chow,
He’s a boshter now,
Though his skin be dry and yellow,
He’s a very splendid fellow,
This we must allow.
Truckle to the Chow !

SOCIETY
A Melbournite (they are very tired of the
police force in Melbourne, and have lost all
respect for it) writes
The Melbourne detectives have had heaps of assistance
from outsiders in their efforts to unearth the murderer
of diamond-dealer Bauer. For days they were busy
tracking: down a story about a man who got into a cab
somewhere near Modern Chambers at something about
the tune of the tragedy. Till they have caught that man
they will make no attempt to run down the people who
were seen getting into trams at the same time and place.
The people who went by on foot will be dealt with subse-
quently, also those who were anywhere thereabouts
on motor-cars, or observed passing in balloons, or
who rushed up on locomotives or traction engines,
ihe only suggestion to which the police are not
prepared to give some attention, it would appear
is that Bauer was really killed in Collins-street!
and conveyed to his room surreptitiously, it being
considered improbable that a man could carry a blood-
stained corpse up-stairs in a crowded building without
exciting some attention. This conclusion reflects great
credit on the force, but, despite the astuteness thus dis-
played, Melbourne people have no faith in the ability of
the police to catch the criminal, although there is some
little hope that the criminal may catch the police.
* » * *
The Melbourne tecs and the Melbourne
press seem to have jumped to the conclusion that
the slayer of diamond-merchant Bauer is some
well-known and experienced criminal. They are
guided to this belief by the adroitness with which
he got away, and the cold-bloodedness with which
he killed his victim in order to destroy the evi-
dence against him. But an amateur, or a person
that the police have never heard of, may get
away even more adroitly than a professional, if
luck favors him ; in fact, it is chiefly by the lack
of sufficient adroitness in getting away that a man
becomes known to the police as a professional
malefactor. When a man has been caught, or at
least discovered, often enough to be known as a
hardened criminal, he is obviously the man who
hasn’t put up the best of records as a swift
and noiseless vanisher round the corner. Therefore
it may be argued that it is the man whom the
police have never yet bowled out who should be
looked for rather than the one they have howled
frequently ; the force should hunt for the man it
has never heard of. Also the amateur criminal,
or the one who has never been found with blood-
stains upon him and the body on his premises, is
often quite as cold-blooded about wiping out his
victim as the known professional; as a case in
point, there is a mere youth in a Victorian gaol
who admits having done exactly that same thing.
Bauer evidently anticipated a visit and knew his
visitor—he was killed while in the act of weighing
loose diamonds. As his papers give no hint of
any appointment, how would it be if the ’tecs
turned their attention to the deceased’s respect-
able friends and customers, and tried to find out
which of them was in such financial straits that
he might reasonably want to kill and plunder a
diamond merchant? The cabman’s story about
the man who rushed out of Modern Buildings and
hooked it wildly in a vehicle to connect with the
Sydney express doesn’t necessarily mean any-
thing. Many times every day dilatory people
rush out of buildings—they couldn’t be expected
to rush out of dirt-boxes or coal-scuttles—and
catch trains with only half-a-minute to spare.
Probably no man in Australia has done it oftener
than Justiee Barton, who is reputed never to have
caught a train by more than a 10-seconds margin
n his life. And no suspicion attaches to him.
j * * * *
“ D.” offers a theory ;
When the Bauer murder is solved it will likely turn
out to be the work of some utterly commonplace per-
former. A series of crimes of this class occurred lately
in ’Frisco, beginning with the slaying of a Jap bank
manager, who was neatly clubbed to death from behind
as he bent to examine a cheque, and finishing, after five
faultlessly executed assassinations of the same kind, by a
bungled attempt on the life of one Behrends, a jeweller.
Behrends, though badly injured (his clubbing had taken
place as he turned to his safe), managed to han«-
on to one of his assailants till the police arrived. Then
it was found that this “ strong- arm ” band of cracksmen,
to which all California had been attributing the horrors
for months past, consisted of a brace of callow youths,
mere amateurs, as it were, in the great game of robbery
with bloodshed. In criminal circles it is mostly the unex-
pected that happens to commit the murder. From
present indications, and despite all talk of “ skilled
work,” “old hands” and the like, a Y.M.C.A. resort
would seem as plausible a place as any other to start a
hunt for Bauer’s slayer.
Melbourne gets its daily sensation now
just as it had its daily race meeting before Bent’s
Gaming Act came into operation. While the
Force was getting out of the limelight, and look-
ing in shady corners for clues to the Mint rob-
bery and the city murder, came news—carefully
suppressed as long as it was possible—of another
unaccountable steal. The Star of Australia Felix
lodge of Rechabites, which meets in the Temper-
ance Hall, just below the police barracks, is about
the most unlucky body of soft-drinkers extant.
In ’92 its secretary got away with £2OO of its
funds, and though he was subsequently convicted
the lodge recovered nothing. Two years ago its
treasurer took £l5O home with him one
night—and hasn’t since been seen or heard of.
After that disaster the lodge took less risks,
and the present treasurer, one Schunke, has to
deposit the quarterly-night receipts in a safe on
the premises in the presence of two stewards. At
10.30 p.tn., on June 10th, Schunke and these
stewards locked £l5O, in notes, gold and silver, in
the drawer of a small safe in a cupboard in the
Temperance Hall lodge-room, and locked the safe
and the cupboard. Then the caretaker locked the
room door and the hall itself, and all went home.
Next morning Schunke came along to take the
cash to the bank. Everything was locked as he
had left it—he having one safe-key, the bank the
other—but the cash was gone, and a shocked and
surprised Schunke hurried to the police office to
pile further trouble on the shoulders of the har-
assed ’tecs. A back door to the building was
open, but in this case, same as in the case of the
Mint robbery, the cupboard, the safe and the safe
drawer had all been opened with proper keys,
and carefully relocked, and there had been no
need for violence whatsoever. And when the fat
man meets the bald man at the corner he remarks
that it is a really astonishing coincidence.
* •* * #
There is no lack of charity in Merrie
England. Miss M. Loane, in her recently pub-
lished “ The Next Street But One,” says :
Letter-writers in the daily papers this winter have sug-
gested endowing the poor with old tea leaves, stale
bread, and pieces from the children’s plates. Why omit
sour milk from the cat’s saucer, and bones that have
ceased to interest the dog? I should think little of the
poor if I did not firmly believe that they would starve
rather than eat the scrapings of nursery plates—and what
must children be, brought up in the houses of people
capable of writing such maddening insults and calling
them charity ?
Miss Loane adds that one of her last experiences
as a district nurse was the offer, for the use of the
poor, of a feeding-bottle no longer needed by the
half-dozen puppy dogs for which it had been pur-
chased. This sort of reading fills one with a
yearning desire to be able to sentence all the
wealthy and charitable females of the country to
three years domestic service in the families of
small suburban grocers.

“ Lantana ” : Passing recently along the
vicinity of Goulburn and Elizabeth streets (Syd-
ney), I was edified by the spectacle of a white
girl of about 16 toiling between the shafts of a
fair-sized truck, laden with crockery and assorted
debris. The “conductor,” a male thing of the
Assyrian persuasion, was doing all the grunting
while he shoved with one hand at the rear of the
vehicle. Went in search of a policeman, but the
vehicle and its human “shafter” passed on dur-
ing my absence. I had a vague idea I could get
at the savage for cruelty to humans, or something,
if I managed to interest the Law in the matter.
For a generation or so a certain
Victorian shire council was bossed by
the secretary—a jovial old soul, w'ho
made fearful and amazing errors in the
municipal accounts, and was usually
non est on council days, but who was
kept on because no one had the heart,
or the pluck, to shift him. The inevit-
able reformer arrived, being elected to
the council on the distinct understand-
ing that he was to be appointed chair-
man and that his first official act would
be to fire the jovial secretary. It was
a blistering hot day when the council
met for the first time after the
election, and no progress was made
because there was no secretary and
no minute book. The older hands
lit their pipes and waited philo-
sophically, but the reformer rose
in his place, and started to re-
form. For three-quarters of an
hour he vainly threw himself
up against the dead wall of
apathy, and tried in vain to find
a seconder for his proposal that
the drinky, confused and in-
visible secretary should be dis-
pensed with. Then he dis-
gustedly retired to an adjacent
pub wrote out his resigna-
tion. Ten minutes after he
was gone the convivial secre-
tary arrived, in his shirt-
sleeves and with a bell topper
over one eye, and struck the
table hard. “ Thish meetingsh
adjourn’,” he said. “ It’sh
too hot f’r me, anyhow.” And
the unreformed shire council
smiled and adjourned, blandly
indifferent to the fact that
thistles and stinkwort were
spreading over the land.
The case of Silk, the naval engineer, who
was belated near Port Darwin for five days or
thereabouts, suggests that some naval engineers
are mighty poor navigators. Silk got belated
eight miles from a town. There was a railway
line to guide him ; if he found the railway line,
he couldn t help finding the town, and any man
who could tell the points of the compass by the
sun, and could walk in a fairly straight line,
couldn t help finding the line. Under these easy
conditions, the distinguished explorer lost his
road, his gun, some of his clothes, a dead duck
that he was carrying, a wallaby tail (also dead),
and himself, and was at last rescued and towed
into dock by a nigger.

An outfitter’s circular serves to recall the
fact that it doesn’t take much to construct the
yelling variety of University student, 1 which utters
its loud arrangement of sounds at Commencements
and such. “Gown, £1 10s. ; trencher, 10s. 6d.,”
says the manufacturer. Then there are the shrieks
—say 5d.; and the student itself, 2d. less than
nothing. Total, £2 Os. 9d.
In Sydney, t’other night, about £2300 was
raised at a meeting of the Foreign Missionary
Society. It seems a lot of money to plav-up in
educating Mr. and Mrs. Tanna to a taste for
trousers. Late Arbitration Court proceedings
suggest powerfully that a mission to the people
who sell the trousers to the converted Tanna
family would do more abiding good.

Seeing that Greek mythology still
crops up freely in poetic literature, oratorical
allusion, and even in journalism, a word of en-
couragement is due, perhaps, to a booklet of “Old
Myth Stories—Simply told for Little Australians.”
They are not at all improving to the infant mind,
but the youngster who knows nothing of Perse-
phone and Midas and Atalanta and Atlas, &c.,
is likely to find the ignorance inconvenient at one
time or other in the future ; so it may be well for
Little Australians to become familiarised with the
names and doing of the old, mildewed kings and
gods and goddesses in a Hans Andersonian way.
The yarn concerning Ceres and her daughter
Persephone is at least as valuable as the rhyme Of
Mother Hubbard and her Dog. The same re-
mark applies to the fable of Ulysses and the
Sirens, which is a subject for the painter’s art,
whereas no artist of eminence has interested him-
self in Mrs. Hubbard and the animal that didn’t
get a bone. If the “Old Myth Stories” are un-
avoidable, and people are bound to “take” them
as one takes the measles, they had better be
taken early and easily. Miss Emma Pyke, of
the Yic. Education Department, is the authoress
of the booklet aforesaid. She got it published
last year, and somebody has lately remarked that
it supplies a Little Australian want. At any rate,
it is amusing to anyone who has read the original—
and less moral—versions of the same old myths.
The only important myth that is not in the book
is Spruce Myth.

A shortage of army officers is one of the
most serious of the problems which face British
War Minister Haldane just now. The extrava-
gant living that was formerly customaiy in all the
Guards and Cavalry, and in many of the Infantry
regiments, has lately been discouraged, and a fall-
ing off has occurred amongst the wealthy youths
who used to do a little sociable soldiering for the
sake of the gas
and gaiters at-
tached to the
business. Yet
the discourage-
ment hasn’t been
sufficient, nor is
the pay good
enough, to give
a chance to the
brainy man of no
means, who
might otherwise
make the Army
a profession. It
is hard to con-
ceive on what
principle Britain
recompenses its
officers. Thecost,
for example, of
the gorgeous
mass of steel,
iron, tin, lace,
cloth, feathers,
&c., that goes to
make up the
dress-uniform of
a Life Guards
subaltern isalone
far in excesss of
that youth’s first
year’s pay. Pos-
sibly it is tacitly
understood that in
war-time a certain lati-
tude shall be allowed these
starveling warriors in the looting line. Yet loot-
ing is a terribly precarious, and often dangerous,
method of adding to an inadequate income. And
half Britain’s wars are against people who have
nothing to be looted, except bullocks and garden
truck.
A curious breach of promise case has
struggled to a close in the Sydney courts. It was a
yellowish case, with much hard assertion in it.
The Syrian plaintiff was a lady who hawked
things with a horse and cart—a hawkeress, or
hawkerina. She had a glass eye, and said she was
24, and indignantly denied the charge of
being 40. She was attractive, barring the eye, and
the only objection to it was its glassy stare ; also,
she didn’t look 40, and her name was Cissie Zathar.
The defendant, Moses Hanna, was a Syrian
kid, who was alleged to have been only
16 when Cissie blandished him with her best eye
—or he mashed her. He was said to have sworn
by a strange saint named Maroon that he would
marry Cissie, and to have given her a handker-
chief, and to have objected to her speaking to
Malanck. The lady was partly corroborated by
her relative Bakash. On the other hand, Boy
Moses indignantly denied having ever succumbed
to the charms of Cissie, and pointed out that some
at least of his alleged love letters were signed with
the firm’s name, and dealt solely with business.
He denied having seduced the lady ; he denied
having kissed her; he denied knowing her age ;
in fact, he repudiated Cissie wholesale and retail,
except as a customer at the shop of his Syrian
parent. He was partly corroborated by Basha.
It was a long-drawn-out case, with many side
issues in it. The general tenor of the defence was
that Cissie was an elderly, brownish siren who
had beguiled little kid Moses, but an analyst gave
it as his opinion that a certain birth-entry, which
purported to provethe amazing youth of Moses; had
a curious discrepancy in it as. regards the age of the
ink used; and then Cissie was so good to look
upon that it seemed a shame to call her 40. So
the jury gave the lady a verdict of £250. Later
on, when she learns the mystery of Costs, she may
know more than she does now about the value of
a verdict for £250.

They hange d Peter Sadeek at Broken Hill
the other day (it was Broken Hill’s first hanging),
and then they held the usual inquest to find out
what he died of. After deep research they dis-
covered that there was no ground for any sus-
picion that he had perished in the bush through
want of water, and they brought in a verdict
accordingly. Then they buried Peter Sadeek, in
the blessed hope that he will preserve his balance
on the bridge of Al-Sirat at J udginent Day:

The confusion in the inner circles of the
N. S. W. Alliance has been so bad lately that Mrs
Christina Elizabeth Clark, a lady of the most
teetotal character, has been awarded heavy libel
damages against Mr. William Winn, a gentleman
who never takes any alcoholic stimulant what-
ever. About seven costly lawyers, all of whom,
as far as this paper is aware, drink a little, will
make a lot of money out of the affair. The
gentleman who doesn’t take anything will have to
pay £650 and a fearful lot of Costs, unless he gets
a new trial (possibly before a drinking Judge)
and a different kind of verdict at the end of it ;
and even that would only mean that one or other
of the strictly Cold Tea parties would have more
Costs to pay to the moderately-drinking legal
profession. And if the present verdict stands,
there is always a stack of Costs which it is
impossible for the winner to recover from the
loser; and it is quite possible for a lady who
shudders at the very sight of liquor to find a dis-
appointingly small sum left out-of a £650 verdict
gained against a conscientious Keehabite brother,
after certain drinking lawyers (drinking within
reasonable limits, of course) have had a bite out
of it. This paper can only say that it regards the
whole business as a regrettable mistake. When
two Recliabites fall out, and go to two
solicitors who keep something at home in the
decanter, and the briefs are drawn up and
submitted to barristers who (quite possibly)
smell of the whisky and soda they had at lunch,
and the case, after paying fees to all manner of
officials, who probably take a drop now and then,
goes before a Judge, who, as likely as not, enjoys
a taste occasionally, and is referred to a jury,
which, for all we know to the contrary, may
imbibe rum as a regular thing, and the c»se is
heard in a court right opposite a public-house, it
seems a grievous waste of good Rechabite money
and principle for the profit of the ungodly.
BALLADE OF A SLUDGE BOARD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sludge Board writes in an airy fashion about etc.
—Age Leading Article.
Of tribes and septs in their armor clashin’,
Historians write in tedious style ;
Of Rome and Nineveh, Carthage, Bashan,
And long-dead nations round the Nile ;
They publish volumes, pile on pile,
Nor on a tired world take compassion ;
Their books are heavy and only rile—
But the Sludge Board writes in an airy fashion.
In mood divine, from heights Parnassian,
The poet watches the stars defile,
And all things kindle to burning passion,
Where Life, and Beauty, and Love beguile—
In his bright universe naught is vile
That Fame’s eternal lightnings flash on ;
He dreams and writes with a god-like smile—
But the Sludge Board writes in an airy fashion.
ENVOI.
Prints though there be that charm awhile
The minds of men, they are grey and ashen
Beside those glories of grace and style
That the Sludge Board writes in an airy fashion !
VIC.
O. C. CABOT.

THtJoNE
J. F. Archibald’s memories in The Lone Hand
for July (a larger instalment is given than in
previous issues) covers a wide range of interest.
The old Melbourne Daily Telegraph, the
Argus, the Sydney Daily Telegraph (in the
days when it was known as The Morning Rum),
the “ Black Wednesday ” of the Berry regime,
the girlhood of Madame Melba —all these are
touched upon with bright and humorous pen.
The method of these memoirs is to tell a good
yarn whenever it occurs, and “ bother the
sequence.”
Particularly strong in fiction will the July
Lone Hand be. Some of the stories :—
“Riches of Exile,” by Howard Ashton, a
Romance of the Foundation times of Australia,
with remarkable illustrations by Norman Lindsay;
“The Ridiculous Family,” by Henry Lawson, in
which this fine writer gets back to his old form as
the limner of the Australian bushman; “ Cold
Feet,” a story of humor and heroism, by Albert
Dorrington; “The Genius,” a charming child
story, by Alice Rosman, with illustrations by
Alex. Sass; “Sturdy Does It,” a cheery yarn of
human nature, by G. Bron; “ Happy Jack,” a
humorous bush sketch, with illustrations by Alex.
Sass ; “Two Battlers and a Bear,” a continuance
of this delightful comedy series, by Edward
Dyson, with illustrations by “Hop”; “The
Secrets of a Prime Minister (No. 2): The Inci-
dent of the Beautiful Terrorist”; and several
others.
More serious articles will not be neglected.
Frank Myers has a prose poem of the Bush, and
also a glowing appreciation of artist Stieeton.
The usual literary and dramatic features are in-
cluded, and the pictures represent a very decided
improvement on previous numbers. As a frontis-
piece, a portrait of J. C. Watson in red chalk, by
Norman Carter (suitable for framing), will be
given.
Many were unable to secure the May and June
numbers. Make sure of getting The Lone Hand
every month by subscribing now, direct to the
office, or to the nearest newsagent.

“ Xley ” : I was at Halifax (N.Q.), and
scraped an accidental acquaintance with the
solitary carrier’s cart. Heaven knows what it had
done that day before I lighted on it. Anyhow, at
10 a.rn., it carted alotof empties to the station. At
11, it fetched a coffin for a Chow, who had died
from consumption or anything yqu like. At 11.30,
it carted the dead Chow and four live ones to the
cemetery. At 1.30, it brought a lot of edibles for
Governor Chelmsford’s reception at the station.
At 2.30, it carted away the triumphal arch (which
had been built by five Japanese, two Chows, two
Hindus, and a Dago, with one blackfellow looking
on), and a lot of bottles to the corporation tip. At
4, it took the scenery and props of a belated
dramatic company to the station. Then the horse
and man had a drink.

first-class artists are engaged. The establish-
ment is under the Patronage of Lord Northcote.
Address, 30 Sydney Arcade (upstairs), entrance
from George or King-streets.*

“ Thomas Clodd ” : The germ-hunters of
Maoriland are in a state of hysterics over two
alleged cases of plague in Auckland. Two girls
working in one building died. One died after a
short illness. It is alleged she wouldn’t have
died if she hadn’t been frightened to death. She
was in a weak state of health after an operation,
and the Germ Department, feeling suspicious,
sent its ambulance along, and insisted that she
should go straight to the hospital—alone, except
for the driver. No relatives or nurses were al-
lowed to accompany her. She passed out in a
hurry and a scare. The other girl had had a
tooth extracted the day before she died, and wild
yarns were circulated that she had swallowed
some mouth-wash —sworn to be harmless—and
perished in consequence. Further, it was alleged
that the girls had eaten from the same tin of
salmon, and had been killed by fish; but as they had
never even spoken to each other, this yarn was ex-
ploded in court. Just after the Bull-Boer Bait, the
Maoriland experts discovered the only case of small-
pox ever found in M.L., and burnt an enormous
quantity of soldiers’ property, besides the pro-
perty of the khakero who had the pimple. The
Germ Department published , photos without
faces—of detestable cases of smallpox, and led the
public to believe that they represented the con-
dition of the spotted ’ero, who admitted after-
wards that he had had the time of his life in
quarantine. They take epidemics in a serious
spirit in Ward’s country, and wild whirling doctors
ai e liable to set fire to the house almost before
the patient is out. of it, on suspicion that the case
is Asiatic cholera, or Black Death, or something,
or almost anything.
Australians
often meet persons
of “Oxford culture ”
who condescend to
shed light on benighted
outlying portions of the
earth, and some of them
are impressed. A recent English writer gives a
list of some of the things that one variety of
Oxford culture does not necessarily include :
No one who wishes to become a B.A. or M.A. of Oxford
is forced to study English composition or literature, the
history of the British Empire, the geography of the globe
we inhabit, the scientific discoveries and inventions
which have profoundly altered the conditions of modern
life, any of the fine arts, any of the laws of England, the
rules that guide the law courts in estimating the value
of human testimony, the art of government and econo-
mics, the art of war, or French, German, or any modern
language.
Come to think of it, an affected drawl, a languid
style of walking, and a few bits of dead language
are not much to make up for all this spacious
absence.
