So long as there are insincere class news-
papers, and “justices” who administer the law
with due regard for the rights of
“class,” so long will there be.
three kinds of “ justice ” —justice
tempered with mercy, justice
tempered with grovel, and justice
served out with a loud yell. A
late notable instance of merciful
class “justice” and newspaper
“discretion” is that which con-
cerns a young and foolish (to
say the least of it) medical
student at Melbourne Univer-
sity. At that institution there
lately occurred a series of petty
thefts, the most serious being
that of a microscope from a
woman-student, who valued the
goods at £B. The matter was
put into the hands of the police,
and they unearthed the culprit,
who was fined a fiver. In press
circles the affair was wrapped
in a heavy shroud of silence,
and hurriedly buried out of
sight, the convicted young man
not being an unfortunate can-
vasser or insurance tout who had
got away with 4s. 9d., but a per-
son whose “career” would be
spoilt by publicity. This kind
of incident, however noble and
just the decision, must embitter
the heart of the bloke who has
been put away for six months be-
cause he made off with a leg o’
mutton from a butchery, or the
sad-eyed slouch who exchanged
his worn-out bluchers for a pair
handily dangling from an opulent
shop-front. This kind of person
has no career to spoil, but he may have a “re-
cord ” —which the J.P. usually takes care to look
into before doling out mercy or dealing out judg-
ment.
* * * *
The Danysz committee still tries to bear
up against overwhelming evidence, and professes,
according to its organ, the Sydney Telegraph, to
look hopefully forward to a trial on the mainland, where
the French scientist himself anticipated probably better
results than were obtainable on the island, owing to the
influence of the sea on soil and herbage there.
But there is no chance of Danysz being
allowed to try his rabbit virus on the main-
land. He failed badly under specially favor-
able conditions. With his rabbits so crowded up
that there was every chance of them dying in any
case, the Danysz remedy failed as regards a
large proportion of them. The inference is that it
would fail much worse in the interior, where the
rabbit, instead of being penned up as a prisoner, is
leading the strenuous open-air life. It isn’t
worth while, under the circumstances, to let loose
a disease which might attack the flocks and
herds and domestic fowls, and even human
beings, though Danysz says it wouldn’t. Danysz
hasn’t been so much of a success that Aus-
tralia can afford to take his word in the
matter. He is in the position of a man who
couldn’t shoot his rabbit at close range when it
was tied to a tree, and still he claims to be a
marksman, who can be let loose to shoot
promiscuously and be trusted not to hit the wrong
animal. The Telegraph says that it was the sea air
and the herbage that spoiled his aim, but even
that eloquent assurance isn’t sufficient.
“All, my Beloved, fill the Cup.” You
may just as well fill it with the purest spirit avail-
able. Wolfe’s Schnapps is the liveliest stimu-
lant and best tonic known for mind or body.*
Melbourne bakers have the same weakness
for water as Melbourne milkmen, and several
have been prosecuted for watering their bread.
In one case the loaf was found to contain 43J per
cent, of water. Probably this baker delivered his
bread in a bucket. What with watered whisky,
watered bread, watered milk and watered stock,
water is about the most expensive commodity
going.

The result of a recent marine investiga-
tion, held at Auckland (M.L.), is highly interest-
ing. TheTe Aroha, a small paddle steamer of 50
tons, intended for river work only, was sent on a
voyage from Auckland to the west coast of the
North Island, and on the first or second night out
it foundered half a mile off the shore. The men
on board escaped by a narrow margin. The Court
found: (1) Ihat the vessel left Auckland quite un-
fit for the voyage, and in an unseaworthy condi-
tion. (2) That when it began to make water, its
bunkers were so rotten that the coal washed out
and clogged the pumps, so that there was
no chance of keeping it afloat. (3) That by
reason of their action in sending such a
death-trap to sea, the owners had broken
most of the clauses in the Maoriland Shipping
Act. (4) That, as one especially bad breakage,
the vessel was sent out without a certificate. And
this lawlessness which, but for the luck of the
weather, might have been wholesale man-
slaughter, so angered the Court that it ordered
the owner of the vessel to pay £2O 7s lOd costs
of the inquiry. Jrerhaps it was all that it could
do ; but the British Shipping Act provides heavy
fine and long imprisonment for the person who
sends out an unseaworthy ship, and the M.L. Act
is commonly believed to do likewise, and if it
does, there must be some authority with power to
proceed in such a bad case as that of the Te
Aroha.

It was alleged on analytical evidence be-
fore a Sydney coroner last week, that the Aus-
tralian Gas Go. of Sydney does not supply its
customers with coal gas, but with a mixture of
coal and water gas, which is more poisonous than
pure coal gas, and being less smellful, gives far
less warning of a leakage. The mixture has a
larger proportion of carbon monoxide—the choke-
damp of the coal pit—than pure coal gas, and that
makes the difference; it is also much cheaper to
make, and that makes the other difference. One
fatality has already been attributed to the escape
of the substance that kills you suddenly and with
little warning.
* * *
An Adelaide youth last week had to stand
and hear the Criminal Court play tennis with his
chance of liberty. It was an exciting rally. (1)
He was found guilty of assault. (2) But the vic-
tim was (as the Judge candidly remarked) “a
mature woman of shameless, filthy, and intemper-
ate conduct.” (3) But even such a woman has a
right to protection. (4) But prisoner bore an
excellent character. (5) But he had already been
in the reformatory for throwing a dog into a fire.
(6) But it was only a lot of smoking boughs, and
the deed was a “ playful act,” and didn’t damage
the dog so much as might be supposed. (7) But
the Judge had happened to be passing on that
occasion, and said it was a brutal proceeding. So
prisoner got six months for assaulting a woman,
apparently because two years ago he was unkind
to a dog in the presence of a lawyer who has since
become a Judge.

of Police, Melbourne,
has A Man Like Johnson.

on the inefficiency of the police, the Vic-
torian Police Department has given in-
structions that in future the detectives
are not, under any circumstances, to
give any information to the Press, and
a policy of secrecy has been followed
for some time.

ABORIGIN
ALITIES
THE RABBIT-PROOF FENCE.—AN
ABORIGINALITY OF SOME
TRUTH.
The recent earthquake at Kingston (S.A.)
has made the south-eastern corner of that State
very thoughtful. In 1897 there was a shock down
there every day for a year, but since then nothing
further happened till the beginning of this month.
Mount Gambier, the chief town of the district, is
built fairly in the crater of an old volcano —sup-
posed to be utterly extinct, but even the deadest
volcano can’t be wholly trusted.
“Jimmie Pannikin”: After rain at Tib-
ooburra (N.S.W.), folk go specking on the
alluvial flats in the township. Where does this
fine gold come from ? Some assert that it comes
from a parent reef, which will be found some day.
Near the granite-guarded township is a pyramidal
hill of diorite. A well-known geologist said that
all the gold in the alluvial came from that knob.
The heat and cold, rains and winds of centuries,
by saturation and desiccation, gradually wore
down a mountain of diorite into a little hill ; and
the gold thus set free has been washed by storms
over the flats. I believe that rich gold deposits
will be found in the curious dry water-courses of
that neglected and remote region.
* * * *
Tell of the good of it,
Say what you should of it,
All that you could of it.
Say what you would of it,
Tell of colds cured by it,
Good health ensured by it,
Long life secured by it—
Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*
“ Lindy Lou ” : Of all pests to which the
goldminer in alluvial tunnelling country is sub-
ject, the worst is the man who neglects to timber.
Sometimes he is too mean to buy timber ; other
times he is too lazy to split it. Sometimes it is
simply recklessness on his part; but anyway he’s
a curse. He will boast of his cheap tunnel when
he is in 200ft. or 300ft., and he claims that his
knowledge of how to slope the sides of the orifice
at the correct angle makes it as safe as a building
society. But it always ends the same way. Some
day his trucker tears along yelling that Bill is at
the face and the tunnel has pinched. Then all
hands rush as fast as their feet will carry them to
the spot, and graft their souls out, and use their
own timber in making things safe behind them.
And all the while they tell one another of the
things they will say to the fool when they get
him out. But, when they find him, he is gasping
and all but done for, and he moans, “Gawd ! but
I thought it was all up this time.” Then they
forgive him, and spill good liquor down his neck.
Also, they put him on to another show, and give
the “ pore devil ” enough stuff to keep him going
for six months. But such is the nature of miners.
* * * *
“ Plumbob ” : I recently caused the death
of some hoodies in an unusual manner. The
boodie is a small animal with a snout like a
miniature pig, though elongated a trifle. He
goes through life wearing a deferential, apologetic
look, and only ventures out at night. When con-
fronted with a light he is quite blind, and keeps
side-stepping to avoid a danger of which he is
conscious though its whereabouts is a mystery.
Iii this state it is quite easy to kill him ; but
killing things causes me no pleasurable sensations.
So I made an agreement with them that they
were not to touch the salt and flour, and I put
the rest of the goods beyond their reach. After
a while they used to pick up the fragments that
remained after a meal quite fearlessly. But I
woke one morning to find most of the self-raising
flour inside two boodies. They had the distorted
abdomens of those who take risks, and even in
death their eyes wore the reproachful “ vvhy-did-
I-trust-you ” look of those whose confidence has
been abused.
* * * *
“ Percole ” : Talking about hybrids (which
we ain’t), I saw something the other day which
knocks the ’possum-cat into a billycock hat. In
a densely-timbered, fertile desert, somewhere
about central New South Australia, I started
a peculiar animal, which appeared to be half
dog, half reptile, half nondescript, and the,
other half nothing in particular. Its front legs
were twice the length of the hind ones, and when
it made a noise it was something between a silent
roar and an ear-splitting whisper. When it was
silent it didn’t make any noise at all. It slid
along the ground at what can only be described
as a crawling gallop, and disappeared into the
scrub with a shriek like the soulling of a lost
whale. An unnaturalist has since told me that I
was fortunate enough to see the only existing
specimen, alive or dead, of the most mysterious
animal known to zoology, namely the dingoanna.
There is only one other creature as scarce (he
informs me), and that is the hybrid which is a
cross between a two-legged bird and a pain in the
jaw, known as the cockatoothache.
* * *
“ David H. Richards” : One of the most
exasperating experiences that can befall anyone
in this exasperating world is to be camped for a
lengthy stretch with a mate who can descant on
nothing but his past drunks. Especially if it
rains for 39 days and nights, and you have nothing
to read, and no gun wherewith to do murder, and
no lines wherewith to fish, and nothing but the
wetness of Nature to help you bear your lot,
and restrain you from slaying your com-
panion to an excessive deadness.
Then you get into the poetic habit
of yearning, and you yearn with a
yearning vast and solid for some-
one who will say something about
the man who got a slush-lamp
and went searching for honest
men, but found only land agents;
or about the ideas of Nero in
the matter of matricide, or the
amours of Paris, or the Dago
Renaissance, or the dry-rot in
modern English drama, or the
advisability of an Australian Life
Assurance Department, or Jason,
or skipper Kidd, or the millen-
nium, or the glory of the ever-
lasting stars, or the question
whether Cain married a monkey,
or about the canoes that go by
in the night, or the smoke-room
yarns of Boccacio —anything, in
fact, but beer. The sound of your
mate’s voice makes you curse beer;
and a man has to be desperately exas-
perated before he’ll curse beer.
“ F.H.R.” : The Australian bushman is a
versatile crittur, and takes naturally to batching
in general. Most of him can cook as well as eat.
Most of him can sew some, and spends much time
putting decent patches on trousers that ar&hardly
worth the time and trouble; but though he washes
his own clothes, he is not an expert at laundry
work, and always considers it effemininate as well
as laborious. The part he really excels in is the
wringing. He generally gets a mate, if one is
handy, and they twist opposite ways for all they
are worth. Neither would allow the other to
wring the end out of his grasp, so it develops into
a muscular competition something similar to a
scratch pull. When not another drop of moisture
can be squeezed out, they unfold the trousers or
shirt, and generally find it twisted into rags.
Then busliie hangs it across a log where the
goats can get at it, and is content.
■Jr * * •
Tickling in the throat seems trifling, but
you know these little, insignificant complaints
gradually develop into the most serious, unless
checked in time. Nature invariably sounds the
alarm, and if you don’t heed, disastrous results
generally follow. When there is a tickling in the
throat you may rest assured there is some cause;
some foreign substance lodged or some inflamma-
tion. You must take Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy. You can feel at once its soothing,
quietening, and healing influence. It is no ex-
periment. Not only does Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy reduce the inflammation, but strengthens
the delicate lining of the throat, makes it stronger
and more able to throw off pulmonary diseases.*
“Oxo.” : The altered climatic conditions
of Southern Gippsland, during late years, seem
to support the contention that the rainfall is con-
trolled by the forest areas. Old inhabitants aver
that, previous to the influx of settlers and the
consequent clearing, the rainfall was considerably
heavier. Now the climate is becoming somewhat
similar to that of the Northern districts. Minus
its copious rainfall, Gippsland is inferior to
Northern Victoria for fertility, and, placed under
similar conditions, must be superseded thereby.
Agriculturally speaking, Gippsland is very
patchy, and the Bent Closer Settlement policy as
applied to it is a farce. The Allambee Estate,
now being subdivided, is in a fearfully precipitous
district, road grades being, in some instances, as
steep as one in three.
NEW YEAR MUSINGS.
INEBRIATED PARTY (at the ringing out of the old year) ; “ Lesh sheel
thish year or lasht year, or the nex year.”
“ J.G.” relates a mean-man story :
He was a big bachelor cocky, and owned over 900 acres
of land in Northern Victoria. One day, whilst riding"
around his paddock, he was thrown off his horse, or got
bucked off, or the horse shied and he slipped off, or he got
drunk and fell off. Whichever it was, his neighbor saw him
lying on the ground one red hot afternoon, and, going-
over, found that the mean man was unconscious, and
that his leg was broken. As the damaged one had no
relatives or friends about the place, the neighbor had
him brought to his (the neighbor’s) house, and bound up
the damaged part of him as best he could. Then he
harnessed a horse in a trap and drove the sufferer
to the local hospital. On the way the broken-legged
mean man revived a little, and asked the sound legged
generous man to look after his farm until he recovered.
The sound-legged man agreed to do this, and suggested
that the broken-legged man should leave his valuables in
his charge. But the broken-legged man said he wouldn't
trust anybody with them. He had his bank book, show-
ing a credit of several thousands. And perhaps he had
an idea that his neighbor would forge the broken-legged
man’s name, and draw out the money. After he
was admitted into the hospital and put to bed,
his clothes were taken away, as is the custom in hos-
pitals, so that the broken-legged man could not get
up until the doctor allowed him. Whilst the wardsman
was hanging up the broken-legged man’s coat, the bank
book dropped out. The warder carried it to the secre-
tary for safe keeping. The broken-legged man was in
the hospital for about four months, and when he was
going out, the secretary suggested that he should give a
donation to the hospital funds. The ex-broken-legged
cocky then pitched a whining tale to the effect that he
was too poor to do this ; he had no money, owing to
droughts and high-priced labor, and his farm was
carried on at a big logs, and he only kept it because it
was a hobby of his. “Oh, I see,” said the secretary,
“ then this is not your book. I thought it was. It must
belong to somebody else of the same name.” Thin the
ex-broken-legged man felt his coat pocket, and all but
fainted, as he recognised that his book was in the hands
of the enemy. Finally he said he would give 10s. to the
hospital funds. But the secretary pointed out that per-
sons with means had to pay £2 a week, and the wretched
ex-broken-legged man didn’t get away until he hail
signed a cheque for £4O. Then he went home, and al-
though his neighbor had looked after his farm for him
all the time he was in the hospital, he didn’t as much as
say “ thank you.”
“G. Elburg ” : Remember a joyous raid
once on a fan-tan establishment in Port Darwin,
round which Governors and Ms.P. have just been
wandering. The fighting trooper was stationed
in the yard at the rear, while the Inspector and
another made their way in through the front.
The backyard sentry noticed a Wood-pile, so
selected a waddy of convenient length and weight,
and stationed himself near the door. After sounds
of trouble in the building, the door burst open,
and a yellow stream emerged in haste, and imme-
diately struck hard trouble about the head. The
Inspector came last, with a Chow’s neck in each
hand. “ Made any arrests, constable ? ” he asked.
The trooper pointed to seven recumbent bodies.
“ Take your pick, sir,” he responded respectfully.
“ Sancho ” : “ Micawber ” (B. 30/5/’O7) is
“up the pole ” in his remarks upon push phrase-
ology. “Bonzer,” long since defunct, has been
succeeded by “Just the mud.” Anything at all
off-color is derided as being a horrible “ frisco.”
Possibly the word is derived partly from “fiasco’’
and partly from the Californian earthquake.
* * * *
“D.H.R.”: Re bush J’s.P. (B. 2/5/’O7).
A man of many acres but diminutive erudition
sat, like Sancho Panza of old, upon the court
stump to hear a dispute between two others of his
kind over a bucket. The first man said he had
lent the second a bucket to bring some water from
the river, and he hadn’t returned the article —as.
a matter of fact, he had let the utensil slip.
After much evidence (which consisted mainly of
recriminations and reflections from both sides on
the other side’s ancestors) my J.P. asked the
plaintiff what value he placed on the vessel.
“Twenty-five bob, ver Honor,” replied the
plaintiff. “ What !” roared the astounded beak.
“Twenty-five bob for a bucket!”
“Twenty-five bob for a bucket” is a byword on
the Warrego to this day. In the early days of
Bourke, a newly-invested J.P. with a mouth for
booze was dragged, in the absence of all other
authority, to adjudicate on his maiden case, which
was one of riotous behavior. When the J.P. had
been assisted on his tottering way to the Bench,
the solitary constable of those parts went off into
a lengthy recital of cross-counters and frayed
uniform. When he had ended, a silence heavier
than a sermon fell over the precincts. For awhile
the limb o’ the law waited for a conviction to
reward his eloquence, till suddenly the cop realised
that the beak had fallen asleep. Hastily, and
almost irreverently, he awoke him and explained
matters. “ Oh, take the fellow out and hang
him ! ” was the verdict.
GOIN DOWN TO TOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the last mob’s shorn and raddled,
And it’s Christmas time once more,
And the old mare’s standin’ saddled,
Svvishin’ flies off at the door ;
Oh, you’re light of heart and merry,
And you’re strong, and fit, and brown,
And it’s good-bye, Tom and Jerry,
For you’re goin’ down to town.
Goin’ down to town,
Strong and fit and brown,
With a cheque pinned in your pocket,
You are goin’ down to town.
At the rails the boys are waitin’
With commissions by the score,
And old Bill is just debatin’
If he’ll have “ a dozen more.”
And it’s cartridges and baccer,
And you start to write ’em down,
Till you reckon you must yakker
When you’re goin’ down to town.
Goin’ down to town
(At last the rails are down),
And the old mare’s like a filly
When you’re goin’ down to town.
Oh, it’s grand the boyish riot
That is runnin’ in your heart,
When the whirrin’ Wolselev’s quiet,
And you’re ready to depart
From a year of solid graftin’,
On the plains so bare and brown,
From the beltin’ and the shaftin’
You are goin’ down to town.
Goin’ down to town,
To buy the girl a gown—
Oh, your heart is full of music
When you’re goin’ down to town.
And you pass old Regan’s quarter
Skirtin’ out a mile or more,
For you know that Regan’s daughter
W.li be waitin’ at the door ;
And you reckon you suspect her
When it comes to “ lambin’ down ” ;
So you’re dodgin’ Regan’s nectar
When you’re goin’ down to town.
Goin’ down to town,
Nary lambin’ down,
You’ll get lots of better l'cker
When you’re goin’ down to town.
And you feel a strange emotion
While the mare is goin’ free,
For you’re thinkin’ of the ocean,
And the salt breath of the sea ;
Oh, you’ll see the breakers swingin’
Where the misty headlands frown,
And you burst out sudden singin’
When you’re goin’ down to town !
Goin’ down to town
To buy the girl a gown—
Oh, the old mare shakes her bridle
When you’re goin’ down to town.
H.S.W.
HORAOK CHARLES HALLORAN.

“ Quan” supplies some camel lore :
While the smell of a camel will cause horses (or, at
least, such of them as are not on everyday speaking terms
with that personification of stink) to light out for the
horizon, it has the opposite effect upon cattle, which will
charge the perambulating smell with 19 h.p. energy.
Yet, if the camel bellows, most cattle, even in the height
of their maddest charge, will turn tail. The horse is
supposed to have a good memory ; yet horses which are
accustomed to camels, but which go for a few months
to a place where there are none, are, upon their return
just as afraid of them as if they had never seen them pre-
viously.
* « * *
“ Krys. W.” : The cakewalk had pene-
trated to the bush darnce, but only one couple
from somewhere nearer civilisation had any idea
of dancing it. Accordingly, when the concertina
started the inevitable “When the Billy Boils
Over,” this particular pair commenced to prance
and throw their legs about and shy at each other
and at the startled push. Finally, just as they
were doing their best, the “skirt” somehow got
her foot caught in the pocket of the bloke in
front, and came down a “bosker.” So did the
bloke, whose rightful property the pocket was.
Before the prancers behind could stop they
wandered on top of the fallen, and sat down
abruptly also. The swearing of the M.C. and the
wail of the horrified concertina added to the
general din. Finally the prostrate crowd was
sorted out, and the “skirt’s” foot was got out of
the bloke’s pocket. Whereupon the M.C. rose
and went for the offenders with the following
gentle exhortation, plentifully strewn with
“color”: “Look ’ere, you bloke an’ his piece,
next time yer comes ter a darnce, darnce that
city hop all ther’ way ’ere, and knock off when
yer gits inside.”
“Wang”: Sweet charity, what crimes
are committed in thy name l I remember a few
years ago, at Wangaratta (Vic.), a “carnival” in
aid of the local hospital. Amongst the items for
the amusement of the assembled crowd was a wild
bullock riding competition by local Buffalo
Williams, A particularly miserable walking-
beef was hauled into the arena. Then 13-stone
weight of gladiator leapt on his back and started
ripping him with spurs. Also a man came along
with a pointed stick, and gave him a few ungentle
reminders that he had to caper about in the cause
of charity. But he refused to caper, and after he
had lost a pint or two of blood and a few square
inches of hide he uttered a moan of resignation,
and lay down. A little farther away I noticed
another hoodlum with a blackened face, standing
in a barrel, holding a dilapidated brolly in front
of himself, while a number of tomfools fired lumps
of wood, weighing about 31b. each, at him at a
ha penny a shot. He ducked whenever one
whistled near his cranium, and thereby escaped
ruin. Had he ducked a moment too” late, he
would have been laid up for repairs, or buried, or
something, and either the gamfe would have had
to cease, or some other jackass would have had to
take the tattered gamp, and dodge the flying
timber in the blessed cause of benevolence.

“Paul Cupid” : The common bush theory
that a snake will not try to go away if you watch
it is both true and false. A person may watch a
reptile while another person goes for a stick, and
never take his eyes off, and still the serpent may
crawl leisurely away when it feels inclined. Yet
if the watcher knows his business, and is wa tch.ng
a snake at, say, Dan, he can keep it there while
his companion goes to Beersheba for a stick. A
snake is, in one re-
spect, something like
the Speaker of the
House ; you must
catch his eye before
you can do much
good. When the rep-
tile crawls away
while being watched,
it is clear that the
watcher has his eyes
fixed on its body or
tail, which is no
good. To keep a
reptile stationary
you should, if pos-
sible, get straight in
front of it, and con
centrate your gaze
steadily on a spot
between the serpent’s
beady optics. Keep
your gaze on the
same spot, don’t
blink, and that snake
will not try to go
away from you,
though it may start
to come towards you.
■But don’t lie afraid ;
it won’t bite. If you
are afraid, don’t try
the business at all.
The reason the rep-
tile will not go away
is because man can
fascinate a snake, if
he only knows how
to apply the power.
No doubt it is this
circumstance that
gave rise to the be-
lief of some people
that if you put any-
thing on the ground
in front of a snake, it
will .watch it while
you go for the al-
ways-absent stick. I
knew a city Johnny
who had heard this.
Johnny was saunter-
ing by a dam, study-
ing the landscape,
w hen he nearly
stepped on a mag-
nificent specimen of
Pseudechis porphy-
riaeus. Johnny gave the first leap of his life,
and making a flank movement, put his hard-
hitter down about 20yds. in front of the snake,
and then went in search of a stick. In about 20
minutes he returned with four fathoms of young
tree on his shoulder. The snake was gone.
Johnny threw the stick into the dam, com-
menting to himself on the unreliableness of
bush legendry in general. Then he picked up
his hat, and the snake hurriedly uncoiled itself and
darted away, while Johnny gave the second jump
of his life, and said “Bai Jove!” and “ Bai ” several
other things.
“8.0. V.” : I’d never make a living as a
murderer. I had the job of killing a fowl last
week, so asked Smith to “Cumangiveusahand,”
and even offered to go for a bottle of beer whilst
Smith did the job on his own. Smith wanted to
go for the beer too, so in the end we both took
part in the execution. After chasing the feathered
biped for 23 minutes, we caught it and tied its
legs. We then prepared the block and axe, same
as for Polly, Queen of Scots. The fowl persist-
ently refused to keep its head on the block, and I
refused to hold it on when I saw the madness
with which Smith lashed out with the axe. So
we fixed its legs to a peg in the ground, put a
string round its head and pulled its neck over the
block. Then Smith belted all the universe with
the axe and split the heavens. He almost severed
the bird’s neck. With an irresistible flutter it
left its moorings, and flew up, peg and all, on to
the shed, and there, with its head hanging on by
a few feathers, it gave three agonising crows and
then flapped back on to the ground. Smith and I
watched further operations from the bathroom
window, with the door locked. After 11 minutes
we took courage and a washing-board, and went
out and smote the fowl to death. Bloodstained,
we brought the executed bird to the kitchen, and
learned that we had killed the only rooster by
mistake. I refused to do any more killing.
Smith went home drunk. I’d sooner go Judding
than slay another fowl.

“ Combo Jim ” writes (there is red dust in
his letter, and the postmark signifies that it comes
from the desert at the back of everything save
the crow, the rabbit, and the dingo ; it is even
west of the mortgage):—
“ Jimmie Pannikin” asks if any contributor can throw
light on the history of those conical-shaped aboriginal
stones found west-o’-Darling-. Having lived all my life in
the district referred to, I can give “Jimmie IV’ the
explanation furnished by the aborigines themselves. The
stones are called “Death Stones, and the birth and
death of every member of a family is marked on that
family’s stone, much the same as‘in our own family
Bible We do not bury the Bible with the deceased.
Neither do they bury their “ Death Stones, but keep
them as records to hand from one generation to another.
The stones are family possessions, not tribal. Therefore
when the family becomes extinct, through sickness or
war, the stone is left behind by the tribe when it shifts
camp. A nigger carries nothing that is of no use to him.
The various compositions of the stones are due to the
laziness of the aborigine; he uses the handiest stone
he can find wherever he may be when he wants one. In
some districts slate predominates, in others sandstone,
and so on. The markings represent the births and deaths
of the blackfellows’gins and the piccaninnies of the house-
hold that at one time possessed the geological relic,
Presumably when a stone becomes covered with records,
the family gets a new boulder and leaves the old on
behind, but that is mere theory.*

“ Ruru ” : Forethought is not usually
reckoned a Maori virtue, but the Maori matron
displays it sometimes, F’rinstance, at the Eller-
she races (Auckland), lately, a gorgeous brown
female vision paced the lawn. Her silk skirt was
of that aggressive shade of pink which borders on
cerise. Her flannel jacket was the color of a
purple shriek. An emerald-green scarf was round
her throat, and a scarlet one floated airily from
the Panama hat. But the general effect was
marred by a bulky brown paper parcel carried
under one arm. The reason of the load appeared
later, when a heavy shower drove the crowd to
the shelter of the trees. Here the wahine publicly
divested herself of all her gorgeous upper raiment,
produced from her parcel a black stuff skirt and a
shocking hat, put them on in the full glare of
publicity, packed up her red and green helong-
ings, and once more faced the weather. That
visiting clerical potentate who once changed his
robes on the pavement in Sydney wasn’t a cir-
cumstance to the lady for calm publicity.
BALLADE OF BARRACKING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For cricket dead, let fall no tender tear,
Another altar claims the oil and wine ;
The Pigskin God of Winter, He is here !
And now, with parted lips and eyes a-shine,
There runs in rapture to St. Rugby’s shrine
The Barraeker who, taking little reck,
Cries to the skies with speech incarnadine,
“Jump on 'is chest, an’ twist his blanky neck."
With shrieking joy he views the fray severe ;
The crackle of the dislocated spine,
The hanging scalp, the freshly severed ear,
Fill all his being with a joy divine.
To higher horrors all his thoughts incline ;
While grandstand weaklings shudder at the wreck
He gaily doth his campaign plan define :
“ Jump on ’is chest, an’ screw his blanky neck.”
The Referee, with vitriolic jeer,
He doth to hells quite fathomless assign,
When—checking Murder in her wild career —
The ruling whistle lifts its piercing whine ;
While from his soul, as from a noisome mine,
He digs out reeking curses by the peck,
And bellows fearsomely in frenzy fine,
“ Jump on ’is chest, an’ break his blanky neck.”
Envoy.
Blokes I Now the Maori from across the Brine
Approaches on the heaving steamer’s deck.
To bear our laurels back is his design,
“ Jump on ’is chest, an’ rend ’is blanky neck.”

PERSONAL
ITEMS
The violently British character of his com-
munications suggests that Melbourne Herald’s
“Special Commissioner” to the Imperial Confer-
ence ought to be known as Colonel Hoo-Reay.
Queensland’s J. G. Drake, pressman,
Senator, Attorney-General, and soldier, has, it is
gazetted, permission to retire from his life of
martial enterprise and still wear his uniform. So
Drake will have at least one coat that isn’t turned.
When he is not on his travels, Vic.
Premier Bent regularly carts home for Sunday
leading a bundle of the papers that are politically
opposed to him. Rather a mortifying way to
spend the Sabbath, but at least it gives the victim
a chance of learning something, which is more
than can be said for the Unpleasant Juddy After-
noon.
It took the late Hannah King Lethbridge,
of Bay of Islands, 91 years to get tired of Maori-
land, and she is entered on the page of fame as the
first white woman born in Geyserland. The field
of Waterloo was hardly tidied up when she
eventuated. Her father was chief officer of the
brig Active, which went to M.L. in 1814 with a
load of missionaries, whose descendants now have
their holy tentacles around territory that should
support thousands of small men. He tired of the
sea, and went to Sydney, where he married a Miss
Tollis, daughter of an army captain, and the pair
returned to the Bay of Islands, where the first
white girl-child was born.
“Madame 8.,” who was convicted in
Sydney, last December, of murder by means of an
illegal operation, and sentenced to imprisonment
for life, is dead, after six months’ gaol, spent in
the hospital.' The original sentence was death,
but the lady with the initial was evidently dying
on her own account, and the Law declined to
hurry the process. “ Madame,” to all intents and
purposes, received no punishment at all, as she
would have been in hospital anyhow ; still it is
hard to see what else the law could have done.
The only Australian Judge who had nerve enough
to recommend the hanging of a dying person (he
would have recommended the hanging of a dead
one if he thought it would do any good) is dead
himself now.
The last female of the Swan River (W.A.)
blacks died lately. She wasn’t even a queen, as
the ancient female black survivor is generally
said to be; on the contrary, she was, as she
admitted, a shockingly bad lot, even judged by
her own tribal laws ; and some of her quasi-matri-
monial experiences led to her hurried and pro-
longed absence from the Swan River haunts of
her people, which was probably why she was the
last of the Mohicans. According to. white-
pfeller notions also, she was a bad old lot, who
spent her last moments vigorously cursing every-
body who wouldn’t bring her beer. Her name
was Balbuk Yooreel, or “Fanny ” for short; and
Subiaco’s society people may now score it off their
visiting lists for good

Premier Price, on his way back to Adel-
aide from Brisbane, found time to look in at the
inter-State football match in Melbourne, S.A. v.
Victoria (Australian rules); but he had to leave
to catch the express, thereby missing the finest
finish on record. He told the reporters in Adel-
aide next morning that various Vic. legislators
had barracked for their own side ; “ but I think I
made more noise than a dozen of them while I
was there,” said Price with modest pride.
A young Adelaide lawyer, son of the
deceased veteran Villeneuve Smith, has had his
character grievously assaulted by a barmaid who
was giving evidence in the Criminal Court. He
was trying to show that she was unsober on a
given occasion, and she retorted with “You have
forgotten the time when you asked me if I would
like to have a drink with you, and I said to you
that I did not drink.” The shocked barrister
protested that he had never seen the lady before,
and offered to go into the box and swear to it.
But the Court had no time for this side issue ; so
the lawyer may go down to his grave under the
stigma of having once said, “Will you join us,
miss?” to a young lady behind a bar.
“ Thomas Clodd ” from Maoriland :
Likely that William Jones, Maoriland’s stop-gap Pre-
mier, who works harder and blows less than any other
M.L. Minister, will drop polities pretty soon. Fact is,
the straight-going Jones has been turned on too often to
do several men’s work, and he is now a mere case of
angina pectoris. It is pathetic, in the light of Jones’
break-up, that only a week or two ago he warned a mob
of school children that politics was merely “dead-sea
fruit, beautiful outwardly, but dust and disappointment
within.” The Tory press sneered volubly until the
wretched words ‘ heart-disease” were whispered. Jones
is the one man of the M.L. Ministry whom no one has
ever accused of deceit or duplicity, and it will be a hard
job for Ward to find anyone equally respectable for gap-
filling if William goes out of the political industry.
A Sydney firm of auctioneers is offering
for public sale a life assurance policy for £982 on
the life of Dan O’Connor, at present on tour, and
last heard of in Tipperary. The man who buys
the policy looks for his profit, of course, in the
insured’s early death. Till that happens he has
simply to pay premiums and keep on hoping.
Also he has to keep an eye on the insured, so that
he can prove his death. If the insured wanders
over the horizon of his friends, and dies at last
unidentified in unknown parts, the buyer may go
on paying premiums for ten years after he is dead,
and then have hard work to get the money. Of
course, Dan won’t do that; a man with such a
persuasive voice and such whiskers couldn’t be
lost. But Daniel is only 63, and he has just
dodged an earthquake, a burning city, and a fall-
ing hotel, without turning a hair.
Castlemaine claims that it possesses the
venerablest inhabitant in all Victoria—Joseph
Hicks, who is said to have just turned into the
102nd lap of his earthly race. Castlemaine has
to take'Joseph’s word for it to some extent, for
about 1806 birth records were very loosely
kept, and the race of quite reliable centen-
arians isn’t due for nearly 50 years yet.
Anyhow Joseph is said to have been at
Forest Creek since 1855, and in the ’6o’s of
last century he is alleged to have owned a
three-horse vehicle, which took prisoners from the
goldfields to Melbourne at £7 a head. Some of
them were hanged on arrival. Hanging didn’t
cost anything like £7 a head.
Carruthers is the most absolutely boss
Premier New South Wales has had since the days
of Parkes. It is said that he will not
allow any of his Ministers to spend
more than 2gd. without reference to
him, and. in Cabinet, Hogue, Lee, and
Moore, if they have any opinions,
keep them out of sight unless they
know they agree with the views of
Pretty Cocky. Even Wade hides
under the table when Carruthers
frpwns. (Waddell will probably crawl
under a chair.) Carruthers was
schooled by Parkes, and the old man
always declared there could only be
one opinion in his Cabinet, and that
opinion must be his own. Bruce
Smith and McMillan were trouble-
some, but Carruthers learnt his lesson
better than the others. And the
result only shows how violently a
futile opportunist can drive a team of
oxen to nowhere in particular. Car-
ruthers is a hard driver, who hasn’t
the faintest idea of his destination.
Melbourne’s champion revenue
provider— Samuel Pinnock — turned
up again at Port Melbourne Court,
the other morning, to pay fines for
offences committed vicariously—by his
employees, to wit. Samuel’s weakness
is sand—plain, yellow sand, belonging
to Edward VII.—which he persists in
removing without the owner’s consent.
Pinnock has paid about £1000 in fines,
and this would have bought about all
the sand in Bent’s electorate, but he
prefers to “shake” it and chance get-
ting caught. And chance has been a bad*friend
to Samuel.
Frank admission of one Richard Pearse, a
Ballarat city councillor : —
1 know I am an ignorant old fool.
It was this Pearse who was Mayor of Ballarat
when a banquet was given to Lord Tennyson, and
his offhand style of oratory kept the table in an
uproar. Mrs. Peacock tried in vain to restrain
her Alexander from letting off his historical
guffaw, and Lady Tennyson had tears in her eyes
when honest old Dick Pearse touched her on the
blue-blooded shoulder with his friendly paw and
remarked, with unction, “Me and ’er ladyship ’es
as thick es if we’d known each other orl our lives,
ain’t we, yer ladyship ?” Pearse wanted to run
for the State Parliament in the Anti-Sosh in-
terest, but only got three votes on the nomination.
They can have their picturesque funerals
even on the lonely fossiekers’ fields of the Far
North. The other day a tin-washer, a veteran of
70 named James Moodie, died on Gowrie Creek
(Q.); and his mates (as far as any fossicker ever
has mates) buried him in his battered sluice-box
in a grave dug on his claim. A worn-out old
mate, and his worn-out tools, and a worn-out
claim —there was a funeral for them all.
Divorce Court Justice Simpson (of N.S. W.)
is the only remnant of a band of gallants.who once
made Sydney “Block” (George-street and King-
street) a sort of local Rotten Row. When he and
Dailey and some of the other departed were young
bloods, “doing the Block ” was much more than a
mere" 1 ' walk round town,” as it is now. To watch
the excellently-groomed Judge strutting past the
Post Office or" along King-street is quite a lesson
in how it should be done. After he finished the
Crick-Willis trial at Darlinghurst'he took the first
tram to the city, and was quite the biggest man
on the Block that day. He doesn’t often miss
once round on a fine afternoon.
NO LINGUIST.
“ But why have you given up golf ? ”
“It's only temporarily. I’m going in for a cours
of bullock driving to make my vocabulary
last around the links.”
General Gordon, N.S.W. Commandant,
has two nicknames in the service. One is
“Maria,” but that is really one of his Christian
names—Joseph Maria. When he is not “Maria,”
he is “the Army.” The S. Australian Govern-
ment made him a Brigadier-General when the
Defence Force of that State consisted of few more
than himself and the barracks cat. When he is
neither “ Maria ” nor “ the Army,” he is “ G.—
Mark II.,” to distinguish him from the Inspector-
General, who is “ G. —Mark I.”
N.S.W. Entomologist Froggatt gets away
for 12 months to interview the insects of the
world. An inquiring little man is the Entomolo-
gist, and when he is in his office in the time-worn
Admiralty Stores at Circular Quay, the room con-
tains about as much knowledge regarding insects
as can be found in all the rest of Australia put
together. Froggatt is an enthusiast, and would
chase a fly right up to the Gulf if it were a new fiv.
And he always overtakes his fly in the end.
Jimmy Hogue, now N.S.W. Education
Minister, who has represented the Glebe electors
ever since he has been in Parliament, reckons he
holds the seat in the hollow of his paw. So when
he heard that a local person named Cole intended
to challenge his selection as the Deform candidate,
Jimmy smiled. Later on he heard that Cole was
a probable winner at the ballot. So Mr. Hogue
had to throw himself about violently, make
speeches, distribute handbills, and toil like any
common or backyard candidate. At the selection
he scraped home by a bare 18 votes. Machine
politics are going strong in N.S.W. these days,,
and the Deform Juggernaut nearly ran over
Hogue.
In the days when Dick O’Connor, father
of the High Court Judge, was Clerk of N.S.W.
Parliament, and Henry Parkes kept a shop in
Hunter-street, there was a certain link between
the two men—Parkes mended flutes badly, and
O’Connor blew through flutes moderately. O’Con-
nor had blown away some of the innards of his
pet flute, and Parkes botched it for a pecuniary
consideration. O’Connor’s feelings about Parkes’
work were too deep for words at the moment. A
few years later, when Parkes, M.L.A., signed the
roll for the first time, he glanced up at the indig-
nant Irishman. “You don’t remember me, I
suppose,” said Parkes. “Oh, don’t I,” said
O’Connor. “You’re the omadhaun that ruined
my flute for me.” The comparatively hairless
Parkes of those days said nothing. Neither his
whiskers nor his vocabulary were full-grown then.
A tale of the brush :—•
A kindly Australian artist of some distinction tells of
an interview he had with a broken brush. The brother
brush was a pavement specialist doing landscapes, ships,
and still-life studies on a Melbourne footpath. He was
very depressed. The Melbourne people had no apprecia-
tion of art, he said, and he had gathered only lOd. in
coppers for his day’s work. The artist was touched by the
woes of his humble brother. “ Extend your gallery,” he
said. “Give them something they'd like. Here, while
you’re working on that, I’ll sketch in a picture at the
other end.” The painter took a few chalks, and set to
work, and turned out quite a vivid color-sketch of two
footballers jumping for the ball. The big man was rather
proud of his work. He was anxious to see how the public
would take to it. The pavement specialist arose and
examined it Critically. Then he took his rag and started
to demolish the drawing. “ Pretty good for an amateur,”
he said. “ But, of course, I can’t leave it. A man in my
jine can’t afford to trifle with his reputation.”
The N.S.W. State election casts its shadow
ahead. 01.1 members and fresh candidates can
now be seen promenading in public with their
wives, and even (in desperate cases) carrying a
baby. They stand up a good deal in trains to
give a lady a seat (especially if the tram is robing
through the candidate’s own constituency). They
help ladies and children into trams and trains. If
the candidate has a friend with him in a public
vehicle he is often heard discussing serious topics
with that friend, denouncing vice and speaking
highly of simple home virtues. His boots begin
to creak as he enters church (this attracts atten-
tion), and he drops his coin into the plate from a
greater elevation (this, makes it bounce). Also
Abbott, of Wingen’s burning mountain, has
started writing letters to the papers, and Abbott,
of Wingen, is a sure election barometer.

William Zeal, the ancient Victorian Tory
politician, has been conducting a fierce contro-
versy with a parson member of the Queen’s Statue
Committee, re the infamous conduct of the com-
mittee in not giving him a front place high up at
the unveiling of the image. His claim is based
on the fact that he contributed more than some of
those who were quite conspicuously situated, full
in the eye of the camera. Never did the ArgOs
permit a more ignoble controversy to leave a
muddy footprint in its columns, but the argument
at least shows how little mind is necessary tc
make a successful President of the Leg. Council,
and in making manifest the ease with which Zeal
outruns discretion.
Perth papers record the transfer of a wine
and beer license from J. M. Lofthouse to G. F.
Wheatley. Latter, only five years ago, was presi-
dent of the Westralian Methodist Conference.
He retired from the ministry to go into the land-
agency business, and in the course of four strenuous
years he has been orchardist, secretary to the
National Political League, Parliamentary candi-
date, boss of a printing establishment, restaura-
teur, lay preacher and publican ! Presumably he
has now found his vocation. Wheatley’s pre-
decessor in the Conference was T. A. James, of
giddy memory and parts unknown —the gentle-
man who wasn’t drowned, but merely went on
a whirl with a girl.
Died at Casino (N.S.W.), on June 2,
allegedly aged 97, T. H. Green, who took a
prominent part in the foundation of Brisbane,
and was a shareholder in the first steamship
company formed at Moreton Bay. For the last
15 years or so he lived a hermit’s life at Casino.
THE FREE AUTOGRAPH.
Alfred William Eustace, from Chiltern (Vic.), was the
first to use gum-leaves as a medium for painting dis[?]
scenes. Specimens of his art on gum-leaves s nt. to the
late Queen Victoria, the Emperor of Germany, and the
Czar of Russia, were acknowledged by autograph letters.
But why trouble about yum-leaves when a hand
painted bomb would be equally effective !
“ 1i.8.” writes :
Died at Melbourne, the other day, Lieut.-Colonel Niue!
William Kelly, a big, fine, picturesque sort of soldier who
had seen service in S’Africa, and who knocked down land
with a little hammer in Melbourne when the war was
over. He began to battle in Melbourne when he was 12
years old, put up a plucky fight, and made a place f< r
ids firm of Carney and Kelly. In conversation he had
a queer and oft-repeated mannerism : “D’ye understand
what I mean?” and this he tagged to the simplest of
questions. He had been under sentence of death for the
last rear, ever since his first apoplectic stroke ; but he re-
mained active and debonair to the day before his last.
He turned o\lt to Ricardo’s funeral, and looked big and
strong, but almost too large for his uniform. The four
hours’ march to Kew cemetery may have contributed
to the second stroke ; often there is nothing so fatal to
mourners as a funeral. A good man and a fine fellow was
Nigel William Kelly. Peace be \> it.h him !


Cream Separator.
During the last few years MILK SEPARATORS have been very extensively
used fey Dairy Farmers. The reason lies with the con-
siderable advantages derived from the use of this machine.
Flfi
m
'!
TT is now-a-days Simply Impossible to Profitably Work a dairy farm Without a Good
Milk Separator, and no farmer should be without a BALANCE.
The BALANCE separates, in the Shortest Space of Time, whilst the milk is still
warm, i.e., immediately after milking. Whereas, with the old pan method of standing,
the milk, which has first of all to be cooled and freed from air, and then stood for days
in large rooms with specially regulated temperature, and then left to separate by itself,
considerable time is lost, and the profit to the farmer reduced to a minimum.
It is claimed by the makers that, by using the BALANCE for separating- milk,
an average increase of 10 per pent, in the quantity of Butter may be expected, in com-
parison with that obtained by ordinary methods, and the increase may be even as much
as 15 or 20 per cent.
The scale of separation given below is understood to be for Milk Direct From The
Cow (as used for tests in the laboratories) and not for milk which has been transported.
The results become shialler the longer the milk has been standing, and the farther it
has been carried.

The WILD CAT
COUMN
The one cheerful feature about Westralian
finance is the size of the State’s sinking funds, for w hich
Australian Treasurer Bigjobn Forrest, its first Premier,
was responsible. For its loans, as a general thing, a 1 per
cent, sinking fund has been provided—substantial, but
not too much, considering that heaps of the borrowed
money has been spent on goldfields works, and the best
goldfield eventually becomes nothing more than a place
with a lot of valueless holes in it. When the Common-
wealth takes over the State debts, the Treasurer’s idea is
to provide for a sinking fund of £ per cent. ; and as
Westralia isn’t as “ flush ” as it was, and is strongly dis-
inclined to tax itself, and would be very glad, therefore,
if it could dodge paying J per cent, on its loans, it
would cordially approve of the transfer of the
debts on Treasurer Bigjohn’s terms, but for one thing—
it doesn’t want to be checked in its future borrowing
madness. Of course, the Westralian politician who knows
anything at all about the matter knows that the Com-
monwealth will have to take over the debt with the
sinking fund condition attached, and that, unless all the
lending Cohens agree, the sinking fund can’t any more
be cut down than the rate of interest can be ; but the
Westralian politicians, egged on by the Government
Statist, propose a little juggle—that the State should pay
only J per cent., and that the Commonwealth should
advance enough to make up the usual 1 per cent, pro-
vided for (in a few cases it is more). For last year it
would have worked out this way :
Provided by State .. .. .. £87,792
Advanced by Commonwealth ... 121,700
Total Sinking Fund .. .. £209,492
After the sinking funds Originally contracted for had
paid off the debt, the State would, of course, have to go
on paying back to the Commonwealth the money ad-
vanced. It is another way, in short, of borrowing over
one-half of the sinking fund ; and it is very like playing
the confidence trick with Mr. J. Bull Cohen.
The folly of any such proceeding would be less
the Commonwealth’s than Westralia’s. The State has
suffered already, and must go on suffering, through pro-
\ iding not for too heavy a sinking fund, but for too light
a one. It is the, no doubt just, boast of “ barrackers” for
Bewick, Moreing and Co. that of some 20 mines which
obey are controlling in Westralia, only two would be
running were it not for the reduction in costs which
they have effected. Dividends (paid nearly all to per-
sons outside the State) are in the aggregate pretty well
maintained, but the output of gold is slumping horribly.
The richest of the cream has already been skimmed ; and
now costs—the money spent mainly in the country—
have to be cut, and cut, and cut. While the cream is
being skimmed, and huge profits made, the sinking fund
should be very high—not 1 per cent., but in many cases
(as Bigjohn Forrest saw) 3 per cent. The men who run
the mines, when they spend money on works, provide
often for a contribution out of profits of as much as 10
per cent, for the return of capital; and the State’s works
are, in many cases, no more permanent. If, then, Wes-
tralia had demanded from the first that out of its riches a
substantial sinking fund should be provided, it would
have less to worry about now. Yet even the present
is richer by far than the future is likely to be.
This mere handful of mines cannot go on
paying over £2,000,000 a year in dividends. While
the present riches last, therefore, the 1 per cent,
sinking fund (or, in a few cases, more) should not be
monkeyed with. It is not too much now; it was too
little in the past. Even a 1 per cent, sinking fund in-
vested at per cent, will take nearly 44 years to wipe
out a debt, and it is a mighty good goldfield that will last
anything like 44 years worked as the modern engineer
works it, with shafts rushing down 200 ft. a year. The
less the present has to carry, the more the future will
have to bear—the future that will be left with the
skimmed milk while the present has still some cream to
feed upon. Even if Westralia presses its proposal, then,
the Commonwealth should not listen to it.

“W. Sandison” writes; “Re Goldsbrough,
Mort and Co., Ltd. Bulletin (a3/6/’O7), in its criticism of
this co., remarks upon the leniency of the debenture-
holders. As you say, there may be an explanation.
Three trustees, I know, were appointed, who were sup-
posed to represent the debenture-holders ; but, although
I was a creditor of this company, I had no voice in their
appointment, neither do I know when they were ap-
pointed, nor who appointed them. What I do know is
that one of these trustees was a large shareholder in
Goldsbrough’s. Another had been Goldsbrough’s agent
prior to the first reconstruction. They had been in
receipt of large remuneration from the funds of the
company. These gentlemen may nevertheless have
watched the debenture-holders’ interests; yet I, for
my own part, would not have chosen them for
such a work. I also note, after the latest
reconstruction, that large blocks of debenture-stock were
held in the name of one of these trustees and others. 1
also note that Goldsbrough's in 1902-3 bought a large
quantity of debentures, and it would be interesting to
know whom they bought them from. As regards the
latest reconstruction, had the facts been put before the
Court, it would never have sanctioned it. For years I
and others have endeavored to induce our Government to
appoint a commission to inquire into the working of
Dibbs’ Reconstruction Act, with the view of probing the
methods of some of these reconstructed companies, but
so far without success.”
“A Goldsbrough-Mort Creditor,” writing on
the same subject: “In the valuable summary of Golds-
brough, Mort and Co.’s reconstructions (B. 13/6/’O7), while
the fact is made clear enough that the shareholders were
relieved of a heavy liability, it is not, perhaps, sufficiently
stressed that the debenture-holders were made to suffer
severely by the 1901 reconstruction, 40 per cent, of the
B stock being then cancelled, representing, in round
numbers, £500,000. The whole transaction has been dis-
creditable, and must affect the opinion which is held of
us in England, where most of the debentures were located.
It practically amounted to some of the wealthiest men in
Australia getting rid of their own liability, and then
tearing up the company’s promise to pay.”

The claims during the year have been extraordinarily
heavy, and this has not been due to anything very ex-
cessive in the way of surrenders. Whether the mor-
tality experience has been less favorable than the expec-
tation will not, however, be ascertainable till the next
actuarial investigation and report. The largest increase
in outgoings has been under the heading of matured
endowments, and those are claims which have possibly
arisen through effluxion of time. The expenses have not
been small either, so the additions to funds have been
very poor. In fact, allowing for £34,330 which has been
written off the Investment Fluctuation Reserve during
the year, the funds have actually diminished, which is a
rare experience for an Australian office. The chances
are, however, that the £34,330 written off the Investment
Fluctuation Reserve had really disappeared before—not
very many investments depreciated last year—so that the
writing-off makes the office no poorer. The transactions
in connection with this reserve are shown hereunder :

As for the expenses, this shows 10 years’ ex-

But the position is really better than that, because a
good deal more new business is now being secured.
Assuming that the old or renewal business cost 7 per
cent, of premiums to manage (it probably cost a bit more
in an office of this size), then the new business last year
cost 173 per cent, of the new premiums received, whereas
five years ago the cost was 231 per cent., and 10 years ago
it was 230 per cent. But, despite the improvement
shown, the figures are still much too high. Reckoning
on this basis, from 130 to 150 per cent, of first year’s pre-
miums ought to be the limit of the cost of new business ;
and there are offices that have got it down below' 100 per
cent.
Unfortunately the office does not atone for too
heavy costs by high interest earnings :
Interest Earned on Average Funds.
1899 .. £3 19 4 per cent. 1903 .. £3 19 8 per cent.
1900 .. 31711 „ 1904 ..414
1901 .. 317 11 „ 1905 .. 317 11
1902 .. 319 4 „ 1906 .. 318 1
The official report deducts the Investment Fluctuation
Reserve from the funds, and shows the earnings as though
they were wholly derived from only the Insurance Fund ;
but there seems to be nothing whatever to be said in
favor of that system. It merely has the effect of inflating
the return. If the Investment Fluctuation Fund is an
actual reserve, then it is represented by assets, and those
assets are earning interest just as much as the assets re-
presented by the Insurance Fund. The return is dis-
tinctly poor. The trouble seems to lie in the dispropor-
tionate amount of freehold and leasehold property
held—no less than £786,068, or nearly 26 per cent,
of the entire assets, while “ Government and other
debentures ” represent less than 12 per cent. In
1893 the “freeholds and leaseholds” item represented
just over £500,000, so it looks from that, as well as from
the interest return, as though a good deal of the property
dates back to the days of boom values. Banks, finding
themselves in the same position, have been able to unob-
trusively lop off some of the boom value by applying the
surplus profits of extra-good half-years to the purpose ;
hut the insurance office can’t do much in that way—it
makes practically no profits (apart from sales of securi-
ties) that it can avoid showing. - And if it applies those
profits to writing-down purposes, the bonuses must
suffer. Still, if these reflections are not very cheerful, it
can at least be remembered that the recent course of
property values, in Australia at least, has been all in the
office’s favor; moreover, the progress made in securing
new business at smaller cost than hitherto ought to make
figures look a good deal better in the future, when this
new business will be old or renewal business, and won’t
cost much to manage.

The spring in the profits is remarkable. Ever since the
troubles in the early ’nineties this bank has kept its divi-
dend fairly low, and probably it has thus been able to get
its book values down to bedrock. In the present state of
Australasian prosperity sales of most properties are pos-
sible ; and it may be that this hank has realised some
securities well above the written-down values. Certainly
ordinary banking business has been most profitable
lately; but not so much more profitable during the past
six months as to account for all the improvement shown
above. Nor do the detailed figures of the balance-sheet
otherwise explain matters, though they do show a sub-
stantial business expansion:—
May, 1906. May, 1907.
Notes in circulation .. £60,365 £64,535 Inc., £4,170
Bills, etc. .. .. 14,435 10,855 Dec., 3,580
Deposits at interest .. 254,725 245,929 Dec., 8,796
Deposits, free .. .. 280,851 310,375 Inc., 29,524
Liquid assets .. .. 223,076. 174,525 Dec., 48,551
Advances .. .. 550,404 630,537 Inc., 80,133
Premises, etc 28,964 27,904 Dee., 1,060
Outstanding features here are the large proportion of
deposits not carrying interest, and the increase in ad-
vances. The bank is now working up to the full extent
of its visible resources. In spite of the large proportion
of call money, only 27 per cent, of its liabilities to the
public are represented by liquid assets. Many bankers
w'ould not venture that far in ordinary times. True, the
public is very well protected on account of the large
capital in proportion to debt; for £1 of the shareholders’
money in the business, there is only £3 ss. of the public’s,
as against between £7 and £8 in the case of some of the
big Australian banks. It is on this account that the
profits have been comparatively small in spite of the very
large proportion of the whole resources that are put to
use. On the basis of capital (£152,040) and reserves,
recent profits have been as follows :
Nov., 1903 .. £5 2 8 p.c. Nov., 1905 .. £6 4 1 p.c.
May, 1904 .. 514 2 „ May, 1906 .. 6 2 3 „
Nov., 1904.. 518 1 ~ Nov., 1906.. 614 2 „
May, 1905.. 514 5 „ May, 1907 .. 913 5 „
The bank has its head office in Launceston, and the bulk
of its business comes from the north and from the mining
districts on the West Goast; and the activity in the latter
parts, due to the phenomenal prices of metals, has no
doubt also had its effect on profits. Unfortunately, it is
not an effect that is at all likely to be permanent, in the
present degree, at any rate.

There is always some doubt about this society’s figures,
because repairs and additions are apparently charged both
to contingent reserve and to profit and loss account
direct. In the foregoing, the amount by which the con-
tingent reserve has been reduced has been added to the
amount charged to profit and loss account for repairs and
additions, and the lump has been treated as written
off. The recent history of the society is further traced
in the following:—
1900. 1905. 1906. 1907.
Deposits .. ..£10,153 £14,455 £14,598 £22,382
Overdraft .. .. 3,054 25
Due on mortgage .. 4,467 8,204 8,204 8,204
Cash balance .. .. 1,737 6,069
Advances .. .. 35,305 38,649 40,813 42,665
Interest earnings .. 1,530 2,991 3,017 3,566
The capital is £16,825 ; so the deposits now considerably
exceed the capital. Advances have not, how ever, kept
pace w'ith the growth of deposits, so that £5OOO of the
borrowed money is itself on fixed deposit; and there
isn’t much in that. Like several others among the build-
ing societies, the Argus can command more deposits
than it can find immediate customers for ; and that,
and the steady increase of borrow’ers, seem to
point to a revival of building-society business.
For many years after “ the boom ” practically no building
was done in suburban Melbourne ; now, though the jerry
speculator hasn’t yet turned up to any extent worth men-
tioning, there is again a fair amount of the other sort of
building in progress, and this other sort is the only sort
w'ith which the building society has any right to interest
itself. Probably the average Melbourne society has
learned that lesson by heart; if it has, it can look forward
to a better time than it has had for the past 14 years.

The Zinc Corporation, Ltd., formed in 1905
to make millions out of the zinc in the Broken Hill tail-
ings and ore: —

Feb., 1906 .. £240,000 .. £11,631

The liabilities at the end of February were as follows :
Block 10 Co. (for purchased tailings), £111,429 ; sundries,
£23,506 ; net overdraft, £9011; interest, £823. In addi-
tion there are large contingent liabilities in connection
with contracts made with Block 14, the British, and South
cos. ; if these contracts are not carried out, the Zinc Cor-
poration forfeits £22,500 plus interest, which amount has
been put up, and is included at present as an asset. Of
the capital so far raised, and apart from the £22,500 just
mentioned, £334,712 is represented by “ tailings dumps
and purchase consideration account.” “ Purchase con-
sideration ” is represented on the other side by 80,000 £1
shares “ paid up otherwise than in money”—apparently
the little bit of fat which the men who strike these happy
ideas for making millions reserve for themselves without
any base cash consideration. In construction and equip-
ment £78,570 has already gone, railways and rolling
stock have cost £8687, and £27,910 covers machinery and
stores in hand and in transit. Nobody can accuse
the co. of wasting time. It had the money
or the credit, it believed the metal w r as
there, the price of zinc was extra-good, and it
sailed in, at first rather triumphantly, later on with
much more diffidence. And nobody can . ccuse the
promoters either of not, at the outset, believing thoroughly
in their scheme. The angry people are those who, mis-
led by the early enthusiasm, rushed the shares up to a
large premium. At the end of February of this year the
shares were 375. They are now quoted from London at
12s. 6d. buyer, and it is still only June.
Unfortunately, t he co. is not yet out of its troubles,
by a long way. It was established on a basis which
almost compelled it to look for big profits ; so it turned
aside almost contemptuously from the processes w'hich
for years past had been bringing in a little profit to the
Sulphide Corporation, Block 10, and, to a smaller extent,
other Broken Hill cos. Its promoters had, no doubt,
been misled by tall, loose talk on the part of some B.H.
metallurgists visiting London. Those visitors deserv e much
in tie way of forgiveness ; for at a time when there w as a
chorus of’ Stinking Pish they apparently tried to counter-
balance things by ~ .ooping joyfully of the little things
they knew about. And apparently the man on the other
side, used to the Australian who said the worst things of
his country, mistook these for the same sort. Hence the
trouble wliich they subsequently struckin vast quantities
where they had expected nothing but smooth sailing.
Now', having learned something (at a cost of, perhaps,
£75,000), the co. goes to work in a much more sober
frame of mind, and there are plenty of reasons why it
should succeed in a measure. At least three cos. have made
profits out of “ zincs,” and the Sulphide Corporation is
at this moment making probably from 12s. 6d. to 15s. per
ton, or, if it isn’t now, it know's pretty well that it will
shortly be able to. The Zinc Corporation will, of course,
have to pay for the tailings (about ss. per ton) out of what
it makes ; but even then the margin is good so long as
metal prices keep good.
* * » •»
The future of the spelter (zinc) market is the
same large mystery as the future of the other metal
markets. At present the price is about £26 per ton.
That is very much above the average of the past
25 years. Previous to 1905 the year’s average had in only
one year of the 25 gone beyond £24 ; and in 13 years it
was below £lB. In 1895 it was £l4 12s. 2d. Still, for the
past 10 years the spelter market has been firm, and the
average for a year has never gone below £l7. With the
generally higher level of prices now prevailing (due to
the bigger gold output, or something else), nothing very
shocking in the way of a slump need be expected, unless
the metallurgists make some very startling discovery.
And if they do, then, if the Zinc Corporation has the use
of the discovery, a fall in price won’t matter so very
much.

* * * *
Principal dividends paid by Westralian gold-
mining cos. to 31st May last: —Boulder Prop., £2,447,425 ;
Horseshoe, £2,355,000; Oroya-Brown Hill, £1,898,741 ;
Ivanhoe, £1,768,750 ; Fingall, £1,475,000; Perseverance,
£1,251,250; Kalgurli, £495,000; Associated Northern.
£490,000 ; Sons of Gwalia, £425,675.
South Kalgurli goes from bad to worse in depth.
At 1200 ft. thereare a few small, payablepatehes sandwiched
in between big blanks, but at 1500 ft. it looks as if there were
going to be one long, continuous blank and no patches.
Hope Nicholson is having a hard struggle to maintain a
7dwt. extraction ; and unless the West (Morty’s) lode,
which is opening up fairly’ well above 840 ft., comes to the
rescue, he will have to “ sky the towel ” bef re long. The
treatment plant has been laid up for repairs, so there will
be about 1400 tons less treated this month, and 700 tons
less in July.
* * * *
Resources of Oroya-Brown Hill and Associated
Northern (W.A.) are being conserved for all they are
worth, on account of the rapid depletion of the rich pipe
of ore, and a commendable desire to make it go as far as
possible. Oroya-Browm Hill should produce £30,000
worth of gold monthly for the next three years—equal to
30s. per share in dividends; after that it may have to take
a back seat among the big producers. The Associated
Northern is at present making a profit of about £10,500
on an 80s. extraction ; but a drop to 605., or thereabouts,
is inevitable before the year closes. The mine should run
for two years on this latter grade, and then it will gra-
dually snuff out. Both cos. have large cash reserves,
while in addition Oroya-Brown Hill has a valuable asset
in 70,000 Oroya-Black Range shares.
* * * *
First clean-up from the new mill on the Ivanhoe
Junction, Kalgoorlie(W.A.), turns out the expected fiasco.
As usual, the plant is blamed; in this case the gold isalleg d
to have disappeared “down a crack in the battery.”
Another yarn is that the cyanide vats leaked and allowed
the gold solution to run to waste. The fact is, the mine
hasn’t any payable ore, and the plant—an eccentric ami
decrepit-looking thing—was not warranted, and should
never have been erected. Over £50,000 has been frittered
away on this wild cat. It has produced no payable gold,
and it is unlikely that it ever will.
* * * * .
Great Fingall (W.A.) shows an improvement
at 1000 ft. from shaft in the No. 13 level, north end, where,
for 73ft., the ore has averaged about £2 per ton. It may
be only a “ flash in the pan,” but it looks promising.
Murchison Associated, Ltd. (W. A.), is try ng to
engineer another reconstruction, making the filth or
thereabouts. The present scheme is to raise £27,500 by
a shilling assessment, and keep 50,000 shares (£12,500) in
reserve. The co. has been kept alive for years past by
reconstructions, the circulation of taradiddles about its
prospects, and the occasional publication of line drawings
representing the property to be a next-door-but-oue
neighbor to the Great Fingall, and to have the continua-
tion of its shoot. As the two mines are over a mile apart,
and as the Murchison Associated has less chance of
getting the Fingall reef than its bottom level has of being
struck by lightning, the thing has been scandalous. The
co. has its office in London.
* * * *
On 31st March last, Golden Ridge (W.A.) was
estimated to have enough ore in sight to keep 20 head of
stamps running at least 12 months. Reported since that
large additions have been made to reserves. The direc-
tors anticipate that an eighteen-penny dividend will be
declared in August, and hope for quarterly’ distributions
of the same amount. As no mention is made of building up
a reserve fund, the inference is that the Ridge authorities
intend to boost shares by dividing profits as soon as they'are
earned. The Golden Pole people did this ; but the here-
after came sooner than was expected, and brought with
it a big slump in shares and the ruin of persons who were
confiding enough to believe the gaudy yarns spun to
them, those who are tempted to buy Ridges at the pre-
sent price should remember Poles. Also they might
profitably reflect that the mine stands by itself in an
unproved locality, and has only been partly prospected
to a vertical depth of 300 ft. If values gave out at the
next level (and, unfortunately, quartz reefs have a knack
of becoming unpayable at shallow depths in the West)
the Ridge will fizzle. For the present, however, every-
thing in the garden’s lovely; and shareholders aston-
ished the newspaper reporters at the recent half-yearly
meeting by clinking glasses, instead of squabbling and
inviting each other to “ come outside ” as of yore.
* * * *
The Phillips River copper district (W.A.) is
doing little more than mark time until the railway from
Hopetoun Harbor comes along, when it is locally ex-
pacted that 3 per cent, ore will show a profit, though
that, of course, depends on the price of copper. The
best way to develop the field would be to form properties
into groups and work them on a large scale, as Kaufman
is doing. Leaseholders, however, do not seem to think
so, to sit down and ask prohibitive prices for
individual surface shows. And buyers are not rushing
small, isolated surface shows.
•x * * *
Meekatharra (the only goldfield in W.A. where
working prospectors have bought and erected mills out
of profits) is agitating for 23 miles of railway to connect
it with Nannine. Hitherto, stiff cartage rates for timber
and fuel have been the camel blocking Meekatharra’s
track to glory, and railway facilities would, so to speak,
get rid of the camel. Big bodies of schist streaked with
rich quartz leaders occur in this district, and small for-
tunes have been made working these leaders and leaving
the schist alone. Meekatharra has not yet been proved
in depth, though ; and, judging by the character of
its formations, it is doubtful whether it will successfully
pass this test. , * #
Dummying is said to have, become a fine art at
Greenbushes tinfield (W.A.). Alleged that most of the
land is held without registration, and the dodges for
shepherding areas are said to well deserve a chapter in an
encyclopaidia of bluff and imposition. Apart from that
question, A. E. Morgans, of Perth, and Frank Moss, of
the Kalgurli, have acquired most of the good things on
this field ; and if values hold down, and are equal to ex-
pectations, big fortunes will be made by these specu-
lators.

The management estimates that there ure 30,000 tons of
navable ore in sight; also that when a cyanide plant is
erected, in three months a profit of between £lOOO and
£l5OO will be earned monthly. Meantime ore treatment

“ At an extraordinary meeting of the Zinc Corporation,
a resolution to alter the articles of association so as to
place the head office in London, was unanimously carried.
It was stated that, although the nominal management
was in Melbourne, the policy and direction came from
London, where most of the shareholders now were.”
Now speaks sorrow !
All the shares —
And to-morrow
All the bears
Can beg or borrow —
Go to Bull!
He is full !
Full of Zincs 1
Full of Zincs !
That’s why he drinks ;
Hence his sorrow'.
Bought ’em up to fifty bob ;
(Now’, if you’re inclined to rob,
You may net eleven and six).
Sad to-morrow !
Fifty bob to nearly nix—
Zincs !
Stinks!
Now the office goes to Bull ;
He is full—
Full of Zincs—
Though the awful facts he blinks,
Taking in despair to drinks ;
Hear Bull’s boodle as it clinks,
While Bull sadly pays for Zincs,
Sellers playing lofty jinks,
Each his profit loudly chinks,
Made of Zincs—
Name that stinks
Worse than many kitchen sinks,
Bad as minks 1
Zincs!!
Bull’s full!
(Crowded Bull).
These are nice Imperial links,
Binding us to London town —
Zincs are down !
Holder’s brains are full of kinks—
Holder’s outlook black as inks ;
Seller loudly daily winks.
As he walks ’mid roses, pinks,
And the Heidsieck gaily drinks,
Bought with Zincs I
Fifty bob to ’leven and six !
Fifty bob to nearly nix !
Hi! Drinks!
Zincs !!

Marvel Loch, Southern Cross (W.A.), has fur-
