* * * *
From an uninterested man, who was asked for
his opinion : “ The flat underlay of the reef in the Oroya-
Black Range (W.A.) is not likely to be of much benefit to
the Sandstone Development Co., which holds a big area
of ground adjoining. One of its leases —the Juno—must
get a bit of the reef if i t holds down ; but, as the shoots
of gold dip south, values will soon pass once more into
Oroya-Black Range ground.”
* * * *
The May returns from the Junction North,
Broken Hill, don’t show the improvement forecasted by
the stock’s lusty barrackers. The ore treated was 3926
tons, and the approximate profit was £2865, less office
expenses, depreciation, etc.; say, £2250 net, which is an
outside figure. That means 11s. 6d. per ton. Reserves
are estimated at 300,000 tons ; at 11s. 6d. per ton, that is
£172,500, or 19s. 2d. per share (there are 180,000 in the
co.). No doubt, more ore may be opened up ; possibly,
also, the extraction may improve; and something may
also be allowed for the zinc-bearing tailings. But a good
set-off for all that is the certainty (as far as anything can
be called certain) of a fall in the price of metals. In the
foregoing, lead was taken at £l9 17s. 6d,, the month’s
average, and silver (fine) at 2s. BJd. Whether a mine,
which on this basis has 19s. 2d. per share in sight, can be
fairly reckoned worth 355. is purely a ma, ter of opinion.
The man who says it is, must be a person who takes a
large size in risks.
* * * *
Broken Hill South Blocks are sharing the de-
mand for Broken Hill Souths, and that demand, which
comes mostly from London, is apparently due to the
phenomenal firming of the lead market to £22. Buying
orders at £7 18s. were received in Australia early this
week, and that means a shade over £8 delivered at the
other side, which brings this stock close up to
the top reached during the November boom (£8 155.).
At the end of 1904 they stood at £2 7s. 6d. for paids, and
40s. for the others. Certainly for this mine the 1904 con-
ditions can’t altogether return again, because the ore
developments since then have been remarkably good.
That, however, with a mine like the South, whose ore
reserves have never been in doubt, is of much less conse-
sequence than the price of metals ; and in that respect it
is quite likely that the 1904 experience will be more or
less repeated. And the 1904 lowest price for lead was
£ll, or just half this latest quotation. South Blocks
made a net profit in May of £4471, which works out
at 13s. 9d. per ton of ore. There was approximately
782 tons of lead in the concentrates, so the net profit
was at the rate of £5 14s. per ton of lead. In
other words, South Blocks’ profit would disappear if
lead fell £5 14s. below the May average, which was about
£2O. Thus, on present figures, profit would vanish if
lead fell below £l4 6s. ; and, except in 1900, there wasn’t
one year between 1885, when Broken Hill started, and
1906 when lead didn’t fall below £l4 6s. South Blocks
must therefore do better work before its future can be
reckoned as assured.
* * * *
Yambulla, on the N.S.W. South Coast, lately
had the usual visions of Foreign Capital coming in with a
benevolent smile and a free hand to “ open up the dis-
trict,” an admittedly neglected one. The report was that
a Melbourne co. had been as good as formed. But some-
thing seems to have gong wrong with the works. For
some years a small co. worked the old Yambulla mine,
and took out about4ooo tons for Bdwt. per ton, all from
shallow levels ; then after getting down to 180 ft., the co.
abandoned the business, and Mjradian and Pola took it
up. They opened out at 180 ft., and from a reef running
up to as much as 12ft. wide they have got out 2000 tons
for about 2100 oz. It was generally understood that
“ Melbourne capital ” was going to give £7OOO for an in-
terest, and put up enough to provide new machinery,
etc.; but after an inspection of the mine had been made,
“ Melbourne capital ” turned out to be less generous than
had been supposed, arid so work is going on in the old
way, with, according to some estimates, 18 months’ stone
reasonably proved.
* * * *
New Hillgrove Prop. (N.S.W.), which has not
received its electrical plant as soon as it had figured on,
must have a lot of financial leeway to make up, on account
of its heavy capital expenditure. Shareholders and
dabblers, therefore, want to see a statement of liabilities.
Before the shaft collapsed things looked strained. A
shilling dividend cost the co. £lO,OOO, and the market
value of the mine was ten times that much. Yet all
there was to pound out profits was a 10-head mill—-a good
enough little plant, but 10 heads take a long time to make
£lO,OOO. The co., therefore, did wisely to get more
battery power and another mine the Eleanora—
though the latter’s previous history was poor enough. The
New Hillgrove Prop, itself has had some very rich
stone; but it was in short shoots, so the upper levels
were quickly worked out, except for some rich stone
near the shaft, where it was unsafe to work. The deeper-
level shoots should be longer ; and as Baker’s Creek,
next door, is prospecting the deep-levels to advantage,
things should go fairly well with the New Hillgrove, in
spite of the dead-weight of capital expenditure. The
battery is of Bendigo make.
* * • «
The New’ Hillgrove accounts for the co.’s year,
which ended on December 31st last, arrived by mail this
week; but nearly six months, have happened since
December 31st, and the co. has tfad its hand in its pocket
(or in its banker’s pocket) all the time. When the books
closed, cash and liquid assets were under £lO,OOO in
excess of outstanding bills; and all that went long
ago in shaft-sinking, plant, and the new electrical
installation. Last year showed a loss of £7059 ; that was
exclusive of capital expenditure on shaft-sinking, but in-
cluded £9019 written off for depreciation of plant and of
mine work previously charged to capital. Management
expenses were, as usual, heavy; actual mining cost
£13,804 (exclusive of shaft work), while management ex-
penses in Hillgrove and Sydney ran to £4320, and in Lon-
don to £l9Bo—and what was done for the latter amount
Heaven only knows, seeing that the consulting engineers’
fees are charged to the Hillgrove and Sydney account.
* * * a
Homeward Bound, Wyalong (N.S.W.), is
struggling with the hard country by introducing air
compressors to work machine drills. The chief trouble is
the smallness of area ; it would be more profitable to
amalgamate with the two adjoining claims and sink one
good serviceable shaft. The three properties among
them only take in about 1000 ft. along the reef.
* • * *
Captain Richard, of Mt. Morgan, w r ho was
rushed off to America as soon as the Electrolytic Refin-
ing Co. was fixed up, will be back again this month. As
a matter of fact, though the co. was only completed in
February, it had been on the stocks for months—almost
ever since F. H. Snow made the contract with his Ger-
man-American principals, to handle the Morgan output
—so many things were already in train. The engineer
and manager of the new co. will be Benjamin Magnus,
who formerly ran the Calumet and Hecla refinery at
Buffalo ; and the works will most likely go up at Kembla
(N.S.W.). This will be some consolation to Kembla for
the painfully slow progress made by that other smelting
co. which, over 12 months ago, was going to rise out of
the ashes of the Dapto works. In fact, that co.’s progress
is so slow that you can only measure it with good instru-
ments.
* * * *
Manager Coakes, who is leaving the Catherine
Reef United, Bendigo, after half-a-century’s service, was
a bit of a terror to journalists. Not that he was unkindly
or harsh, but none quite knew how he would take the
way a bit of intelligence was put. His last clean-up at
the old Catherine was tip-top—23o£oz. from 31.0 tons.
After all, the mine hasn’t done badly out of the present
shoot even as far as it has gone, for a big lump has been
paid in dividends, and a big capital expenditure wiped
out. The new manager is a young Bendieonian, with a
tolerable amount of self-confidence. This, plus the mine,
ought to put him up a rung or two on the ladder.
* * * *
With the death of cricketer “ Bob ” M'Leod,
the modern spirit in the directorate of the Long Tunnel
Extended disappears. When he went on to the Board,
matters were very blue with the old co., and shares were
at a figure not seen since the call days, 30 years back.
“Bob” owned to the last that he knew more about the
pitch of a cricket field than the pitch of the shoot
of gold in the Extended mine. Still he knew what
was discipline, and the mine profited by that knowledge.
He never had any prejudices about the underlie of the
reef being against the mine, but had the sense to pick
out among the staff men who could put facts straight to
him. And, above all, he did not mind talking straight on
the many occasions that straight talk was needed in the
Extended’s Board room. An odd coincidence w'as that
with his advent into VValhalla, the reef in the Extended
lost some of its underlie, thus giving the mine a longer
life. Now, at the time of his death, comes the news that
the reef is making west again more rapidly than it did
for hundreds of feet overhead.
* * » *
Cassilis (Vic.) has at last got its electric scheme
planned. Not before it was want ed either. Idea is to put a
power station on a fall near the junction of the Oobungra
and Victoria Rivers, in Gippsland, and convey the elec-
tricity to the mine, to be used for hauling and every other
purpose possible, even to browning the manager’s toast.
The co. is the sole survivor of the many that have tackled
the mineral lodes of that part of Vic. Thanks to the
average run of the ore being an ounce, it has only called
on shareholders to put up about £6OOO, on which a return
of about £14,000 has been made in dividends. That looks
well; but it doesn’t look as well when it is known that
the gold won is of the total value of £250,000. A big
portion of this has gone in plant, for almost every new
man who has come has made a change in the designs of
his predecessor. Had the shareholders been at all a
determined lot, they would have diverted a good deal of
this expenditure into their own pockets, for they would
have put up capital long since to provide proper equip-
ment, instead of grubbing along midway between a call,
a bank overdraft, and a dividend. The electric scheme
will help, for there is no decent fuel in the locality. No
doubt it will be carried out, though the fund built'up to
pay for it out of profits is already coveted by some of the
shareholders.

Birthday New Find, Berringa (Vic.), doesn’t
pretend on its native heath to be much more respectable
than a Wild Oat; but the public is fed with cheerful
reports, so calls are got in pretty regularly from the out-
siders. A lengthy term of shallow work has not dis-

recently added to the plant, and it will probably rust
out before there is enough stone at grass to keep it
running full time. A while ago, a trial crushing of a few
hundredweight of stone was put through, to encourage
the holders of shares, now called up to 6s. 2d., and
marketable outside the home district at about 3s
• * •
The7d. a yard wash which the dredge-man is
nowadays pursuing, recalls the record of the Allied
Armies. That co. worked the ground now shaded by
Ballarat’s most decorative villas. It only had three
acres, but in three years those three acres gave £lOl 350
worth of gold. The wash was 30ft. deep.
* * * •
J. Bull will put up a vast sum for the purchase
of a mine, but after that he is a poor call-payer. Eighteen
months ago the Victorian Star Gold Mines, Ltd., was
floated in London in 150,000 shares of £l, and nearly all
the working capital went in buying pumping and winding
plant. The falling off in the yields of the Victoria
Cornish has presumably caused John to shy off further
Victorian Star calls, and consequently the mine has
temporarily closed down. In the old days of Daylesford,
the Star, on Specimen Hill, was a mighty gold producer. ’
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers, at a ver-
tical depth of 2650 ft., has cut 6ft. of mineral stone. This
may mollify some of the discontented shareholders, who
have been criticising directors and management. Total
cost of handling stone runs to 38s. 9d. per ton ; 22,725
tons were milled for £21,317, and £14,941 came in from
concentrates—an average yield of about 325. It has
become a habit with some managers to lump the cyanide
return with the mill recovery; but that kind of thingisboth
objectionable and misleading, for often a rich lot of old
tailings happens to be included in the total. The Bril-
liant Extended gold from cyanide works was £4538, which
is equal to about 4s. a ton on the ore milled ; so even
reckoning cyanide returns in, the yield did not pay the
total costs. The co. owes £5327 to its bank, and £2400
on its new compressor, and the debt isn’t likely to get
any smaller very soon. Some 60,000 tons are estimated
as reserves in sight ; but with a reef containing patches
of white stone that have to be left, the actual reserves of
profitable ore are a doubtful quantity. The co. has a
large area (75 acres), and good stone at 2650 ft. is a favor-
able sign ; and the sick share market has brightened up
in consequence. There is certainly as much reason to be
hopeful about the Brilliant reef as about most things on
an old goldfield, and its record is one of the best.
0.K., the North Queensland copper mine which
was put oh too high a pedestal and then was trampled on
after it had been knocked off, made a profit last half-year
of £13,491, according to profit and loss account. ■ As a
matter of fact, though, profit and loss figures are not
worth much in such a case. On the one side, owing to
the severe wet season, the furnace was in blast for only 99
days, yet for the balance of the time many of the'ex-
penses ran on. On the other side, large" sums spent
during the half-year in development work have been
charged not against revenue but against capital; and
there was also heavy capital expenditure in other
ways. At September 30, 1906, the bank balances
and matte and copper in hand, after paying outstanding
debts, were £14,802. In dividends, £16,872 was paid
during the half-year ; and the haif-year ended with over-
draft and other liabilities £3735 in excess of the value of
copper, etc., on hand. In the two months that have
passed since then smelting has been continuous ; and
working on 10 per cent, ore the look of things soon
changed. At March 31 the co. owed the bank £14,253 ;
at the end of May, chairman John Newell said the co.’s
credit balance had already got up to over £lO,OOO. So
far, the mine is still handling ore averaging over 10 per
cent. ; and that can be kept up for the rest of the
year at least. After that, on present prospects, the
figure will gradually taper off to about 6 or 7 per cent.,
and wdth the appliances now' available there will be good
profit in that. It has been suggested that, as recent
developments have shown mostly about 7 per cent, ore,
the average of the ore treated should be reduced as soon
as possible, so that there may be something like uni-
formity. The directors’ idea, however, seems to be that
the more copper that can be turned out in the present
market the better ; and, anyhow, very little of the lower-
grade ore can be conveniently worked at present. Divi-
dends will be resumed probably in August.

Copper and tin shows that John Moffat is con-
nected with in N.Q. have mostly built up solid bank
balances ; so, in spite of disappointments respecting mine
development, Mt. Molloy pays its 17th div. of 6d. without
worrying about the funds ; and Dalziel’s, as forecasted
has also blossomed into a long-expected “sixpenny’’’
About 400 tons, which look like 5 per cent, tin oxide
(say, between £4 and £5 a ton), are now going through the
mill from this mine. The developments have been poor •
but the open cut turns out fair ore at a low cost’
The show is well managed; yet tin is so erratic and
pitchy a mineral that it would be sound policy to
acquire more ground. It is unpalatable to find that the
tin doesn’t go down; but the district is prolific in tin
shows, and a big co. should not confine itself to a small
area.

Chillagoe(Q.) smelters are now treating over 3000
tons of lead and copper ore per month, and this accounts
for a g-ood deal of the improved railway returns, for nearly
two-thirds of the material handled is outside ore. Total
production for Mayr was 149 tons copper, 216 tons lead,
and 30,7660 z. silver ; but it is well to note that 3387 tons
of ore were smelted, so the average return, although
metals are at top prices, was only about £'6 per ton of ore.
Chillagoe’s own ore does not come up to that average-
-1176 tons copper ore yielded 47 tons metal, or 4 per cent.,
besides 6|oz. silver per ton, Mungana sent 151 tons for
14 tons copper—a small return for such a big co. But,
although this mine is classed among copper shows, it
sends out more silver and lead ; for the month, 690 tons
of ore returned 09 tons lead (10 per cent.), with nearly
lOoz. silver. Chillagoe thus depends on outside shows,
including Mungana, to keep its furnaces going ; so a man
who invests in shares or debentures is practically backing
the resources of the district; wherefore, as before pointed
out, the fact that the smelting return even on present
metal prices is only £6 per ton of ore is worth noting.
Out of that, mining, freight, and smelting costs have to
be paid.
* * • *
“Mainshaft”: Re statement (B. 6/6/’O7) that
there are doubts about the permanence of the Charleston
mines (N.Q.). Three Charleston properties, owned by
John Candlish—the Queenslander, Havelock, and Nil
Desperandum—have a mill record of 35,000 tons for
49,0000 z., with from 50s. to 735. gold in concentrates and
tails. All of these shows have strong reefs in the sink,
and are still producing ; and one of them has proved a 3ft.
shoot of ounce stone for 1200 ft. Further, the Dry Hash
has a silver-lead lode, whose last exported parcel returned
60 per cent lead and 15dwt. gold. If Charleston isn’t
good enough to build a railway to, the rest of the Ether-
edge field can be counted out as well.
* * * *
“Redoubt”: “ Mainshaft’s” notes on the
Cloncurry position in the market (B. 6/6/’O7) are good, in
so far as they relate to the present position of the prin-
cipal mines; but the principal mines are very few in
number so far. Further, the present position of the
mines is not what concerns the investor. He looks at the
future, and that’s a bit hazy. But the present position
does concern the speculator, though, and Australia at
this moment is a pretty sick kind of speculator, and
“Mainshaft” unconsciously advances the reason. He
says from “November last to the end of February, Mr. Bull
fed greedily on Cloncurry copper, and his table manners
were beastly, and then New York gave him an emetic.”
Not so. Bull had his feed of Cloncurry copper any-
thing from seven to 30 years ago, and from the incep-
tion of the present metal boom he has been feeding his
calves, of which Australia has been the most avaricious.
Bull is not eating ;he is getting rid of his load. Note
what “Mainshaft” says The market strengthens on
Australian buying one day ; in comes Mr. J. Bull, a
strong seller, and knocks the market out.” Just so.
Bull is still feeding his calves. He made the market
originally, and has been selling the stock from the high
prices down to the present low or normal ones. John
Bull received no emetic from New York. The New York
position right through the chapter, panic and all given
in, has been a strong one. Even to-day it is New York
which is keeping up the price of standard copper—the
speculative copper. New York is coming in daily to buy.
It buys standard copper in London at a price payably
below its own electrolytic, and takes it to America and
converts to electrolytic. New York has been keeping
up the standard-copper price by buying, and England
sells its standard copper as it has been selling its Clon-
curry shares. Australia has got the Cloncurry shares at
high prices (and it can have more), and America has got
the standard copper. Not until England comes in and
buys back some copper co. stocks (notably Cloncurry) will
Australia look otherwise than sorrowful. England has
got our cash. And serve us right.
• * • •
“ Quartpot ” : Re Argylla, of Cloncurry
(B. -23/5/’O7). True, the outcrop is large, but it is what is
locally known as “ white oxide ” (quartz). There are two
small, short seams of rich ore running diagonally into
this outcrop, on which two 6x4 prospecting shafts are
being sunk, and at time of writing they are not looking
too favorable. Previous to recent visit of }4r. Hill, senior
partner of Messrs. Alex. Hill and Stewart, the co.’s con-
sulting engineers in London, the co.’s late and sanguine
general manager had let a contract to sink a main shaft.
But Mr. Hill stopped that, and substituted the two shafts
mentioned. Very doubtful if the two shoots of ore go
down ; and this is characteristic of most of the ore occur-
ring in quartz on the field, and the chances of the mine
being the “boss” of the field are very remote. Of
all the Queensland Freehold’s properties, undoubtedly
the Just-in-Time, orMalbon, is the most promising. Two
prospecting shafts are on ore 4ft. wide, of glance and
carbonates, and there are some 80 tons of 30 per cent,
ore at grass for about a month’s work. The Argylla has",
perhaps, 10 tons to show for very nearly the same amount
of toil.
* * » *
“Quartpot,” from Cloncurry: “Noteworthy
that, from this much-boomed field, only about one-
quarter the copper is going away now that went over 12
months back, though the price then was some £7O per
ton, as against £lOO now. The rich surface patches are
becoming scarcer and more difficult to find, and the
gouger’s lot is not such a happy one as of yore. Carriers
are scouring the field to get back-loading, in shape of
copper ore, which, however, in spite of reduced rates, is
unobtainable. The absence of water, too, is telling
against the gouger. The late wet season was a light one,
and, in consequence, long, dry stages have to be tackled.”
* * * *
Shades of Lonergan and .J. P. Madden ! The
sails are to be bent once again on that apparent derelict
the Mt. Lyell Comstock, and an attempt made to get the’
craft out of the region of perpetual calm into which it
was supposed to have drifted. Hill and Stewart, mining-
engineers, of London, are now the metallurgists that are
to solve the problem that has baffled many a good man,
as well as many a duffer—how to treat profitably the
low-grade copper-bearing schists of the Mt. Lyell region.
Moore, at the South Tharsis ; Trent and his subordinates'
at the North Lyell ; and the Mt. Lyell staff, with
the wet magnetic separator, have all passed by with
bowed heads. Hill arid Stewart’s idea is to concentrate.
The ideal concentration would be that of a smelter if
pyritic ore w'ere available, or if the Comstock ore were
sufficiently high in copper to be a workable proposition.
Sticht, of Mount Lyell, has so far gobbled up in the end all
the ore that has been won by previous experimenters at
heavy cost. This, too,in all probability will be theendof this
latest effort in experimenting. Hill and Stewart admit
that water concentration is of no avail. The process to
be relied on appears to be the Cattermole, owned by the
Minerals Separation, Ltd., which several of the Broken
Hill mines are adopting. It is an emulsion process
minus the Elmore vacuum. Ore sent from the Lyell
Comstock, and treated by it, ranged from 2‘3 per cent,
copper to 3'7 per cent., and a recovery of between 70 and
80 per cent, was made. This was laboratory testing. On
this basis, it is W'orked out that the ore gives a product
valued at £2 10s. 9d. per ton. The outlay is put, at £1 5-
3£d. per ton, leaving a profit of £1 ss. s£d. But tli,’.-e
figures are on the dangerous basis of copper at £lOO per
ton. As it is said that there are 150,000 tons of 4 per-
cent. ore available, Bull is to put up £54,000 to give the
new process a trial. This ought to be ample ; but Bull
will be wise if he goes very slow at first, so as to find out
whether working tests overcome those difficulties of
recovery which made Sticht say that 7 per cent, copper
was needed to earn profits from the Lyell ores.

m .. ” : The ferry service between Port
idaint! r f n rom n^ Wlllia m- StOW S, (Vic ’ ) is causin F many com-
plaints from the public. For several years past and
until just recently, two boats, the Gem and the Queen
naintained a half-hourly service between the two places’
bonrnp h connecting with the trams at the Port Mel-’
r!nwav Pier ’ ftnd J he Queen with the trains at the
1 P . A nionth or two ago the Railway- Depart
ment called for tenders for an improved service connect'
ing with the trains, and offered a substantial subsidy.
The subsidy was smaller than that given in previous
years, so the boat-owners interested decided not to
tender. The Department then raised the figure, and
called for fresh tenders. An outside syndicate proposed
bringing a boat over from Launceston and securing the
contract. Simultaneously with the announcement that
the Kail way Commissioners were giving favorable con-
sideration to the Launceston boat, the news was pub-
lished in the Melbourne dailies that the owners of the
Queen, Gem, Baldrock, and Motor Gem had amalgamate
and formed a concern to be called the Victorian
.terry Co. The new company promised a ferry
service that would not be surpassed in Sydney
Harbor or Hobart. “ Come fair, come foul,” their
boats would be found running according to time-table,
ihis sounded so nice that the Railways (it once accepted
their tender, and gave the Launceston-boat people the
go-by. How the trouble has commenced. Out of the
four boats that the Victorian Ferry Co. has under its con-
trol, three are laid up, and the Gem is running* on its own
doing only one trip an hour. The co. considers the con-
venience of the public to such an extent that the hitherto
30-mmutes service has been reduced to a 60-minutes one.
As soon as the contract expires, it’s about up to the Rail-
way Department to build its own boats ; then, perhaps,
, ilhamstown will know the joys of a quick service and
cheap fares.
A new improved drill maker and sharpener is
superseding' the somewhat expensive method of sharpen-
ing drills by hand. The machine will be welcomed by all
mining cos., because the men are able to sharpen up to
about 140 drills an hour without trouble, consequently
increasing the output and reducing the cost, which secures
to the mines substantial benefit. These machines have
been supplied to leading mines everywhere by the makers,
Messrs. Word Bros., of San Francisco, who have ap-
pointed W. & J. Lempriere, of Melbourne and Sydney, the
sole Australian agents. The machine has the distinction
of being a drill maker as well as drill sharpener .*

The introduction into the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small hand rock-drill for
boring “ pops ” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“ hitches ” for timber, and squaring- up shafts and levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad. The best machine for this work is the
“ Shaw ” Hand Rock -Drill and is manufactured by the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 161bs.
and has only one working part—the hammer —which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. In some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 2£in. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now using the Shaw-
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne, where
the machine may be seen working.* [c]

SYDNEY.
POLITICAL
POINTS
Only three out of Victoria’s 22 Premiers
have had a longer run than Bent has had now—
Turner. McCulloch and Gillies. By the end of
May, Thomas will have had 1209 days of glory or
the other thing. But he will still have to go over
a year more before he reaches the nearest one of
the other three.
The High Court decision in regard to the
S. Australian Senate seat, which was left in doubt
through the muddling of a local official, shows
that, given a fair count, Labor would have won.
It follows that a Labor man will be sent in by the
S. A. Parliament for the vacancy. That will give
D moeracy a majority against Anti-Sosh in the
Federal Top House.
The fact that the Kidston Government has
not, by this time, lodged in gaol 200 or 300 of the
insufferably insolent Tory law-breakers who faked
the postal vote at the late Queensland election,
intercepted and stole voting papers, and illegally
bullied and threatened voters, is disappointing.
There seems to be material enough to land several
hundred prominent Philpites, male and female,
in gaol for lengthy terms, if the matter was only
followed up.
The shifty Joseph of N. S. Wales has a
characteristic way of providing for reduction of
members (which he doesn’t believe in, but has to
pretend to believe in). Not this Parliament, but
the next is to be asked to authorise a referendum
on the subject, and that will leave it to a still later
Parliament to put the reduction, if sanctioned,
into force for the Parliament next succeeding,
some six years hence. Having explained this,
Joseph flies off at a tangent to explain that there
really should not be any reduction, partly owing
to the grievous wrongs of N.S.W. and partly
of all things on this earth, because of the
new Local Government system—the system which
was to relieve Parliament of so much of its work
as to make a whole lot of members unnecessary.
HARD TO PLEASE.
The Bishop of London headed a deputation from differ-
ent denominations to the music-halls committee of the
County Council, protesting against exhibitions of living
statuary.
THEATRICAL MANAGER : “ Ga rn ! Watcher growlin'
at ! Ain't they a fine body o’ gals ! Wot's
wrong with their figgers, anyway ? ”
The Bulletin hasn’t yet received any
particulars from Mr. E. J. Hodges, of Carrington-
road, Marrickville, the anti-Federal gentleman
who declared in the press that it was distinctly
set forth in the Constitution that the capital was
to be fixed during the life of the first Australian
Parliament, and who accused the Australian Par-
liament of breach of faith in not so fixing it. This
paper asked Mr. Hodges to point out the hitherto
unheard-of clause on which he rested his state-
ment, and, so far, he hasn’t done it. On present
appearances, he doesn’t seem likely to do it. It
looks like a back-down or a crawl-out. At various
times The Bulletin has asked quite a number of
slanderous persons who accused Australia of
breaking—or proposing to break—some pro-
viso or other of the Constitution, to point out
the clause which contained their alleged proviso,
and it has had the bad luck not to get a coherent
reply from one of them. It begins to think that
every one of the State Frights party invents his
grievance and his slander against Australia, and
is so well aware that it is invented that he won’t
even attempt to give particulars. Senator Gould,
the man who said there was a clause in the Con-
stitution which prohibited a Federal graduated
land-tax, has now been silent for about eight
months, though The Bulletin has been asking
him for his clause at intervals all that time.
Despite the huge increase in hank and
savings bank deposits in Victoria during the
past three years, there is no tendency to
speculation. The savings bank deposits grow
greater by half-a-million annually, and the
interest allowed averages only 2£ per
cent. Yet there is no gambling in
land or shares. Perhaps the me-
mories of the land boom are still
joo near. There are no build-
ing societies or land banks to
help along a boom in land or
houses, and most small in-
vestors are buying their homes
through the suburban brancli
bank. This is all right until
the banks want cash or till-
money in a hurry; then good-
bye to the Queen Anne villa in
Malvern, and farewell to the
Gothic cottage at Balaclava.
The Melbourne Stock Ex-
change is desperately dull, and
only dog-eat-dog business is
being done. The fact is there
is no new public money going
into the Stock Exchange. All
the old fields—Walhalla, Mal-
don, Ballarat, Bendigo, Ruth-
erglen—are dead or dying, and
nothing new is being found.
Daylesford is a flash in the pan,
and by Christmas it will be as
dead as it should always have
been. Lenticular bodies of
quartz, hard to find and mostly
patchy in gold, are the characteris-
tics of Daylesford, and too uncer-
tain for the ordinary investor. The
public wants something fresh to
play with—some new field or some
new gambling counter. Possibly the
promised stiff Protective tariff will
create big new industries, to be carried
on by joint-stock capital, and the ex-
pected boom may follow next year.
Meanwhile, with the Collingwood tote closed,
no betting in the newspapers, the Chinese casinos
shut, the two-up schools kept on the run by the
police, the crack clubs closed, barred, and bolted
at 11.30, Melbourne is bereft of chance, risk, or
speculation, and money accumulates while men
decay.
The paralysis of the Carruthers Govern-
ment in the matter of the crimping evil at New-
castle begins to be ridiculous. Newcastle is
practically bailed up by the men who run the sailors 5
boarding-houses, and who refuse to “supply 55
seamen except at a ridiculous profit to them-
selves, and under wholly illegal conditions. Con-
sequently, the harbor is full of loaded ships which
can’t get out, and of ships which can’t load because
loaded vessels are in the way. The Carruthers
Government knows all about the Federal capital,
and a good deal about how to run the Federal
mail service, but it doesn’t seem to know in the
least how to deal with this conspiracy to black-
mail the commerce of the second port in the
State.
Little Carruthers considerably tired the
representatives of the other States at the recent
Premiers’ Conference in Brisbane with his in-
cessant whine about State rights and Federal
oppression. Said Treasurer Peake to him at last:
“Isn’t New South Wales represented in the
Commonwealth Parliament?”
Federal Minister Ewing is again asking
State Insect Carruthers for particulars, but it is
safe to bet that he won’t get them. Joey was re-
ported as having stated at a gaud Mayor’s banquet
that, though Defence was under the control of the
Federal authorities, the Federal Government had
declined to spend a shilling in N.S.W. waters or
in connection with its navy. Ewing points out
that last Defence estimates provided £195,000 for
expenditure in N.S.W., apart from its share of
the expenditure for stores, equipment, etc., and
he asks Carruthers either to explain his yarn, or
to state that he never uttered the lie ascribed to
him. Australia is waiting with a firm conviction
that Joe won’t explain in the least.
More Carruthers re the Brisbane Con-
ference :
Underlying the whole business (the State debts ques-
tion) is the “ sport of the thing’’—the domineering and
imperious attitude of the Federal Government, which will
brook no delay, and will not condescend to patient dis-
cussion, but insists on the delivery of the ultimatum,
which is to be answered while the sands run down in the
hour-glass. I told Mr. John Forrest that we wanted to
use the good old seven-day clock to measure the time
rather than to be so dictatorially ordered to decide within
the hour. The Commonwealth is only seven years old,
and it wants to do everything within that time, and
spring upon an unprepared people all sorts of novel and
undigested schemes, simply to show its authority.
When the Commonwealth wants to do something
that doesn’t go to prop up Joey’s little speck of
“dignity” as a State Premier, Carruthers says
that the Commonwealth is only seven years old,
and the time is yet too green. But when the
Commonwealth objects to plant the Federal capi-
tal on Carruthers’ back-door step in Sydney,
which Carruthers thinks would bring him kudos
from his Tory friends as the man who ensured the
capital’s trade to Sydney, then Carruthers snarls
about “unreasonable delay,” and threatens to
secesh because the Ma State has been “Robbed
of her Rights” for seven whole years. What a
cantankerous molecule it is !
“ Cam ” writes :
Melbourne Age digs up a disgraceful reminiscence of
the anti-Australian gang which calls itself Free-
trade. In order to compete in price with imported
shoddy, some Australian woollen mills took to making
a sort of flannel which was partly cotton —clean, fresh
cotton, not filthy rags used up again. They didn’t sell
this as all wool, but honestly admitted that it was
shandy-gaff flannel. Nevertheless, the importers, cheered
on by the Melbourne Argus, had it analysed, presented
the analyses to the Tariff Commission with a scathing
denunciation of Australian manufactures, and made a
demand that the nice, clean, pure imported stuff should
not be penalised as against this Australian rubbish.
That was just about a year ago. And the other
day, when it was proved that many imported
silks are fully half shoddy and other foreign matter,
that they are loaded with arsenic and other poi-
sons, and that they are sold by the lying Foreign-trader
as pure materials, the Melbourne Argus again became an
angry paper. But it was a different kind of angry paper.
It wanted to know why the woman of moderate means
should be deprived of the satisfaction of wearing a nice
shimmering material, sold to her under false pretences at
double its value, and it was very wrath indeed at the
foreign swindler being interfered with. The Freetrade
press is the anti-Australian press all the time, and it
seems willing to barrack for any swindle or any poison-
ing device, so long as it is the importer who perpetrates
it, and the mere Australian who suffers by it.
NO SURRENDER !
VALIANT JOR (nailing his unpaid bills to a gum-

to come on): “ Who steals a Capital site
steals trash—but ther blessed cow who robs me

“ P.” makes a serious allegation :
Re failure of the Vic. Labor party at State elections.
A g-ood deal of it is due to Tom Mann and his Socialist
party. Mann wishes to be sole ruler of the Democrats ot
Victoria, and to that end he pushes some of his mosf
enthusiastic supporters into the P.L.O. branches in the
various districts. If a selected candidate does not suit
them, they take measures to kill his political chances.
Witness the treatment meted out to T. Smith at
Fawkner, during the Federal elections. That so-called
fight for free speech seemed to be carried on for the pur-
pose of killing Smith’s chance of election. In vain the
local branches protested and pointed out the harm that
was being done to the Labor cause. As to a fight for
free speech, Mann’s party ..takes particular care that any-
one who is opposed to it doesn’t get a ghost of a chance
of free speech ; and I know for a fact that it was
particularly requested to attend the Prime Minister’s
meetings in force where it was possible. The Mannists
say they intend to kill the P.L.O. and replace it by a
Tom Mann organisation.
Carruthers announced, the other day, in
connection with Sandford’s ironworks enterprise,
that the industry didn’t want any Commonwealth
interference or bolstering. Perhaps not; but
Australia’s friend, the Monkey, knowing
that he must have iron and steel.
wouldn’t take any risks. Consequently
he backed his iron and steel works with
his whole wealth and credit; and he
backed the industry to such purpose
that the output of the Imperial Japan- i
ese Steel Works at Wakamatsu has
already reached 90,000 tons a year,
and, according to a British Con-
sular report just issued, is ex-
pected to reach 180,000 tons by
1912. The Australian iron and
steel industry had no interfer-
ence and bolstering for about
120 years—and that’s just why
it never had the strength to get
born and be an industry. It just
remained a chicken in the shell, while
our friend the Monkey’s chicken was hatched and
was fed up into a very powerful fighting rooster.
Marrow-freezing Maoriland shriek from
a religious paper :
Joseph Ward, our Premier, is a Romanist; and so is
the General Manager of the Exhibition, Mr. Munro. The
Civil Service is largely composed of Romanists. How
they get there, God only knows, but dark and mysterious
are the wavs of Rome. . . . Devlin and Donovan have been
here preaching Home Rule. Joseph Ward was at Devlin’s
meeting. ... If the people of Maoriland only knew what
Ward is doing, and what he has done, they would be up
inarms. He is a dark, wily schemer. .. . Joseph Ward
has gone home to the Colonial Conference, there to wag
his oily tongue, well-oiled with unctuous Jesuitical
sophistries and finesse. . . . We hope you will watch him
well at the Conference, for he is a sly dog, this same
Joseph Ward.
A bit vague and breathless, perhaps ; a trifle
lacking in definiteness, possibly ; but how telling
an indictment withal ! The man is unmasked,
and should go away and drown himself without
delay.
One A. Consett Stephen, presumably the
solicitor of that name, wrote last week to Sydney
Telegraph, suggesting that the N.S.W. State
Government should go to the Orient s.s. Co. and
“ offer them such subsidy as may be deemed
reasonable by them for the taking of our mails,”
and he added that “someone must come to the
rescue, otherwise there will be no mail service at
all —that is evident.” Truly, they are a wild,
mad lot —these provincialists, Australia will have
various mail lines even if there is a temporary
break in the Orient service—evidently Mr. Con-
sett Stephen never heard of the P. and O. and
the Vancouver lines. And his proposal to pay the
Orient Co. for a mail service anything it thinks
reasonable is insanely rash; he has no idea
how much it might think reasonable. Then
there is something fine and large about
the picture of a respectable, middle-aged
solicitor like Mr. Stephen leading a storm-
ing party into the Sydney G.P.O. to get
illegal possession of the Federal mails so as to put
them on the State mail-boats ; and the Federal
troops defending the premises ; and the stairs
covered with nose-blood, torn coat-tails, and shat-
tered top-hats. It is wonderful to consider the
number of high-handed, violent and illegal acts—
acts that would almost certainly end in tumult
and gore—that are advocated in the Sydney press
by wild but provincial-minded gentlemen who
never really hurt a fly except under great provo-
c tion or chloroform.
Sydney Telegraph, in a leading article on
the starch industry (12/6/’O7), did this marvel of
confusion and misrepresentation :
But even this poor pay is not yielded by the market
value of what they produce. Starch enjoys a Protective
duty of 100 per cent., which, vith the cost of freight and
insurance, raises the price in Melbourne to more than
double the f.o.b. quotations in London, the difference
being between £lB 14s. per ton and £4l per ton. There
is an excise equal to half the duty, so that the net Pro-
tection to the manufacturer is 50 per cent., while the
consumer pays the 100 per cent, on the imported article
all the same. And this is to keep in existence an i udustry
that now pleads inability to pay a reasonable wage to
those whom it employs. Every laundress in the < ommon-
wealth is put under contribution, and her scanty wages
reduced in order to make up the starvation pay of the
starch makers.... It may be that what a South Aus-
tralian Judge recently described as the “ esoteric mys-
tery” of the balance-sheet prevented Mr. Justice Hood
from understanding the true condition of the starch
business as presented by the employers’ figures, and
caused him to give a decision based on an incorrect inter-
pretation of them. If that is so, an undue share of the
tax is going into the manufacturers’ pockets, and the
employees are being choused out of a public contribution
levied for their benefit. If it is not so, and things are
what they seem, it means that consumers of
starch are paying twice the market value of the article to
keep alive an industry which does not afford a living
wage.
The real position is this : If consumers used im-
ported starch, they would pay the high price
that the ’Graph speaks of, but they don’t; they
use the local article. The local article, through
excess of local competition, has gone down in
price till it is miles below the present
price of the imported article, and the local
laundress is just now getting her starch
at a cheaper figure than usual. Through the low
price wages are down to what Australia con-
siders a starvation level, though they are still
fully 50 per cent, above English rates. (In some
parts of England just now you can hire a skilled
carpenter, who is much more of an artisan than a
starch-maker, for 4yd. per hour, or 21s. 4|d. for a
week of 54 hours; and the lowest wages paid to full -
grown men in the Australian starch trade are far
ahead of that.) The Bulletin’s remedy is to fix
a decent minimum wage, and let the price of
starch go up to correspond—it can do that, and
still be as cheap as any decent human being
would ask. The Telegraph’s remedy is to open
our ports to the goods of all the world, and to add
to the local competition which has made wages
low, a foreign competition which will make them
lower.
The silence of William Kidston, State
Premier of Queensland, is troubling the Pbilp
party greatly. Most of it almost lives at the tele-
phone, on the off-chance that Kidston will ring up
and propose a coalition ; but so far Kidston might
be dead for all the ringing he does. If William
from Scotland only sits tight he will produce a re-
markable situation. He can meet Parliament and
lay down his programme. Nothing can throw
him out except a coalition between declared
Socialism under Bowman and old crusted Toryism
under Philp. If they do combine to throw him
out, they can’t combine to form a Government,
and no Government that either of them could
form can possibly live except by the permission of
Kidston. Therefore, the obvious course is to go
ahead, and let things sort themselves out. A still
mbre obvious thing would be for Kidston to bring
down a programme of one item —the passing of a
Bill to provide for Elective Ministries with a
tenure of office covering the life of one Parlia-
ment. That is the remedy for the three and four
and five and twenty party difficulty. But Kidston
comes from Falkirk, and they don’t try veiv
radical experiments in a very great hurry at
Falkirk.
WASTED ENERGY.
Rev. Dr. Fitchett prefaced his subject by some compli-
mentary references to Mr. Reid, He was a statesman,
not only of Australia, but of the Empire.. . . When the
history of the first 50 years of the Commonwealth was
written, the name of George Reid would appear in bril-
liant letters.
Little Boy : “ It's no good a-rubbin' of that ole
plate , Mrs. Fidget. The letters is near wore
off already .”
The bailiff has sometimes a better billet
than most highly-paid civil servants. Two of
him in Sydney are said to make £lOOO a year
each. The fee for serving an ordinary debt sum-
mons is out of all reason. Take, for example, the
North Sydney Court. A small-debts summons
costs 3s. for service alone in the borough. If the
bailiff steps across the boundary line into Mosman
or Willoughby, the fee is increased.
“ I applied for the position of gardener at
Mr. Carruthers’ house to a city florist. He said,
‘You will have to work on Sundays, as Mr. Car-
ruthers has tennis parties every Sunday, and yon
will have to mow the lawn ; but Mr. Carruthers will
help you push the mowing-machine.’ I said. Mr.
Carruthers can do the lot, as far as I am concerned,
for I won’t work for Father Peter on Sunday.’
How on earth working men can vote for such
scrouchers is beyond my ken. ’’ Extract from letter
read, at Sydney Labor Council on Thursday of
last week.

THE VARNISHED BOARDj
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 18, 1907.
My Dear Arini,—
Various inventions for the coming W.W. Ex-
hibition are credited to women. One has invented
a billiard table. Most likely this will turn out
to merely signify some changes in the old billiard
table. But if some woman would invent a satis-
factory stove, or oven, or range, others of her
species would arise and call her blessed, and buy
the article. Men have, up to date, planned the
cooking apparatus, and women have to cook
on man’s rascally and incompetent device.
(These few remarks are not intended for gas-
stoves —though they are bad enough.) Wives
and daughters sorrowfully put up with the thing
that the other branch of the human species
devised; the “general” or cook doesn’t. She
just says “I can’t stand that oven. Will you
please suit yourself?” One fiend of a stove I
have in my eye (a most unpleasant thing to have
in one’s eye is a stove) affords no chance for the
removal of the cinders except by groping with
one’s hands from the top and lifting out the
slag personally. The fool-inventor who doesn’t
allow for the raking-out of a firebox from
the front, or by means of a trap door from
underneath, ought to be given 10 years
hard cooking in his own diabolical contraption.
Another good stove to give to a bitter enemy is
heated by a flame that passes first over the top.
The heat is over instead of under the cooking
cavern. So nary a “rise” is there in the puff
paste, or the sponge cake or scones. Then con-
sider the dirty and unnecessary labor in getting the
beastie clean ! The lifting off of circular iron lids,
the pulling out of little knobs, the brush-work
that covers one with soot, and the fooling with
iron tools that often stick fast down in the narrow
alleyways of the vacuum spaces ! No wonder
Sarah Janes give notice and go into cigar
factories, like Carmen, who, I firmly believe,
acquired her vile temper through beginning life
as a general servant in Spain, and presently
threw the stove bodily at her missus and left
with all her finer feelings demolished.
The small woman with the figure of the adoles-
cent has at least one advantage over the larger
lady of the same age, even if she doesn’t show off
her gowns so well. She looks years younger.
Miss Marie Hall and Miss Lonie Basche are
almost the same age. There is only a few
months’ difference (Miss Basche takes the differ-
ence) in their ages, yet Lonie (awful name), the
Large Girl, looks almost maternal near Marie,
the Small Girl. Lonie (amazing name) has a
beautiful figure, and she could make her fair and
rather big head quite picturesque by curling all
the straight, rat’s-tail ends of hair, or getting a
hairdresser to give eighteen penn’orth of moral
support. On Friday, Mrs. Mayoress of Sydney
Hughes asked a large piece of Sydney society to
“meet” the star fiddler. Star and star-gazers
met at “ Cranbrook Cottage,” the Hughes’ place
at Rose Bay. The two girls were escorted by
.manager Tait.
A sympathetic manager, watching over the
“ meeting ” of a youthful star with a lot of dressy,
assured, experienced women, is an object of com-
miseration no matter how happy he looks and how
well his frock coat fits him. Even though
his tie is of the latest shade of brown—
“ scorched bread ” brown —he is to be pitied.
He longs with a savage longing for the star to
verbally interest the crowd that he desires to see
later on in the front seats of the Town Hall, and
he regrets with a fierce regret that when the
reception is over she hasn’t said anything but
weather things, or repeated the dry facts she’s
already told those stump-extractors—the press
interviewers. “ Don’t you think,” says a musical
amateur, showing a lot of auriferous teeth, “that
the perfect accord of the human voice and the
violin is most magical in its charm?” And the
star replies fervently, “ O yes ” (of course, she
doesn’t say “Yairs” or “Yep”). If she were
older and more knowing she might hurl a long,
rhapsodic reply in the golden teeth, and the
musical amateur would go away and tell her friends
that the star was the embodiment of a gigantic
intelligence. Especially would she say this if the
rhapsody was wholly unintelligible. There is more
“ bluff ” put up about music than about any
other art.
But to get back to Marie Hall. At her Meet
with society she looked very artistic in a frock of
something green and silken trimmed with moss-
green velvet, a black picture hat, and a necklace
of gold beads. She is a pretty girl in a refined,
uncommon way, but one doesn’t appreciate her
personal charms except at short range. She isn’t
a telescopic girl. The beautiful, quiet eyes, sweet
smile, perfect neck, and silky hair look best the
more closely they are inspected. Marie Hall can
bear being walked round three times and viewed
narrowly from every point. On a platform she
looks insignificant, for want of significant dress-
ing. I wouldn’t mind wagering that if a smart
woman of the world turned her clothes intellect
onto little Hall, she could make the girl as much
a topic as was Paderewski. Her frocking (at
least on Saturday night) accentuated her mezzo
coloring and slenderness. Can’t the Tait
induce her to get into a long, long-tailed, waist-
less robe of vivid scarlet, or turquoise blue —
the brightest turquoise, with gold or silver arab-
esques? And her hair should be frizzed out
like] a mad nimbus. Marie Hall ought to be
gowned in a style that would make the musical
amateurs think of a clarion blast and the Turkish
Patrol. Then she and her fiddle would make
something of a picture. At present, they remind
of the first wash-in of a sepia sketch.
As a musician, the girl is difficult to place. She
is great within the limits of her age, her physique
and her condition—all of which must be taken
into account in estimating her position in the
violin world to-day. Her playing is virginal.
In that sense, her expression of emotion is
limited and sometimes monotonous. But time
and experience
Lady Northcote was at the young violinist’s
first recital, and seemed to gloat over the Max
Bruche concerto. Mrs. Carrefio has been received
by her Ex. privately. Miss Leverrier, another
Lady of the Ivory, was at the House Excellent at
the same happy hour, but the piano fled into a
place of concealment, and there was no music.
A sixteen-year-old bride is a rarity these days.
Miss Vera Isley, the Ohatswood girl who sang for
the Yorks when the public schools gave the Royal
pair a treat, is the that-aged bride. She recently
married a Moree solicitor, a widower named
Zlotkowski. The honeymoon is being spent at the
Solomon Islands, where the bride will have time
to spell her new name in peace.
Miss May Yohe has married for the third time.
It happened in New York, and the third sole
object of the lady’s affections is a commercial
man named Newton Browne. Sydney first saw
May as a coon-song singer. Then as Lady Francis
Hope. Then it heard of her as divorced and
married to an American magnate’s son, named
Strong. Now she’s Mrs. Brown. Later on she
may be Mrs. Smith. Some day she’ll be dead.
That frisky, risky Hartwell and lady left these
shores on Saturday. When they decided to cut
their legal bonds they used no superfluous words in
the letters of farewell they wrote their respective
legal mates. The beautiful lady wrote about six
lines beginning, “I have leftyou forever,” and say-
ing who the new love was ; and the pretty gentle-
man wrote to his consort, also saying, “I have
left you for ever,” and explaining that he could no
longer go on living the semi-detached life. Per-
haps the consort is going to live up to the family
motto of her hubby (“ Contented with his lot ”),
for she hasn’t started to divorce him.
Met Miss Heinze, the Carmen of the German
opera 'co., to-day, and nearly dropped dead from
a rush of admiration to the head. When I saw
her Carmen I vowed it was Bruce Smith in dis-
guise. But Heinze is perfectly beautiful. She has
an extra chin, it’s true, and she is heavier than
twenty-four years should be. But her features
are as classical as those of a Greek marble, she
has eyes like Palotta’s —grave grey eyes with
great black iris, and her hair is a flaxen-golden.
A real blonde. What a pity Carmen can’t be
played fair! The wife of Rilnger (call him
Ringer, or you’ll make him mad) is another very
handsome circumstance. She is dark, and doesn’t
sing—just sits in the stalls and admires Him.
He’s a gay young athletic party. His wife says
the English are thin because they cannot eat their
own cooking.
Prof. Starr Jordan left Sydney last week. One
of his last jaunts was to the Hawkesbury College
with Mr. Frank Coffee, an old schoolfellow. The
pair of old-young boys were born in the same
town, and attended the same school in Western
New York.
Mr. Leonard Dangar and Miss Mary Anderson,
of Glen Innes, are being congratulated on their
engagement. Also, Rev. O’Breznan Ball, of
Kelso, and Miss Lee, of Bathurst. Rev. F. Met-
calf, chaplain of the Powerful, is engaged to a
daughter of Mr. J. Jackson, an architect of North
Sydney.
A piece of Limerick lace (made in Limerick),
with a design in Australian wild flowers, is in
possession of the nuns of St. Vincent’s. Said to
be one of the finest pieces in the hemisphere. It
will be shown at the forthcoming Women’s Work
Exhibition. There will also be exhibits from
the Parramatta woollen mills—beginning with
the raw material, and ending with a piece of the
cloth made for Lady Northcote. A Sydney girl
is binding the programme for the opening of the
Ex. for her Ex.
The State Governor, speeches, tea, and—in the
language of daily journalese—a large and dis-
tinguished gathering, all helped to make the por-
trait presentation to Alderman Allen Taylor a
live function. His State Ex., who made the pre-
sentation, said among other true and flattering
things, that “the works carried out during
Alderm tn Taylor’s period of office had certainly
made Sydney a much better place than it was
before. He congratulated the committee and
citizens on paying honor to one who so well
deserved it.” Also he said pretty things about
Mrs. Taylor’s capability as first civic citizeness.
He didn’t know that Sonny Forster Taylor has a
new motor car, in which he travels like greased
lightning slipping on orange peel, or he might
have advised Sonny to go slower for fear of not
inheriting the oil portrait of Pa. Many a good
portrait has gone out of the family through a wild
young heir trying to be too much like the son of
Nimshi.
Mr. Ken Mackenzie, of Brisbane, writes to say
that the statement of my Brisbane correspondent,
that he was recently married to Miss Gladys Hill,
is incorrect. But as he is engaged to the lady, and
as she is a lady that any man might be proud to
be engaged to, I can only say that I am sorry the
marriage hasn’t yet come off. I think it would
have been better to hurry up the happy event
than put me. to the trouble of this explanation.
The hats which the demi-mondaines of Long-
champs and Auteuil are wearing to-day, the
nicest and most virtuous hereabouts will be
attaching to their upright brows some twelve
months hence. That is an unchanging law of
fashion. The tiny headwear grotesques of
yester-year are doomed. This month and last,
mighty, umbrella-like structures dominated
the Paris grandstands and hid the faces of
Lutetia’s fairest. Frail trifles like cigarettes
are being discarded for hat ornamentation, and
cassowary and bustard wings are used in
their stead. In fact, the era is fast approaching
in these parts when the short, stout woman, who
staggers doggedly down the street bearing a
stuffed emu on her skull, will be acclaimed the
last cry in the world of dress. It is an appalling
prospect.
One of the new lamp-shade hats is pervading
Sydney on the head of a small woman. It is a
face-extinguisher in red silk, with a big silver rose.
The Franz Degens left by the Kleist for Euro-
pean parts, and Sydney is the darker for the loss
of two bright and attractive personalities. Mrs.
Degen is a beautiful little plump woman, to whom
life is all joy, and her pleasure in being alive is
good to look upon.
A friend sends me from London a glowing
compliment from The Spectator of April 13,
which says:—
Perhaps the greatest of colonial critical journals is the
Sydney Bulletin. Certainly that famous paper stands
easily first in respect of its influence on the social life of
which it is so powerful a reflection. In a sense, the Syd-
ney Bulletin is Australia.
This introducing a paragraph about The Lone
Hand. It makes for joy, since The Spectator
is certainly the finest of the British organs of—to
use a much-abused word—culture. Not cul-
chaw, which is quite a different thing.

about London. It shows the Marble Arch and a

fascinated. You’ll never tire of this great old
Hub.” The other is from Mrs. Walter Elkington,
and carries a rose, russet, and amber view of
Glarens (Heavenly Clarens, birthplace of young
Love. Look up your “ Childe Harold”), and re-
marks that “this is just the entrancing spot you’d
revel in.” Fancy writing those invitations to a
person who is sea-sick on a Mosman ferry boat I

veils have a border frill, and all shoulders slope
down towards the elbow. .. . Ten days after my

Melba’s daughter-in-law) “in a private hospital.
She is recovering from appendicitis. She looked
a dream of beauty, and her room was a bower of
roses and lilies of the valley. Melba, son George
and wife leave in August for Melbourne. The
Star has taken a house forty miles from that city
for six months.”

Bowker.
Mrs. Fitzgerald, widow of the well-known
circus proprietor, has been'in Sydney after a two
years’ absence, that made her heart grow fonder
of this good old city. For a year she has been
going to and fro on the European continent, look-
ing for circus novelties. Mrs. Fitz found them, and
is waiting for more, and in the spring will open in
Melbourne. Meantime, she goes to Maoriland to
have a few months’ rest with a sister who abides
in the Wellington district. Searching for the
trained pony, and the trick tiger, and the witty
clown, the india-rubber human, and the new
novelty which isn’t too old, must be fatiguing
graft. It will be remembered that Mr. Dan Fitz-
gerald died in Sydney, and some months later
Mr. Tom Fitzgerald succumbed in India. It was
a most disastrous year for the Fitzgerald co.
Six humans, eighteen ponies, and a lot of ele-
phants passed out within the twelve months.
“Rosna” writes from Adelaide :
Adelaide Government House has come out of its long,
heavy sleep, and got into its best clothes to welcome
back her Ex., who returned from Britain last week. She’s
a very healthy Ex. after her jaunt. The one chick stays
in Germany at school.
Adelaide Society (for the prevention) of Arts is suffer-
ing from an Exhibition. It had its opening and catver-
sazione on Wednesday evening (12th), the exalted popu-
lace being invited for 8 o’clock, and the riff-raff and
tumultuous herd for 8.45. Also the aforesaid exalted
ones had fruit-salads and other delicacies upstairs
behind a closed door, while the riff-raff and squalid
multitude were fed upon buns and sandwiches
on the basement. Being one of the riff-raff,
I was unable to discover who were concealed behind the
closed door, more particularly as everyone with any pre-
tensions to ton seemed to be among the basement sand-
wich-eaters. They included a Commissioner and his
family, several consuls, all manner of moneyed sassiety
folk, and all the artists who had provided the exhibition.
The show itself is disappointing. Adelaide daubers
seem to have a distressing fondness for bright green land-
scapes and peacock-blue sea waves, and this year many
of them have added to the confusion by putting in
canvases painted in the dark ages and already exhibited.
A few exceptions make the show worth a glande. Will
Ashton, as usual, has done good work, and exhibits,
among others, a fine seascape entitled “Squally Weather.”
Julius Ohlson’s “Coast of the Sirens,” shown at last
Federal Exhibition, re-appears, and is certainly worth a
second view. The most interesting exhibitors of the
year are Misses Rose McPherson and Bessie Davidson,
lately returned from Paris, who show a number of striking
and delicately-painted studies that have already been
hung on the line at the Paris Salon and various other
places. Among them are a fine study of Flemish women,
some gorgeous hydrangeas, “The Studio Window” by
Miss McPherson, and a couple of original canvases of
Tangiers (Morocco) by Miss Davidson. The Hambidge
sisters are, as usual, well represented, and show portraits
of (among others) little Edith Le Hunte and Mrs. Herbert
Rymill. Rhoda Holder shows a clever portrait study
that lately won first prize at a Melbourne exhibition ; and
another successful she-dauber is Miss Constance Stuckey,
who also has foreign study behind her. Hans Heysen
exhibits a number of realistic-looking torrents, glaciers
and other wild things that abound in the Maori isles ;
George Webb has portraits of Sammy Way and Ed. T.
Smith. The only nude pictures are provided by H. P.
Gill, and they are pretty weird.
John Raymond Wilton, a nice Adelaide hoy of 23, has
secured the job of Fifth Wrangler in the mathematical
tripos at Cambridge (England). John was a pupil of
Prince Alfred College, where he collected all the avail-
able scholarships, prizes, etc., as freely as if they were
bottles. Then he went on to our little pet ’Varsity, still
raking in laurels, took his B.Sc. at the age of 19,
and went off to Cambridge with it. There he
won a sub-sizarship (I don’t know what kind of a
quadruped that may he, but it is worth £35). The next
year he gathered in a full-grown sizarship, worth £lOO a
year for three years, and now he has bolted with a major
scholarship in mathematics, valued at £lOO a year for 51-
years. Adelaide’Varsity is throwing up its learned hat.
The laureled youth is a son of journalist Wilton, of the
Advertiser, and is the second South Australian to climb
to these giddy heights of wrangler-ship. The other was
J. J. Stuckey, M.A., who did the deed in 1864.
Harold Parsons, Adelaide’s ’cellist, back from Germany,
'celled for the first time at the Conservatorium orches-
tral concert last week (he is now chief ’cell instructor at
that institution). The Kaiser-country has done marvels
fer him. Another success of the evening was the warb-
ling of Muriel Cheek, a sweet maiden of 19.
Wanda Radford, the Australian actress and wonder-
child, gave a recital in London last month before a
crowded house of titled and exalted folk, who showered
flowers, dolls, sweets and other trophies on her. ’Tis said
that the street outside the hall was strewn for miles with
carriages and haughty mobiles with powdered flunkeys
in attendance, and that the whole peerage came out of its
moated grange. Probably this is an exaggeration. The
child gave representations of Juliet, Portia, Fagin, Shy-
lock, and various other celebrated characters. Animme-;
diate result of the concert was that the kiddy was en-
gaged to perform at a dinner before the Connaught
royalties. The time is probably not far off when Edward
Rex himself will come down off his pedestal, and request
her to sing God Save him.

Certain amateur players from the Lands Office
gave a very clever representation of “Trial by
Jury” last Friday. (They’re funny dogs, these
Lands officials : you’d think they’d heard enough
of juries lately). Miss Jean Maclurcan, in an
Empire gown of white lace, brocade and silver,
as the plaintiff, made the Judge’s mad lapse from
judicial dignity quite excusable.
The Percy Buckleys of Melbourne are in
Sydney, and plunging into theheavymusical season
with apparent zest. Mr. and Mrs. Ernest Villiers
(he is M.P. for Brighton, Eng.) are staying with
Ex. Rawsons at Cranbrook. She is a sister
of Lady Chelmsford, and they are going to Queens-
land presently. The Wilders and their automo-
bile arrived to-day. Albani nestles at the Australia
Hotel. Last evening, at the Albani’s first concert,
the two Government House parties, and the
Admiral, and their respective suites, made a bril-
liant patch at the Town Hall. Albani still flutters
on to the stage with wings outspread, and re-
tains her trick of holding her arms rigidly down
and out in front of her, one small (left) hand laid
open with palm downwards under the thumb of
the right hand. She looks handsome and cheerful.
Husband Gye is a good copy of King Edward.
The Gyes are a most loyal pair.

The doctor has composed both music and lyrics,

From my Brisbane correspondent:—
Thursday saw the return to the Government residence
of her Ladyship and bonny Du Cane. The chatelaine of the
Chelmsford menage is obviously not at all well, but cousin
Enid has taken on a new lease of youth. With the ladies
came a wonderful talking parrot, whose language, it is said,
is something ’orrible ! However, sweet-voiced Miss Scott,
of the cerulean eye, has been placed in charge of the
feathered heathen, whose vocabulary is toning down
wonderfully under her influence.
The Smart Set —smart for Brisbane, at all events—
rolled up in force on Thursday in response to an invite
from the Slade dame, and the pretty lawns and gardens
of Cooreelba were bright with winter hats. Afterall, no
engagement was announced, so presumably the hopes of
a gallant captain still hang in the balance, or the daugh-
ter of the house is still thinking it over. Grace Palotta,
in Assam silk and a dream of a hat, graced the scene.
The air *is thick with rumors of more fete, for the
General Hospital, this time. Meanwhile, the ladies of the
Bowen (Maternity) Home are forging ahead with their
benefit race-meeting, on July 6, which promises well.
Everyone who is anyone is hard at work upon a panel or
a plaque or some stitchery for the Women’s Exhibition,
in which it is confidently expected that the Queen State
will make a brave show. I’m sending a bit of crochet 50
years old myself, and the aged relic should take a bit of
beating.
Id was all very well for the local press to bark viciously
when N.S.W.’s Carruthers said that the Brisbane River
was full of vegetation, bub the statement wasn’t far ou t.
Last Saturday, perhaps as the result of Wednesday’s
rain, a fresh batch of the water hyacinth came down from
the Bremer, and blocked the ferry service in the town
reaches just at the busy 5 o’clock hour. If the Govern-
ment doesn’t get to work pretty smartly, and expend a
little of its Harbor and River funds on eradicating the
pest, there’s a hob time coming for it in the by-and-bve.
A certain parson I hear of allows his wife to employ
“ the fallen,” who is usually a strapping young woman,
whose infant is boarded out. In the ordinary course of
things her wages would be, at the very least, 10s. per
week, but, in the house of the good shepherd, “the
fallen ” gets 6s. The balance is made up with extra long
hours, and lengthy admonitions about her sins. One
lately spent a happy time of rest in a hospital, and
her ailment was simply overwork and misery. The
nurses petted her a bit, and when she was nearly con-
valescent, her reverend employer came—to inquire when
Mary could return to work, presumably still on 6s. per
week.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
It is comforting for Australia to know that
at the artillery headquarters (both Melbourne
and Sydney) there used to be employed two Jap
servants. They were the same Japs, and after
serving the officer man in Melbourne they came
up to Sydney and served the officer man here.
They were good, intelligent servants, too, and
probably what they don’t know now about Sydney
and Melbourne defences isn’t worth alluding to.
“Dejected” (which is a sorrowful pen-
name) holds forth from Adelaide :
I read with interest A. W. Hyman’s par (B. 6/6/’O7) re
fighting qualities of Russian Jews and their suitability
for settlement in Australia. Now, I don’t know much
about their fighting capacity, but I do know that for dirt
and indolence they can beat the Hindu or Chow easily.
I travelled through, and also lived amongst, whole
colonies of Russian Jews in Entre Rios, Argentina, and
of all the lazy, motley crews that have - settled out there,
the aggregate Abraham from Russia is the worst. It
will take Isaac Moscow about 200 generations to assimilate
up to the lowest standard of Australian coekydotn. I
have been brought in contact with nearly all the farming
colonies in America, and for good citizens I give my vote
to the Danes, Germans, Basques (from Spain), and
Italians. The Danes and Germans have assimilated, and
can fight into the bargain. So why wait for Abraham
Muscovy ?
London Morning Leader, a Freetrade
paper, had a curious cartoon re preferential bade
in its issue of May 7. The said picture showed a
miserable, emaciated woman, gaunt and hungry
under Freetrade; a pathetic baby, looking very
withered under Freetrade; a small boy who didn’t
seem to get enough to eat under Freetrade ; and
a small, depressed girl, who looked utterly
wretched under Freetrade. And to them a
large, prosperous-looking, Protectionist Alfred
Deakin was represented as saying : “ Surely
when we propose that your loaf should be
taxed to give us a preference, you are not
going to turn to us the cold shoulder.” The
moral obviously is that the policy which pro-
duced the wretched-looking family shouldn’t be
disturbed on any account, and that it is mere
effrontery, to offer advice to people who know
their business so well that they have filled the
country with 12,000,000 people just like the tired,
shabby wreck of a woman and the three hungry
children.
