MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, June 17, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie.—
“ St. Vincent’s ” is always the ball of the early
season, and Thursday’s was a successful climax to
the frantic planning and practising of many folk
for weeks and more. The Madden set was
worth much pictorially. “ Early Victorian
was the key-note, and the girls wore stun-
ningly short white muslin dresses that looked like
“Quality Street” with their small blue
bows. The ankles were exceedingly neat and
white stockinged, and the shoeing was black and
satin and old-time strapped. The shoulders were
particularly uncovered, and the hair piled up and
out like the frame of a picture. The men angles
of the set might have stepped out of Dickens.
The jolly Campbell captain was a blue-stocked,
blue-socked Pickwick. A Fitzgerald item wore a
green stock with his tight and early toggery. Aide
Vallange just hit the top note of an exquisite
with his carefully-tied white stock and frilly
linen; but his tight-and-early-Victorians made
him look like the long and lean Carlton of jug-
gling fame.
It was a night of “ sets ” ; Mrs. Ernest Brooke’s
Powder and Patches were exquisite and cold-
looking as a snow scene. A gay little agricultural
party wore peasant dress (more short frocks) and
carried hay forks that did delightful damage all
night. Many shepherdesses and Dresden china
folk “ setted ”to partners on their own. It was
nearly eleven before the dancers-in-chief got into
swing. It was too cold and early for “sitting-
out ” without the exhilaration of just one turn,
and later poor Edwin and Angelina had to dance
brilliantly to keeptheir blood in circulation, while
the shepherdesses and hay-rakers gazed at the
stars from sentimental and shadowy corners.
The Alliance Frangaise gave the cunningest
little party on Friday evening. It was in the
Independent Hall, of the Independent Church —
which sounds grim and staid; but under the
magic hands of the hostesses (Mesdames Mas-
seran and Tobin) the whole, effect was dainty and
Parisian. Dead-and-gone clergymen, in solemn
portraiture round the walls, coldly watched
the French frivol. Cute drapings of tri-
color and blazing touches of massed scar-
let geranium brightened the Sombre walls. Mrs.
Janson sang from St. Saens’ “Samson and
Delilah,” and someßomances of Chaminade ; also,
in duet with Napoleon Boffard, she sang from
“II Trovatore.” Mrs. Charbonnet-Kellermann
(who is the proud mother of the graceful mermaid
Annette) played. She is leaving for England, and
medico Crevelli speeched for the Alliance a
regretful farewell. The beautiful little lady
answered for herself in lightning French. With
pretty gesture she declared that Victorians were
most kind to musical artists; they “put the
singing birds into such sweet golden cages that
the birds did not care to fly away again.” An
orchestra gave a good account of itself. It was
called the Victorian Soft Goods Orchestra, but
it had nearly as much brass as the Besses, and
might quite easily have been the Ironmongery or
Hard Ware Band.
The favored few who gained access to the inner
chamber where the Women’s Exhibition posters,
diplomas and medals were displayed last week,
had an illuminating time. There’s nothing better
on the hoardings than the best of the
work. At its worst, it shatters for ever
the i'lusion that women have no sense of humor.
Angels figure largely on all the posters unblessed
by artistic skill. Draw your own inferences.
First prize goes, of course; to Bendigo. “ The
Athens of Australia” always makes a nuisance
of itself in competitive examinations. The poster,
which depicts a row of winsome young things in
pastel shades tripping along to somewhere, will
presently gladden the landscape from the Gulf to
the Bight, etc. Number two is a starchy blue
amazon, with dawn-lit domes and a yellow
sky behind her, a really fine lady for advertis-
ing purposes. The sister of a big black-and-
white man draws third prize, with a wonderfully
clever bit of work. Every known industry is
represented by a fair maid, modern to her finger-
tips. She has grouped these to form an absolutely
correct map of Australia. A florist’s triumph
forms Cape York, a plate of cakes much of the
Northern Territory, the fall of a skirt Port
Phillip, and a shawl Port Adelaide. It is mag-
nificent, but it isn’t proper poster work according
to the committee’s notions. The artist, however,
carries off first prize for diplomas. A South Aus-
tralian artist gets first for medals, a well-known
sculptor coming second.
The University Rowing Club ball, on Monday
night, had quite a lot to say for itself. There
was a large percentage of verdant youth in the
mazy throng, and almost half-a-hundred “ debs ”
blossomed out on this rose-bowered occasion. A
tall, good-looking student, who has made much
splash recently in Trinity exams., engineered the
debutantes’ set, which was managed quite thea-
trically and for one turn held the whole of the
floor space—excepting the official set (led by the
Chance-Excellency) at the other end of the ball-
room. The combined deb ballet carried silver
shepherdess crooks, with vegetable attachments
that threatened danger to any “ transformations ”
and “extra” curls which got into their vicinity.
A giddy whirl it was, softly lighted and well
suppered.
Some of the dressing was gorgeous. I saw a
woman of grace and soft grey hair, in a kimono
embroidered with blue silk —the white cherry petals
might easily have just fallen with a breeze. Little
Leslie Madden made a blooming re-appearance in
blushes and a pink gown. Quite a tired-looking
Ruby wore black, and Mrs. Madden herself
smiled sweetly from a frame of softest blue crepe.
Mrs. Vallange, by the way, mothered the party,
and hustled the aides in the intricate maze of the
“opening” set.
At the University ball Dame Forrest arrived
early, with a jolly budget of young folk ready for
a good time. Bigjohn was to follow with a man
party of Westralians and Bananalanders, whom he
was dining lavishly. Time sped along, and it
took all the little Dame’s smiles and wiles to
make up for the non-appearance of the Acting
Prime Minister. His party included several
notables, and the dance-secretaries and the aides
were tying themselves into knots arranging a to-be
official set in correct placement. All their study
seemed for nothing, when, just on the stroke of
twelve, like a conglomerate Cinderella, the dinner
party arrived in most jovial mood, and the wearied
secretaries roused themselves for one last effort
over the much-postponed Lancers. Carriages had
been ordered for twelve, but chauffs. shivered and
coachmen grumbled till three o’clock, when the
elevated (this means the high and mighty) dancers
at last consented to be toted home.
A clever little Melbourne hospital nurse won
much praise last week. It was her “day off,”
and she was out in her best bib and tucker vvhen
a street accident roused her professional instinct.
It was a case of cut artery. Pushing the useless
crowd aside, the little hospital maid knelt down,
whipped off her garter, bound the injured man’s
arm, and fixed him up just as the ambulance
arrived. She saved the man’s life. He should
be thanking his lucky stars that the prompt small
personage was conservative in her hose-gear. A
“suspender” would have been no use—and
awkward to detach, anyhow.
Dr. Crevelli, the man of the many hand-
some sons, has just bought Gordon Lyon’s
great estate. Since old Gordon’s death, three
years ago, young Gordon has cut well into his
money, and none of it went to the up-keep of the
fine old place. Old Gordon Lyon spent time and
thought and much legal tender on improvements
—especially an extensive irrigation scheme.
These have been left to crumble. Dr. Crevelli is
doing much to tone things up. The house is to
be completely refurnished, and the big and hos-
pitable family will divide its time between there
and the red dwelling of suburbia.
Colonel Ricardo was accepted as a “ first-class
life ” the week before his death, when he increased
his policy by £lOOO. The knowledge of this fact
inspired him with such confidence in his physical
soundness that he went a-hunting again after a 10
years’ spell, and met his death. The Colonel died
poor, and his widow is fortunate in possessing a
father able and willing to provide handsomely for
her and her children.
A rich and generous father is a convenient asset,
whether the occasion is one of weal or woe. One
of the prettiest homes on Mount Macedon has
been presented as a wedding gift by Mr. Hogg to
his daughter, who married Mr. Norman Smith.
Just now Melbourne is roused up to a spasm of
interest in the boarded-out babies. Miss Suther-
land, this city’s expert on the reclamation of
neglected children, is besieged by questioners
anxious to air her views as their own. Said
whole-hearted lady, with a houseful of infants
and a yardful of milking cows to look after, must
find it pretty galling when her scant leisure is
intruded upon by voluble experts anxious to im-
press spinster Sutherland with their alleged
superior instinct for nursery economy. “I’ve had
a husband, and brought up three children,
you know, Miss Sutherland,” purred one tabby,
complacently. “I’ve had no husband, and I’ve
brought up 3000 bairns; what d’ye think o’ that ? ”
was the Hielan’ woman’s spirited rejoinder.
From “Tryphena,” in London :
The junket starts to weary one. There’s such a mono-
tony about the magnificent spread, and the ducal host or
the honorable hostess is only the same common or garden
man or woman, plus a label. The Marchioness isn’t any
different from the rich Mrs. If at all different, it’s to the
advantage of the Mrs. The Marchioness is often a per-
sonage of late hours, of a rackety-gorgeous life, of acute
nerves and frayed digestion, of a w rinkled cheek and a
feverish heart. In the morning, Time taunts her,
and the mirror calls out on her haggardness. At
night, the Graces cluster round, and do her homage at
the toilette table. By the time she is upholstered and
completed she is a picture (a sort of reverend, beauti-
ful Ruin), and she goes forth like an academy gem, to find
her place in the line of distinction. The plainly labelled
Mrs. has started to racket later in life, and so has a
healthy youth to back her middle-aged dissipation. In-
variably the beauty of any of these crushes is the Mrs., and
the gorgeous old painted Ruin is the Marchioness.
In the frilly legion aswarm at the Brewery Tooths’
shivoo the other night, at the most costly hotel here-
abouts, I caught a glimpse of the Rupertswood grass-
widow. Mayfair has added a whole heap of arti-
ficiality to the appearance, if not the personality,
of the lady. All the same, the intelligent beauty
makes good in any company. At the Tooths’ she had
garbed her elegance in ivory chiffons, and illuminated
herself with a dizzy array of diamonds. In her chocolate
tresses, among the alluring crimps and ripples, the stones
flashed with any amount of bold splendor. She has her
place fixed in this cosmopolitan Vanity Fair, and it’s a
high place. An exalted English official as much as said
that the lady had achieved in a few Mayfair seasons what
some Yank sausage-merchants and soup-kings had taken
a gross of years to accomplish. Personality, the clothes
art, and the delicious tiny doll house of a mansion in
Park-lane have worked the trick.
The Osterley Park show of the Jerseys drew the Aus-
tralians together in strong force the other Saturday.
And the rain, which raineth every day in Merrie
England, actually held off. I saw the Josiah Symons
bunched together in a dull trio. The Adelaide Marm is
highly intelligent, but her style is that of a very modest,
hard-working housekeeper, who wants only to look tidy
and talk horse-sense to politicians. Miss Symon is a chip
of the maternal block, with this difference—that she
doesn’t want to talk. Premier Bent waddled about the
historic green lawns of Osterley with a fat-handled brolly
in his fist. Tummy has a way of waddling at scrambles
of this sort that is droller than Harry Lauder’s
screaming Tivoli turn. Even Oom Bill Lyne gropes into
position with more grace, with some knowledge, and with
a sullen determination to “ arrive.”
The Agents-General and their wives are getting no end
of a good time. They do all the year round. The
Taverners are the sleekest and smuggest of the lot.
I rushed to see how the Australians fared at the 1907
Academy. I was disappointed. There’s only one of
Longstaff’s, and this is a mere color-effect in clothes,
though it’s called “ A Woman’s Portrait.” The Countess
of Eldon is the person in pigment, and whether her
severe nobleness or her dress burden (leagues of velvet
trail about and around her) is answerable for the wooden-
ness of the pose, it’s impossible to say. But it is wooden.
I liked immensely a bit of life and color in a Simon
Vedder picture—“ Girl with a Mandolin.” The Venetian-
like hussy looks out from the canvas with mocking eyes
and parted lips on which the words of impudence still
hang. The whole has feeling, character and the life glow.
It is real Girl—not Pigment.
Percy Spence has a drab, dull, dreary gentleman sub-
ject for his one Academy laurel. It’s the Admiral of the
Fleet—Sir E. H. Seymour. The clever Percy has curiously
chosen a drab background for his drab Admiral, and the
cleverness of its treatment (it is clever) is discounted.
To Bertram Mackennal the largest Australian praise is
due. His three exhibits in the Sculpture division are
examples of his improving art. One—memorial to Peter
Nicol Russell—is a colossal work of fine skill and excep-
tional feeling. In a statue dubbed “ Diana Wounded,”
the Australian has effected another fine bit of work. It’s
a delicate but beautiful goddess, with a cut limb, which
she bandages with slow care—her whole bending body as
she does so expresses shrinking pain. It’s a graceful idyll
sn marble. A third exhibit reaches the idealistic stage,
jt’s a large clump of marble, with four female figures
chiselled at close intervals round about it —or, rather, in
it. The title, “ Earth and the Elements,” leaves one still
guessing.
The canvas round which Mayfair crushes closest, and
the critics gnaw their moustaches hardest, and the casual
stranger gasps longest, is the sen-ational work of F.
Cadogan Cowper. It took me two-and-a-half-hours to
squeeze a way in through the first day’s damp crush.
The wait wasn’t wasted. It is a bold dramatic shock in a
Gallery where the landscape stretches, tiresomely green,
and the “ Portrait of Milady ” wearies with its magnifi-
cent dulness. In a convent cellar (it’s little better), Satan,
disguised as a vagrant troubadour, having been fed by
some charitable nuns, sings them a song of love. Beneath
a gloriously-illustrated stained-glass window the dingy
devil, with troubadour trappings and his strong, evil face
in profile, twangs and tootles passionately to the sister-
hood, who, their meal finished, are sitting around on their
plain wooden benches, listening. The groupingis a, delight-
ful effect. They cluster into a small space, turning their
faces full on the warbler. And every face is alive
with feeling. On some, disgust and white fear have twisted
the features all awry. On others, a dawning light of
wonder breaks. On" some, a dumb prayer is written
deep. On the younger, a new mad joy leaps and laughs,
dimpling the rounded faces. One or two black figures
crouch, faces hidden, on the ground. The whole canvas
is alive.
The social sensation of the present Anglo-Australian
tour is the arrival of Mrs. Luke, of vigneron fame, and
her sudden engagement as Miss D’Arcy Lane, on the
Gaiety boards." The beauteous Melbournian has no
knowledge of stage life and no singing voice, to my
knowledge. She may have acquired one in the Medi-
terranean, where most other feminines acquire husbands
or evil reputations. You pick up all sorts of things on a
sea voyage. It says much for Mrs. Luke’s looks that she
was only a week in this over-burdened hub of idle theat-
ricals when she was booked.

That versatile actor-man, Gaston Mervale,
appears in some new rdles shortly. He joins one
of Meynell and Gunn’s new companies, to produce
their plays, stage-manage, and play the heavy
vil-lain (ha-ha !) into the bargain. The public is
bidden to sit up straight and pay attention to his
efforts. We are to have a taste of the great
Belasco’s methods of play-direction, mixed with
Mervale’s own ideas, and memories of his classy
French training. The ingenuous girls who know
h im slightly, and who suffer from stage-mania, pur-
pose rushing him for engagements. They maintain
that he’s really quite a tame and gentle animal, in
spite of his facial suggestion of a Corsican brother,
or someone of that sort.
A forthcoming benefit concert at Melbourne
Town Hall is to have the patronage of all the
resident aristocracy of music. The heroine of the
occasion will be Miss Caroline Rudd, a young
pianiste who represents the teaching of Mrs.
Charles Heath and Marshall Hall’s Conserva-
torium. Her concert programme is Hall-marked
on every link. The Conservatorium orchestra
will be there, likewise Miss Elva Rogers, Miss
Harrington, young Amadio the flautist, and
Bertie Zelman, who does Australia proud on the
violin. As for the list of patrons, it is a guarantee
of a large and toney audience.
The Norman Menzies have gone to live in
Alma-road, East St. Kilda. Mr. M. has bought
the house owned by the late Alfred Joseph, and
called by him “ Sandhurst,” after the city which,
for a time, had the old name of Bendigo dragged

Tittell Brune, tragedienne, leaves us in Sep-
tember to recuperate her art and search for flesh.
The managerial trio got the lady to tour “smalls”

A lace-worker of much art, Mrs. Bolton
Houghton, gathers up some of her masterpieces,
and shows them at the Women’s Exhibition.
Mrs. Houghton is the most successful of local
amateur exponents of Irish crochet. The lay
woman, whose knowledge of lace-making is
limited to seeing a thready dream grow under
clever fingers, is completely staggered at such
infinite application and intricate art as crochet
demands. Mrs. Houghton is the proud possessor
of draperies whose patterns have been copied
from historic vestments, and most enviable
extravagances in the shape of boleros, scarves,
and all the other ecstatic details that make a frock
worth wearing.
The Corot which the National Gallery has lately
acquired for the amount of a small fortune is a
privileged possession we may well be proud of.
The subject, “ The Bent Tree,” offered the master
inspiration for a lyric in pigments. The quiet
scene which flows into a far horizon, the mass of
foliage, and the one scarred leafless tree which
bends across the canvas, say the last word for im-
pressionistic landscape. There is an atmosphere
of reality about all, yet a suggestion of charm and
mystery emanates from the picture which en-
thralls. Curiously enough, while connoisseurs
inveigh against its purchase, and deprecate its
beauties, that section of the community which
calls it “a Cor-rott,” appreciates it more justly.
Members of that section arrive at the gallery in
battalions, and say “Ain’t it lovely !” in chorus.
The hour of cut prices and bargain hats having
arrived, I and another cat went forth to spend
more than either of us could afford, and bring
home some prizes for our vanity. She said:
“Really, dear, sales are worth attending, even if
you don’t buy anything. I find that they’re more
reducing than Turkish baths.” That was an irre-
sistible bait. I wanted a hat—a becoming, unique,
and inexpensive lid, which would take me any-
where. At the first shop we went to, I found it.
It was a seductive concoction of fruit, flowers,
feathers, and felt, marked half-price, and, with it
on, I felt just It. The other cat saw that this
was so, and said: “My dear! You wouldn’t be
foolish enough to buy a thing like that! Why, it
makes you look positively vulgar.” I was dissuaded
by her deceit, and let it go. She paid a deposit
on it herself, while I was in the underclothing
department. Then I saw the whole truth. That
hat must have suited me exquisitely. She per-
suaded me to buy a coat suitable to a decrepit old
maid, and she changed a blouse-length of merce-
rised silk with me for a sequined scarf. I bought
335. worth of nothing in particular, and never met a
single hat under three guineas, barring the one
already mentioned, that wouldn’t shame me in
the wearing. I’ve quarrelled with her, and the
mercerised blouse-length won’t wash. All my
available cash has wasted away, and a woman
pulled two tail» off my muff and.spoilt it. Moral:
Don’t go with another cat to bargain sales. Go
by yourself—by your own cat, so to speak.
A small but “ correct ” audience turned up for
Albani. Just the sort one would expect from
adorers of everything “beloved by the Queen.”
The company includes good talent, but has? hope-
lessly old-fashioned provincial English atmosj here.
Albani is a Reputation. She clings to the .'ear
old fashion of running on and off the platform, end
repeats the ultra-dramatic gestures which she
learned of yore in the Italian opera school. She
is dressed just as beautifully and correctly as a
fine French doll. A dapper little tenor, with a

contralto help things along; but the life of the
programme are violinist Haydn Wood, and festive
little Australienne Myrtle Meggy (Meg Myrtle
would suit her piquante style), who plays the
piano prettily enough.

United Service Magazine (Eng.) on Im-
perial naval defence: —
The colonial contributions to the Imperial Navy are at
once too little and too much. Too little because they do
not amount to the cost of a single battleship; and too
much because they really constitute a contradiction of
the cherished British principle that taxation and repre-
sentation should go hand-in-hand. We must trust our
colonies wholly if we desire reciprocal treatment, and
every Briton knows that we can trust them. The prin-
ciple of assisting by contributions alone is out of date
and vicious.
There is more than one way to cure a
cough, but the best way is by means of Angier’s
Emulsion, because it not only soothes the lungs
and cures the cough, but also improves digestion,
and acts as a soothing tonic to the entire system.
In three sizes, of all chemists.*
Waiters at the Gate.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We are waiting on the strand of our undefended
land,
Just a nation of Micawbkrs, young and old ;
Man and ,woman, girl and boy. expectation is our
joy,
We are quite unique, a wonder to behold
As we watch, with folded hands, from these
silver-crested sands,
For an imminent millennium, maybe,
While the Fates, untiring, spin weft and warp to
wrap us in—
- Webs, perhaps, to prison folk who will not see.
Dilatory dreamers all, deaf to threat or duty’s
call,
Hugging sundry cherished fetishes to heart,
As all-placidly we purr, “ Surely something must
occur
To make needless any effort on our part.
Heaven’s help our course shall guide, with the
Dear Old Land beside,”
Quite forgetting, as assuredly we do,
Heaven’s aid is for the man who his own career
.can plan,
And then strives amain to put that programme
through.
Here’s a country with no fleet, and an “army”
that, if neat
In its serviceable trappings on parade,
Is so very sparse and small that it scarelv weighs
at all
As a counter when the game of war is played.
And its rifles are but few, and are nearly worn-
out, too,
While it will not make munitions for itself —-
Local powder, shell and shot would, it grumbles,
“ Cost a lot ”
Holding safety aye subordinate to pelf.
But, in consequence, the fate of this white man’s
ultimate
And non-garrisoned, lone outpost seems to shake
In a balance with a slope to a quarter where
there’s scope
For developments to make the boldest quake.
Still, unheedful of the gibes of the rampant
colored tribes
That are threatening to close us in their ring.
We keep looking very sage, while around the
heathen rage,
And remark that “They imagine a vain
thing.”
So we sit and meditate at the Yellow Peril’s Gate,
Self-disparaged, and impervious to shame,
And one hardly likes to say what may happen on
the day
When the smoke beyond us kindles into flame.
Shall we fists and feet oppose to the lethal-
weaponed foes
Whose encroachment we should any time
expect ?
When their guns in battle bang, can we throw
the boomerang?
Or the waddy wield with conquering effect ?
From a vulnerable shore, as our fathers did
before —
Scarce a whit more self-reliant than were they—
We are looking over sea, through the mists of
Yet-To-Be,
For protection by a people far away.
Dwellers in a distant land, who, beset on either
hand
With impediments and problems of their own,
May not over-keenly care in the hour of our
despair
How a parasite's redemption they postpone.
N.S.W.
TALAFEKAU.
J- Y. Chataway, the Queensland politician,
whom the Argus acknowledged as the author of
its articles on the Northern Territory, while the
S.M. Herald printed them as from its own
“ special commissioner,” ground his little axe in
the last of the series. He denounced the pro-
posed transfer of the Territory as “a bad bar-
gain,” because S. Australia stipulates that the
transcontinental line shall be finished. Chataway,
as a man whose billet depends on Queensland
votes, wants the line to wander across to Camoo-
vveal, and then go on wandering until it strikes a
line that wanders out from Cairns. What parti-
cular good to Australia a line would be that
wandered between Port Darwin and Cairns,
Heaven only knows. What there would be for it
to carry even Heaven doesn’t know. The rail
that links Adelaide, Melbourne, Sydney and
Brisbane runs up only as far north as Rockhamp-
ton, and after you crawl out of Rockhampton you
are three days’ steam before you can crawl along-
side the Cairns mudbank. For a “ Defence” line
Chataway’s isn’t a route ; it’s a joke.
+ ; ■
The lords of the Western District of Victoria,
though they fence out the settler, don’t counter-
balance matters to even a small extent by in-
creasing the capacity of the country to carry
sheep. In Follett county, of which only one acre
in every 130 is cultivated, the number of sheep is
actually dwindling; and in the other western
counties the growth is trifling. Yic. Government
.Statist Drake’s 1907 figures show that the big
increases have been in the Mallee and Wimmera
—the two districts in which agricultural settle-
ment has also been largest. So the returns prove
over again that you don’t crowd out sheep by
crowding on men, but the very opposite. If the
Western District squatter held back settlement by
growing more sheep (the jumbuck is a useful
animal, although it hasn’t shown any disposition
so far to bite an invader), it would be something
in his favor; but the only thing he really does
grow is more intolerable.
“0.K.”: Australian cigar manufacturers, as is
now officially remarked for the first time, are even
less true to themselves and their own products than
the hatters, bootmakers, etc. The hatters and
bootmakers merely refrain from stamping their
goods with an unmistakable Australian label or
trade-mark. Purchasers are tacitly invited to
accept the local for an imported article,if they beof
the Stinking Fish persuasion. But there is not, as a
rule, any deliberate pretence of foreign manufac-
ture in the branding of the local hat or boot.
The Australian-made cigar, on the other hand, is
marked under strictly fraudulent conditions. The
labels and bands are made in Germany, and the
language of their adoption is Spanish. Seemingly
it has just occurred to the Acting Minister of
Customs, that the Tariff’s encouragement of cigar
manufacturing in Australia was not intended to
encourage the importation of mock Spanish
printing from Germany.
“Dico” writes from the spot :
I can quite agree with “ Wusslean ” (B. 2/5/’O7) in re-
gard to the invisible townships in Victoria’s Western
District. I had the miserable misfortune to get a shift
to one of these places about six months ago; and have
ever since been looking for the village. So far, I have
unearthed a school (held in a rickety church building),
and two or three isolated and bumble-looking dwellings.
The rest, as far as regards signs of human habitation, is
as Pompeii was after Vesuvius had done throwing
muck about. There is not even a store or post-
office, and the nearest pub is 22 miles off. Yet
the place is marked on all the maps as a town-
ship, and the squire-worshipping inhabitants tell me that
it was surveyed and sold in I-acre blocks about 30 years
ago. It is a splendid site for a town, well-drained, and
right on the shores of Victoria’s inland sea, Corangamite,
I have never struck a healthier spot. The salt air would
make a confirmed dyspeptic long for pork and cabbage,
and the soil and rainfall are quite as good as in the famous
Lancefield district. The numerous lakes around (both
salt ami fresh) are teeming with all kinds of wild ducks
and geese.
But, alas! the squatter-octopus spreads his loving
arms over the surrounding country for leagues. From
here to Camperdown (22 miles) the traveller passes but
two dwellings—a boundary-rider’s hut and a farm-house.
And it’s just as bad in all other directions.
I’ve looked at the question from all points of view,
and there’s only one practical solution—compulsion by
means of a Federal graduated land-tax; and if that
doesn’t come, and soon, then Goo help Australia !
A slight digression to wind up. Next to me dwells an
old man who, for the last 28 years, has been employed on
a neighboring station. Lately he has been labbit-ex-
terminator at 17s. (id. a week, finding his own horse, cart,
dogs, ferrets, &c. Well, yesterday the kind squire rode
up to him in the paddock where he vvas working, and
bluntly told him to go, as lie was getting too old. I just
relate that because we hear so much of the generosity,
charity, and g-eneral kindliness of land-snatchers. Isn’t
it amazing! Everyone can plainly see the disastrous
effects of squatter-occupation, everyone hears the loud
howl for land, and should clearly understand by this time
the utter futility of voluntary sale ; yet McDougall,
M.11.R., couldn’t get a seconder for his'motion in favor
of compulsory purchase at a Progress (O, Lord !) Associ-
ation meeting. Mr. McD. had better get away from that
kind of Ass.
Mining in Queensland used to be a deadly busi-
ness ; but less casualness, or more State inspection,
or the introduction of harder planks at the gaols
THE SACREDNESS OF AGE.
DAIRY INSPECTOR (to cocky): “ Your premises are very dirty , I'm sorry to say.”
COCKY: Oh, that's old dirt. It was there afore the Act come in."
which yawn for over-careless managers, or some-
thing, has diminished the tombstone-maker’s little
jobs. From 1878 to 1883 one miner out of everv
267 employed was killed every year; so, before
those four years were over, one out of every 67
was a corpse in a grave. Nowadays the annual
harvest is about one in 550 ; last year things were
good, and there was only one in 739. But even
that figure isn’t as healthy as the record in
N.S. Wales, where it took 943 miners to provide
one inquest last year. This is in metal mines
only. The coal-miner takes worse chances ;
on an average for 20 years, one man in 420 fails to
dodge death in each 12 months. But as death is
liable to come into the coal mines in large lumps,
the figures are erratic ; in 1902. for example, every
125th man in N.S.W. was sent for. When the
coal-miner has a strike, and won’t give in, and
pretty often won’t listen to reason in the end, the
shivering public that didn’t get in a stock of fuel
before the agents ran up the price says that the
coal-miner is an obstinate animal. And when one
looks at figures like the above, it seems a mighty
good thing that he is an obstinate animal. If he
wasn’t, he might be scared right out of the busi-
ness, and then the public would do a lot of
shivering.
The Miners’ Association of "Victoria needs shaking
up. Local conditions of employment are, in many
cases, scandalous. Yet the organisation offers
next to no protection, and possesses about as much
political force as a penny bun. Only about 6000
out of 30,000 odd persons engaged in the mining
industry appear on the rolls of the association,
and these 6000 make no attractive bid to the odd
24,000 who should come in. Things being so, Greed
and Co. can afford to refuse to confer with the
A.M.A. on such life-and-death questions as reason-
able hours of toil in hot workings. The Ballarat
Mine-Owners’ Association has stonily refused to
consider anything proposed for the betterment of
miners’ conditions, which, perhaps, was only to be
expected when starving tributors jump at a
chance of getting ss. a “shift. Also, the Mine-
owners’ attitude is the result of the compliance
shown by certain officials of the A.M.A. to the
wishes of Greed and Co. that there should be no
affiliation with the Trades Hall. Nobody believes
in the yarn about the hardships borne by the call-
payer in the interests of suffering humanity ; for
the call-payer always expects to get his money
back with interest added, and call-payers as a
body are above the suspicion of doing good by
stealth. The A.M.A. wants rejuvenating, and if
there are any brains in the outfit they should
adopt a forward policy of organisation, scoop in
the ‘‘poor tributor,” and make the A.M.A. a
power. Then it could begin to talk back to
Greed and Co.
-
That charming person, the big landlord, who
loves all mankind as a brother, is aghast at the
vile person who wants to break up estates and
settle people in farms and homes of their own,
and turn out the sheep that are on the land now.
A. Beviss, of Dorset Park (S. Aus.), is the
charming person in this case. Says he to the
Register :
Mr. Price told the electors at Gumeracha that we
wanted more men and less sheep—in other words, he
told them they were not paying enough for their meat.
Fewer sheep mean 7d. per lb. for mutton,
ihe thoughtfulness of the landlord for the poor—
he just keeps sheep where men ought to be so that
meat may be cheap for them ! He doesn’t explain
that meat at 7d. would be cheaper to a man who
was earning Bd. than meat at sd. is to a man who
is blocked from earning 4d. Also, the kind land-
lord makes a mistake about men driving off
sheep. Close settlement breeds sheep as well as
men. In Maoriland, in 1880, they only had
15,174,203 sheep. At the end of 1905 they had
19,130,875, notwithstanding that, in the previous
five years alone, 29,951,390 carcases and sheep were
exported. In other words, after 14 years of close
settlement, Maoriland was, in the next five years,
able to export just upon twice as many sheep and
lambs as it began with, and still have larger flocks
than it had when closer settlement started. Also,
in Victoria, as the Government Statist lately
pointed out, the increase in flocks is taking place
only in the districts where small settlement's in
progress. Where the big-landlord curse sits, even
sheep-breeding stagnates.
“ Mallet ” writes from Westralia
I don’t want to be classed as one of the Stinking- Fish
pa,rty, but I don’t believe in bringing people here under
false pretences. The fact is that in very few places are
blocks of good land of any size to be found at all, the
country being patchy, and intermixed with scrub land
and sand plains. Bui where good consistent ground is
to be found it is all taken up, mostly by the land-
grabbers who are keeping the country back.
The best agricultural land is found in the Great Southern
and South-Western Districts. The country around
Northam, Meckering and Grass Valley (absolutely the
pick of the lot) is practically cut up between Tiirossell
and the Dempsters. From York to Beverley we have
Monger ; from Wagin downwards there are the Piesses ;
in the Williams is Hammersley ; in Bunbury, Forrest.
So that if one wants land, he has to go out from 30 to 50
miles from the line to find it.
The “ Selectors’Guide,” issued by the Government, is
particularly misleading. All the districts from Narrogin
to Albany are described in it as having “good roads,”
when, as a matter of fact, with the exception of the old
convict-made Perth-Albany highway, there are no roads
at all. They are merely tracks. And the published
reports re wheat yields are quite misleading.
I should like to see this country opened up and go
ahead, but that will not be, seemingly, until we stop
electing Daglishes, Rasons, and Newton Moores, and
until we get the Federal graduated land-tax. When that
time arrives, we can honestly invite outsiders to come and
settle on the land, but until then there are plenty of
unfortunates here waiting for the bit of soil they
can’t get.
4.
Agriculture is now a science subject in 40
Victorian State schools. The infant first takes
nature study ; when he reaches the third class he
can specialise agriculture till he is 14 ; then he
can take a course at an Agricultural High School;
after that he can go to the University and take a
degree in Agricultural Science. Subsequently, he
can take a microscope and look for a land-tax that
would provide him with the land, without which
all his school-learning is valueless; and when he
hasn’t.found it he can finally take his swag and
streak for Queensland.

Chest troubles and ills,
Keep down doctor’s bills—
Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*

communications insufficiently stamped. Name and

returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

diate relief. I enjoy very

children when they require them, and I think
that is what keeps them in such good health. 1
can speak highly of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root

to others.”

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Ikey Moes of the Victorian Club, Melbou rue,
are having another turmoil over the handicap for their
annual billiard tournament. Some years ago they
objected to one of the entrants, on the ground that he
had deceived the handicapper as to his “ form,” but he
braved the storm and ultimately got away with the first
prize under amicable conditions. It seems that he had
not been playing “schlenter” games. He had merely
concealed his want of practice until the weights were out,
after which he recovered his lost “ form ” with all the
enthusiasm of a bookmaker’s racehorse.. This year the
too-wel)-treated amateur was a Mr. Von der Luft, handi-
capped to receive 100 points in the 250 up. The subsequent
discovery that Luft is a good player “ when he gives his
mind to it,” caused great excitement in the betting club.
Melbourne Age treated it as a matter of vast importance,
and threw out the suggestion of Luft’s retirement from
the tournament. Then he was loudly commended for
having consented to scratch his name and make things
pleasant. But Luft didn’t retire. He hung on to his
rights. So, last week, the committee decided to get rid
of Luft by cancelling the billiard tournament, prepara-
tory to taking nominations for another one. If the luck-
less stranger enters himself for this new tournament, he
will presumably be placed at about 500 behind “ scratch.”
Had the first handicap been allowed to hold good, what a
nice gentlemanly time would the “books” have given
Luft 1
* * * *
With the local billiard world agitated by
thoughts of tournaments, and every entrant dreaming of
a sudden reformation in his play and a new knowledge of
the game, Alcock & Co. get crowded houses for the
Inman-Lindrum game at their Melbourne Billiard Hall,
or “Parlor,” as it is sometimes called. The “Parlor”
holds not more than 150 people comfortably, and if the
rush for object-lessons goes on increasing in volume it
will need a lot more accommodation. Amateurs in ordin-
ary, judging from thei'r unaccountable applauding of cer-
tain shots, and their non-applauding of others, get more
entertainment than instruction from a fine display of pro-
fessional billiards. The accuracy of Inman’s losing-hazard
striking, when he made, say, a break of 100 or 150 off the
red ball after “ potting ” the white, was one of those
points of excellence that everybody could appreciate, but
the unobtrusive cleverness of his play for “ position ”
seemed to be accepted as a matter of course—an every-
day affair—like his easy method of making occasional
plain shots under difficult conditions. Both Inman, from
England, and Lindrum, the Australian lad, are solid
rather than delicately artistic in their billiards. The
man, of course, has more solidity than the less experi-
enced boy, but even in watching the youngster,
an amateur student of the game is struck by
the fact that the art of playing for position after
the stroke is not worth much to a third-rate
cueist. Lindrum, in his teens, is always making
individual strokes which the average matured amateur
player would fail to make twice out of three attempts.
This is more noticeable in the free, all-round play of In-
man and Lindrum than in the big “ break ” manipula-
tions of a Roberts and a Stevenson, who keep in closer
touch with the balls. Last week, when Lindrum scored
100 odd, or Inman 200 odd, in one visit to the table, either
of them woukj make during the innings five or six, or
eight or ten, shots which no fairly capable amateur is
certain of making. The odds against his making
all of them in succession would be fully. 100 to
1. A respectable amateur may know how to do
it, but he is unable to make a certainty of it.
Many lovers of the game remain blind to this simple fact
whilst they are watching a pair of professors at work.
They are more impressed by the judgment shown in the
“ strength ” and tactics of the performer than by the
deadly precision of his aim. And if the professor hap-
pens to be slightly inaccurate in a shot which would be
somewhat difficult to them, they say that he “ broke
down at an easy one.” Moreover they attach no particu-
lar importance to his ambidexterity, which enables him
to make easily with his left hand a shot that puts an
amateur to the inconvenient necessity of using the “rest.”
Inman has the billiard face so strongly typed
that one might guess his business from merely looking at
him as a stranger. Bluff and hearty John Roberts is an
exception, but the rule with the faces of these billiardists
is a cold placidity of expression—an acquired calmness —
a meek, hopeful stoicism, and the long collateral lines in
the forehead when the player is sighting the ball. Roberts
has them almost as markedly as Inman. Boy Lindrum
will get them anon. Seemingly this expression of sur-
prise at the job in hand is the result of a natural lifting
of the eyelids, or removal of the shade from the sight.
* * * *
The weights are out for the Y.R.C. Grand
Nationals, and punters are looking for another Realm to
swear by. Among the light-weights in the Hurdle Race
there is nothing that looks likely to prove so absolutely
“ good ”as Realm last year, or York in 1905. But within
the next fortnight one of the lot handicapped under the
10.7 mark may burst forth as a shining light. Meanwhile,
Lion Heart (9.6) has a promising appearance, and Devonier
(11.4) and Prestonpans (10.12) are better liked in Mel-
bourne than Capulet, the Maorilander, whose reputation
is based on private reports. Among the ’chasers the
handsome Napier is worth considering, in spite of his
12’2 burden.
* * * *
In the three days of Adelaide Racing Club’s
Birthday Cup meeting only £29,000 went through the
totalisator. (This is about the level of recent years, but
in 1899 nearly £47,000 was taken.) The promoting club
gets £2200 of this as commission, and the State Govern-
ment about £730 as tax. The last day’s racing showed a
record dividend, in a small race with a field of 15. The
winner paid £ll 18s. for a pound ticket, and the second
horse (on which only £4 ss. was invested) paid £54 9s. !
* * ¥ *
Grafton (N.S.W.) Argus, referring to Killara’s
recent win of a welter handicap in the Clarence River’s
big town, states that, according to the owner, the only
preparation undergone by Killara was bringing in cows,
and that he was ridden from Nymboida (26 miles away)
on the morning of the race, and home again same day.
Our authority adds “ That Killara was totally devoid of
condition was palp .ble to everyone who saw the horse on
the course.” The Bulletin rises to say there couldn’t
have been much lack of condition about a nag that sand-
wiched the winning of a race between two journeys of 26
miles each in one day—s 2 miles in all; also this journal
wouldn’t like, for the sake of the district’s horseracing
reputation, to even hint at what it thinks of the quality
of the opposing cattle, and the condition they were in,
unless Killara is an absolute marvel.
* * * *
The prosperous Sydney butcher, Mr. L. Uhde,
who died last week, once ran The Grand (Wynyard
Square), and sundry other Sydney pubs, and ran them all
profitably. Over 40 years ago he was a prominent turf
patron, but latterly didn’t follow racing so closely,
though he had “an interest in something ” occasionally.
Thirty-eight years back Mr. Uhde’s Warrior, after
running third in the A.J.C. Metrop., and unplaced in
Sydney and Forced Handicaps, was sold to late Mr.
Saqui, and same year (1869) won Essendon Stakes, Mel-
bourne Cup, and followed with Sandhurst Cup. Though
’twas hard luck to have parted with such a nag just then,
the animal had not proved a bad spec, for his prior
owner, who won some good races with him.
The formidable list of A.J.C. whitewashed
pony trainers, jockeys and such, published a week or turn
since, made it appear that ponydom would be completely
absorbed and live only in history ; but apparently this is
not to be. The A.J.C. committee met last w'eek to con-
sider applications for licenses ; result, certificates issued
to, perhaps, eight trainers and a similar number of
riders. If the pony clubs hit back at the would-be
seceders, the ranks of Sydney’s unemployed will be
largely swelled.
* * # *
The absence of Orcus, the yearling brother of
the mighty Poseidon, from the entries for the liberally-
endowed A.J.C. Breeders’ Plate and Gimcrack Stakes has
been commented upon and marvelled at, in view of the
fact that he cost 3050 guineas—a record figure for a
youngster on this continent. Possibly that astute
trainer, Ike Earnshaw, has advised that it is best not to
get the colt ready for spring meetings. Especially does
it appear so, as Orcus has been nominated for A.J.C.
December Stakes.
■*■*•>.*
The A.J.C. committee, which is always being
appealed to by infuriated denizens of N.S.W. to do some-
thing in the way of challenging the supremacy of the
Melbourne Cup as the race of Australia, has not yet
responded to the appeal. But it has tackled the Derby,
and has added £2OOO for the 1908 event, making it the
richest “ classic ” stake in the Commonwealth. For next
year’s race 201 entries have been received, which is
claimed to be an Australian record.
* * * »
Some more pleasing dividends paid by the
totalisator : £54 9s. on Queen’s Cup in the Disposal Stakes
at Adelaide, Saturday week ; £l6 7s. on Metal Queen in
Port Adelaide Cup, Saturday ; £9 10s. on Lantier in the
Handicap Steeple at Port Adelaide, Saturday ; £l5 18s.
on Montgolfier in Croya Handicap, Kalgoorlie, Saturday ;
£26 16s. on Bi-color in the Farewell Handicap, Kalgoorlie.
Saturday. Of course bookmakers lay odds as big as these
sometimes, but the horses that they lay such odds upon
never win in all the creation of cats.
* * * *
Broken Hill Licensed Victuallers’ Races were a
beautiful epitome of the horse-racinggame generally. In
the two miles Hurdles two animals fell, and Snablets
broke his own neck, and so badly injured his jockey
that the boy had to be taken to the hospital. The Ladies’
Bracelet provided some excitement. Zamiel and Dignum
dead-heated, and when the stewards came to look into the
matter they disqualified Dignum, and took measures for
the arrest of the trainer and owner on a charge of ringing
in and attempting to defraud the club. Dignum was
alleged to be the horse Savoy, disqualified some time ago
for malpractice at Orange (N.S.W.).
* * # IT
The constant dropping of ink begins to wear
away the stony hearts of the racing clubs, and the
y.R.C. has taken some steps to reduce the risk of sudden
death to men and horses at forthcoming Grand Nationals.
The top rails of the fences will be replaced with brush,
and the logs and stone walls reduced in height. This
should have an appreciable effect on the butcher’s bill.
Of course, the change was vigorously opposed by the
“old hands,” who argued that it meant the ruin of the
sport. But the world begins to understand the “old
hand,” and to brush him aside with the contempt he
deserves.
* » * *
N.S.W. Bowling Clubs are going to keep the
memory of John Young, the father of the game, green in
the memories of future generations by placing enlarged
portraits of the old gentleman in the club houses. A fine
shield, which will bear his effigy, is to be made and com-
peted for in the opening matches of the season. The
chief topic in bowling circles just now, however, is the
question of “green fees” and their abolition. It has
been the custom and unwritten law of the game for each
member of a visiting team to hand to the caretaker a
humble bob. A number of bowlers desire to see the
practice abolished, but there is a lot to be said for it. It
has been a valuable source of income to the smaller
and less financial clubs, and has done a lot to bring into
being many of the fine greens scattered around Sydney,
The argument against it is that it makes bowling expen-
sive to men of small means, and to writer’s mind this is a
fallacy. The visiting bowler escapes with an expenditure
of a couple of shillings for the afternoon, even after
paying his tram fare. If he plays on his own
green, it is almost certain to cost him nearer ss.
he must help to entertain the visitors by supplying liquid
refreshment to keep them and himself up during the
heat and burden of the game. Altogether, it seems a
very small item to have caused such a large discussion.
The Association selection committee has decided that the
matches between the N.S.W. and Northern Districts
(Newcastle) Associations shall take place at Newcastle on
June 8, and Redfern Green on June 26, and exciting
contests are looked forward to.
* * * *
The Melbourne glove battle, Sam Grey versus
Lyn Truscott, was another demonstration of the fact
that the referee often takes a lot of beating. Lyn Trus-
cott, as a boxer, is not in the same paddock as the
Victorian veteran, who can put up a spar second to none
on this continent; and yet, after the scrap had gone 20
rounds, and Samuel had put it all over Truscott with
both hands, taking in exchange a casual left hand body
punch and one or two pokey straight left leads, the
referee, Sam Gosson, promptly specified that Truscott
was the winner, and then got out ahead of a storm of
hooting that signified the very righteous disapproval felt
by the mass. Oosson, as a rule, manages a fight admir-
ably, his one fault being a weakness to get between when
interference is not called for; but this decision will
damn his reputation for judgment in the estimation of
the pug-loving public for many a cold night.
•***■*
N.S.W. Cricket Association, on Monday night
last, lifted the disqualifications from the rebellious
players. They will now be eligible to hold office
(if any club is foolish enough to elect them) from July.
In the meantime, a new tiger has appeared in the brambly
path of the Board of Control. Saddled with a £lO,OOO
guarantee, the Board has to keep a sharp eye on the
finances. The Sydney Cricket Ground trustees have
demanded 20 per cent, of the gross takings, in addition
to making the Board pay all ground expenses, which
usually run to 10 per cent. As the Marylebone Cricket
Club has to receive 50 per cent, of the proceeds, this
leaves the Board a mighty narrow margin. The im-
pudence of this demand will be fully appreciated when
it is remembered that the Board will not receive
a penny on account of the thousands who will watch the
matches from the members’ stands; while the trustees
are certain to receive the benefit of a large increase in
the number of annual ticket-holders. This means that,
in addition to having half the accommodation for their
own members orticket-holders, the trustees will take 20 per
cent, of the revenue of what space is left to the Board.
(The Melbourne Cricket Club is charging only 15 per
cent, for the use of its ground.) The situation now is that
the Board will have to reconsider the whole question of
bringing out the English team. It will first ask the
various State associations to indemnify it against loss.
As the guarantee will be a personal one, the various asso-
ciations will probably be dubious about giving it. And
if they don’t, there will be no visit of the English Team.
It was this complication, no doubt, that influenced the
N.S.W. Association to lift the disqualifications from the
players, so as to enable the Board to throw the whole
onus of the impending catastrophe upon the extortionate
Trustees of the Sydney Cricket Ground.
* ’* « *
The campaign of the players for separate repre-
sentation on the Board of Control is meeting with scanty
support. An attempt to gain signatures in support of
the application has failed miserably. The Associations of
Queensland, N.S.W., and Victoria, and also the Mel-
bourne Cricket Club, are reported as wholly opposed to
the idea. The name of the players is Mud again, after
having been several times previously Mud.

Died in Melbourne about 2 o’clock Saturday
morning the one-time mighty cricketer, R. W. M‘Leod.
He was one of the committee of the Melbourne Cricket
Club, and represented that concern on the Victorian
Association. He represented the Melbourne Football
Club on the V.F. League, and loomed large in the mining
world. He was a cricketer from his youth upwards, and
in the heyday of his career he knocked up 300 for M.O.C.
against Essendon. He also bowled and batted in the
Australian XI. of 1893, and left his mark on both N.S.W.
and South Australia. Fifteen years ago he was generally
recognised as third man on the roll of cricketing fame,
the two above him being George Gitfen and Harry Trott.
The Board of Control sent a wreath, and also the N.S.W.
Cricket Association, and the flags at Sydney Cricket
Ground were hoisted at half-mast.

Mr. W. J. Trickett having refused to recon-
sider his resignation of the position of President of
the N.S.W. Cricket Association, Premier Carruthers
“consented” to take the vacant chair, and was formally
elected last Monday evening.

Trouble Curse.
« * * •
The wet week in Sydney culminated with a
great downpour. All Friday night and far into Saturday
morning the floodgates of Heaven were bust, turning
Sydney Cricket Ground into a bog. Most people took it
for granted that the match would be postponed, but the
Queensland captain, confident in his pack, decided to
play. This was bad judgment on his part, for half of his
team was in a more or less damaged state, some of the
men coming straight from the dressers to the ground.
Against this battered crowd,N.S.W. brought a combina-
tion that was, in many' respects, stronger than even the
first team that met the Queenslanders. And, moreover,
the Queenslanders were strangers in a strange bog, and,
after all, there’s a lot in playing in your own mud. On
the other hand, it was a windless afternoon, and the
winning of the toss gave N.S.W. no advantage. Handi-
capped with five “ hospital players,” Queensland was
beaten from the start, and about the middle of events its
strength gave out entirely. In the first half the
Northern tean kept its end up fairly well, and
drew first blood with a penalty' goal. Before the
whistle went the Cornstalks equalised with a try, which
they failed to convert. Early in the second half the
weaker team was further handicapped by an injury to
the leg of one of the forwards, and though, later on, he
resumed, he was obviously a lame bird, and was finally
retired. With one man short and several others almost
helpless, Queensland tried to break through with drib-
bling sweeps, but the slippery ground broke up their
formation and made the t.iing a gamble. On the other
side, N.S.W. vainly endeavored to let the backs loose,
and they slid wildly in every direction except that of the
Queensland goal. It was a forward game, pure and
simple. Although obviously beaten, Queensland put up
a great defensive fight, and managed to prevent N.S.W.
scoring more than another goal and a try. Thereby it
turned what threatened to be a rout into an ordinary
defeat by 11 to 3.
* * * *
The N.S.W. captain did an insane thing in
placing such a sterling player as Messenger away to glory
on th left wing of the three-quarter line. It would have
been all right had it been a fine day with a hard field
under foot, but when the ground was a quagmire and
there was no passing to speak of it was suicidal. Fortu-
nately, Messenger is far too versatile to be squeezed out
as long as he is included in the team at all, and he
played a great game, quite different from that which he
put- up in the first match. It was an individualistic game,
and Messenger put his ears back and practised private
enterprise with the best of them.
The chief result of the N.S.W.-Queensland
Rugby matches this year has been to reveal the New
England player, W. Dix, as the finest full-back the Ma
State has produced for many years. On Saturday, in
spite of the greasy, sodden state of the ball, his line-
kicking was startlingly accurate. He was full of resource,
and his gathering-in was a triumph, while he repeatedly
risked being drowned by falling on the ball when the
Queensland pack threw itself on him so quickly that there
was no time to kick. And he is only a little scrap of a
man after all.
* * * *
No less than 12 N.S.W. metropolitan and
country Soccer clubs have entered for the Gardiner Oup,
and they will be formed into a league for the purpose of
the contest, which will commence about the beginning of
August. By that time the struggle for the Rawson Oup
should be completed.

Owing to the presence of the Queensland team
in Sydney no First Grade Rugby matches were played on
Saturday afternoon, and both the Leaguers (Austral
Rules) and the Soccers decided to spend the day foot-
balling with their mouths. Most of them put in the
time watching the Rugby men mudlarking at Sydney
Cricket Ground ; yet including them there were less than
6000 people to watch the fun.
* * * *
In Melbourne, on Saturday, the expectation of
a great battle brought a crowd that packed the ground
at the Saints and South disturbance, “ Skinner’s hirelings ”
being the only team reckoned capable of “downing” the
victorious seasiders. This they duly accomplished—-by
12 points, and the superior play of four men. Also, their
straight kicking had something to do with it. But there
was little genuine football, and less concerted play;
mostly, it was a rough, scienceless scramble, the big men
bumping around, slinging, charging, and twice coming to
blows. One individual discussion was so persistent that
a constable went, to separate the two, but the umpire
suspended the game and the argument before the Law got
there. Only good umpiring saved the match from de-
generating into a free fight. Free kicks were very plenti-
ful, and verj' little regarded. If teams that play football
are to have a chance against those that go in for rough
scrambles, and wrestles, and fights, and struggles, where
brute force and weight beat skill and system, the um-
pire will have to be given power to order
persistently-offending players off the field, as in
Rugby. Perhaps he may even require authority to
shoot the persistent offender. The match supplied
excitement enough, as in the third quarter, and again in
the fourth, the scores were equal. In fact, up to within
10 minutes of the finish the alleged game looked like
ending in a draw, but the better trained Southerners
then added two more goals to their tally of five goals two
behinds, and went home joyful. Carlton, which is get-
ting back to its last year’s form, walloped Essendon by
104 points to 50. Melbourne beat ’Roy by 55 to 47, after
the best game of the day ; and ’Wood returned from
Geelong winners by six points, the victory being chiefly
due to the Pivot’s awful kicking. The Association
star match —between the two undefeated teams, Williams-
town and West Melbourne—also brought a big “ gate.”
A scrambling, rough-and-tumble game resulted in a 10-
point win for ’Town, which now heads the list. West
and Essendon tie for second place, Ports and ’Scray do
ditto for fourth, and North adorns the rear with a large
oto its name. As North was Premier team three seasons
back, its downfall is notable.
m * * *
The “willingness” of Victorian footballers
and the hastiness of the barrackers will get someone into
serious trouble yet. After the South and Carlton
match on May 25, which South won on its opponents’
ground, a crowd of degraded ruffians hustled the players
when leaving the field, the women, who were, if possible,
worse than the men, sticking hatpins into them, and
kicking them on the shins. One Southerner lost his
temper, and clipped a small boy on the ear, whereupon
the crowd surrounded the pavilion and demanded that
the hasty player be brought out to be slain forthwith.
The police, who, incompetent as they are in Melbourne,
do some good occasionally, got him away by the back ;
but the small boy was a persistent kid, and brought the
case to court, and the justices reckoned £3 a fair thing.
A more strenuous uproar marked the match between
Castlemaine and Wesley Hill, on June 12th, when a W.H.
player, being dissatisfied with the umpire’s decision, pro-
ceeded to smite wisdom into him. The opposing'pla.vers
resented this, and a gorgeous free fight ensued, the game
ending at three-quarter time, with both scores disputed.
They are a hefty lot, these country ’bailers.

All lacrosse and baseball matches in Sydney
were postponed last Saturday owing to the rain, and the
result is r ither serious to the followers of the Canadian
game. As there are no other dates available for the
missed matches, they will presumably have to be dropped.
Balmain and liurwood clubs were both confident of
winning, and had the latter scored, it would have given
the club a lead of four points in the competition, and a
hard and fast mortgage on the premiership.

Mr. E. R. S. Larbalastier is founding an Ama-
teur Motor Cycle Club in Sydney, for the benefit of non-
professional riders. Among the events proposed to be
held shortly are a reliability test, and a hill-climbing
contest, in which the prizes will be £5, £3, and £2, for
first, second, and third places respectively. Unless some-
body has been misreported, the new club is to be an
amateur cash, or cash amateur, one.

The world’s record for motor-cycles climbs
steadily upward. The latest lift was made by C. B.
Collier, the well-known English motor-cycle man, who
covered the mile in lmin. 4 l-ssec.
■ * * * *
Judging by the harvest the surf has gathered
in of late around Sydney, the race of fools who seek ex-
citement in a gamble with the undertow where there are
practically no life-saving appliances, will shortly be ex-
tinct. Last week a pair of them went in at Tamarama
Bay and promptly got into difficulties. One was rescued
by strangers on whom he had no claim, but who risked
their lives to drag him from where his foolishness had
placed him. The other was not so fortunate, and he is just
as dead as might be expected. There are three scenes in
the last act of the fool-bather’s career : (1) “ Be hanged .
Who’s afraid?” (2) “Whoop, isn’t it lovely?’ (3)
“ Help 1”
* * * *
Balmain (N.S.W.) Municipal Council recently
started a municipal bath for the use of the ratepayer?,
and in a spasm of liberality allowed the local public
school children the benefit of the water free. The kiddies
rushed theioea, and Balmain promises to provide next
generation’s Kierans and Cavills. The council has now
decided that the State Government is the proper
authority to provide water for the Balmain youths to
swim in, and has applied for a State grant. And the
local anti-Socialistic member, who believes in Private
Enterprise, and who urges the community not to be a
tired leaner-up against the State Cow, had better clamor
for that State grant with all the resources of his mouth,
for election time is near.
* * * *
The number of wealthy members who are pre-
pared to build and maintain expensive racing yachts
doesn’t tend to increase. The motor car is turning out
to be a serious rival to the speed yacht. Also the mot< r
boat is distracting attention from the white-winged craft.
Sydney’s two big yacht clubs have, therefore, decided io
niake a bid for the support of small men who can’t hoye
to build and run a Sayonara or an Awanui. They ha\ e
practically'decided to encourage a new class of 10-metie
raters, and will offer a third of the total season’s prue
money to the class, provided five boats are maintained in
commission. A yacht of this size is a toy yacht, arid it
won’t be easy' to explain to your girl on the Manly boat
where the 10-metre “yacht” starts and where the 18ft.
sailing boat leaves off.
* # * *
Sydney motor-boats Invincible and Fairbanks
will meet again on the same course —Garden Island to
Manly' and back—on Saturday week, for the Blue Pennant
of Sydney Harbor. There will be three laces., and, of
course, the winner of the majority will fly the Sign of the
speed championship of N.S.W. ihe beauty' of the Blue
Pennant is that the holder of it can be given a month’s
notice to race by anyone, so the flag has a fair show of
being always found on the fastest boat afloat in Port
Jackson. Invincible has now been repaired and is running
better than ever, and Mr. Relph is very sanguine in his
lair at Mort’s Dock. So are the Davies brothers, who
have implicit faith in their semi-hydroplane.
* * * *
Re the crack Achilles, j ust retired from M.L.
racing. His first appearance was at Riccarton (Christ-
church), as a two-year-old, and thereby hangs a tale.
The owners, having discovered what wonderful pace
the youngster was possessed of, decided to go straight
for a coup on his very first race. They accordingly
shipped him from Wellington to Lyttelton, the day
before the race, which, of course, meant his arriving the
day it w'as run. He was given an easy canter the wrong
way round the course to the starting post in order to
take the stiffness resulting from the journey out of
him. Large sums were telegraphed to Cohen all
over M.L. as late as the backers dared to leave
it. But it so happened that they despatched
their telegrams a little too early, with the result
that a good proportion of the investment came back on
to the Tote, thereby considerably reducing the price, and
sending the unknown prad out second favorite. Circum-
stances considered, Achilles ran a great colt, but he was
just beaten by Helen Faucit, a big filly of Stead’s, which,
though not Wonderfully fast, was turned out in the pink
of condition, and thereby prevailed in the last few yards.
Had the stable been content to ship the colt with other
horses a week or so earlier, he would have won as he liked,
and his starting price would, in all probability, have been
no lower.

Headache, Indigestion and
Const]pation.
'AN IMPORTANT DISCOVERY.
It will interest sufferers to know
that a valuable medicine, called Froo-
toids, has been discovered, which is
now completely curing each of the
above-named complaints. Frootoids
are elegant in appearance, and plea-
sant to take, and, what is of the ut-
most importance, are thoroughly reli-
able in affording quick relief. You
do not require to go on taking them
for a prolonged period, as is necessary
with some medicines, which even then
are mostly disappointing; you simply
take a dose of Frootoids when ill and
repeat the dose if necessary, but gene-
rally one dose is quite effective.
> Frootoids are immensely more valu-
able than an ordinary aperient, in so
far that they not only act as an ape-
rient, but do remove from the blood,
tissues, and internal organs all the
waste poisonous matter that is clog-
ging them and choking the channels
that lead to .and from them. The bene-
ficial effects of Frootoids are evident
at once by the disappearanc e of head-
ache, the head becoming clear, and a
bright, cheery sense of perfect health
taking the place of sluggish, depressed
feelings, by the liver acting properly,
and by the food being properly di-
gested.
Frootoids are the proper aperient
medicine to take when any Congestion
or Blood Poison present, or when
Congestion of the Brain or Apoplexy
is present or threatening. They have
been tested, and have been proved to
afford quick relief in such cases when
other aperients have not done any
good at all. It is of the utmost impor-
tance that this should be borne in
mind, for in such cases to take an or-
dinary aperient is to waste time and
permit of a serious illness becoming
fatal.
Frootoids act splendidly on the liver,
and quickly cure bilious attacks that
antibilious pills make worse. Many
people have been made sick and ill
by antibilious pills that could have
been cured at once by Frootoids.
People should not allow themselves to
be duped into contracting a medicine-
taking habit by being persuaded to
take daily doses with each meal of so-
called indigestion cures that do NOT
cure. Frootoids have been subjected
to extensive tests, and have in every
case proved successful in completely
curing the complaints named.
A constipated habit of body will be
completely cured if the patient will on
each occasion, when suffering, take a
dose of Frootoids, instead of an or-
dinary aperient; by so doing, the pa-
tient will require doses only at longer
intervals, .and will so become quite
independent of the necessity of taking
any aperient medicine.
Frootoids are only now being placed
on the Australian market, consequently
you may at present have a difficulty
in getting them from your local che-
mist or storekeeper; but ask for them,
and if you cannot get them at once,
send stamps or postal note for price,
1/6, to W. G. Hearne, Chemist, Gee-
long, and a bottle of them will be im-
mediately forwarded to you post free.
Chemists, _ storekeepers, and whole-
salers can now obtain wholesale sup-
plies from W. G. Hearne, Chemist,
Geelong, Victoria.

Dandruff
Are you troubled with the discomforts of
this unpleasant disease ? Yes it is a disease,
and a very fatal one so far as your hair is
concerned. Dandruff is the forerunner of bald-
ness. If you have dandruff you should take
steps at once to cure it, otherwise it will keep
on increasing until it totally destroys your hair.
What Dandruff Is
When your hair roots become weak either
from a run down condition of health or from
neglect, they begin to decay. When decay
sets in it is accompanied by a microbe growth
which continues to multiply, and as it does, it
absorbs all the natural oil of the hair and
scalp. When the scalp is deprived of its
source of nourishment it grows thin, tight, dry.
Dandruff is simply particles of the dead scalp
which have become dry and peeled off.
How To Cure It
You can go on forever ‘‘pouring'’ things on
your head and yet get no benefit from them.
You must remove the cause. You must
strengthen and rebuild your scalp; make it
strong so that it can withstand and overcome
the dandruff germ. Make it fleshy, pliable,
vigorous. There is only one way of doing
this and that is Barry’s Tri-coph-erous. It
goes right to the Cause of the trouble. It sup-
plies the hair roots with nourishment; it re-
builds the scalp ; it kills the dandruff microbe.
Listen To Reason
No one can grow hair in a night, but every-
body who has’nt chronic baldness (which n
incurable) can grow strong, thick, luxuriant
hair, if they will give the necessary time to the
proper care of their scalps. Nourish your
scalp ; build up the wasted tissues with Barry’3
Tri-coph-erous. This is the way to get results,
and results are what prove more than all the
advertising in the world. Part your hair with
a coarse comb, then sprinkle on the Tri-coph-
erous. Wet your scalp thoroughly but don’t
drench it. After this manipulate or “knead”
