The imported Johnson has turned a deaf
ear to the deputation which has been clamoring
for a system of season tickets on the Sydney
trams. Said Johnson :
There was no service in the world which could be com-
pared with the Sydney tramways, during the busy hours
of the day, for rapidity and frequency. They must bear
in mind the length of the penny section. _ He had no
hesitation in saying they could, not do better in any coun-
try or city in the world.
When the S.M. Herald heard that Johnson had
reached Adelaide on the way out to his job, it
breathed heavily, and declared that now our tram-
way system would soon be put right. And John-
son declares that the system that Oliver, the con-
demned and sacked Australian, built up cannot be
excelled anywhere in the world.
Premier Carruthers, who has had it dinned
into his ears for the past three years that the
crimping of seamen at Newcastle had reached
serious proportions, paid a flying visit to
Coalopolis recently, and returned to Sydney with
the announcement that he had discovered the
existence of crimping. He then addressed the
local Mayor, pointing out that he (the Mayor)
could obtain power under the Local Government
Act to regulate seamen’s boarding-houses, and
ordered him to apply at once for these necessary
powers. The Mayor answered in a weary tone
that he had already applied for this authority, and
that his application had been lying in Carruthers’
own office for the past four months unanswered.
Whereupon Joseph subsided again.
With a wild whirl of a Tam-o’-shanter,
some one sent this to Sydney Telegraph
The Federal compact has been broken. It therefore is
open to N.S.W., and she should act on it at once to make
her own management. Let her withdraw from this
hateful Federal compact and muddle; start on her
own lines, and approach Queensland to form with her
territory a nation which would overshadow all the rest of
the continent of Australia in desirability, prosperity, and
progress. The time is now, let her secede, manage her
own affairs. The man who is able and willing to lead
such a movement can depend on the support of thousands
of others who think as I do.—Yours, etc., ’l5 and ’45.
This appeal might serve as a gentle reminder to
Joe C. as to where his secesh vaporings are lead-
ing some people. Rebellion and treason are ugly
words, and the fate of the leaders of the 1715 and
1745 risings isn’t pleasant reading. Those of them
who didn’t hang saved themselves by flight, even
if they had to leave a fairly profitable solicitor’s
business behind them. And when Canada had a
secesh movement it peacefully hanged the principal
secesher, despite his plea that he was a lunatic
when he seceded.
Passionate outburst from Argentina (Pas-
toralists’ Review, 15/5/’O7) by one who is plainly
a member of Reid’s “Democratic League ” :
And there is one pest they (i.e., the pastoralists) haven’t
got to annoy them here, and that is the British work-
man. Oh ! wouldn’t I just like to import a thousand or
two gauchos, who turn out willingly at daybreak and
keep on till dark without a growl.
A wild mad desire to import gauchos, chows and
kanakas in large masses is the sign manual of the
Reidite democrat. And how he does loathe the
great mass of the British people, despite his flags
and his blessed Empire Day, and his habit of
calling England “Home!” To this wretched
Tory anti-British bounder, about three-fourths of
the male part of the British nation is simply a
“ pest.”
“ J.R.” writes rather confusedly :
A case which turned on the identity of a young female
person, who recently died allegedly from the effects of an
illegal operation, has just been tried in Sydney criminal
court for the second time, the jury having disagreed on
both occasions. The Judge remarked, with some asperity,
on the little care taken by the local hospitals to identify
their patients. It seems, indeed, as if it might not be a
bad way for a person who wanted to quietly get rid of his
identity to get himself admitted as a casualty to one of
the Sydney hospitals, call himself John Jones, or some-
thing equally impersonal, get himself promptly dis-
charged without giving much trouble, and then fade
away. To all appearance all traces of such a person would
be lost, but in case of any trouble he could always prove
by the hospital records that he was Jones. A very
curious case in point occurred in connection with the
N.S.W. Old Age Pension Act some time ago. A pension
had been running for some three years, when it suited
some person with a puli to get it stopped. It was ascer-
tained that someone of the same name as the pensioner
(there are some hundreds of the same name) had been
admitted to one of the hospitals 12 years before. There
was ho proof that he was the pensioner, and there was a
difference of five years in their ages. No one knew what
had become of the hospital patient, and the incident sort
of flapped itself to death, but the pension was stopped on
general principles.
There is war talk again in Japan, directed
against the United States. But it seems likely
that poverty will keep the Brown Man quiet for
the next few years. Even the Jap can’t fight
on an empty Treasury, and he recently failed to
convert his dear-money war loans into stock at
more reasonable interest, while the proposal for a
new loan was received with the deadest silence
that the financial world has heard for a long time.
Now, it is up to Australia to make the best pos-
sible use of the respite before the Ape gets his
second monetary wind.
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co., report : There is
no change to report in the local wool market. A steady
demand exists for all descriptions of the staple, at values
about on a par with those ruling at last month’s auctions.
At the Brisbane sales, held last week, super scoureds did
not meet with the support expected. All other descrip-
tions of greasy and scoured, however, met with a ready
sale at late rates. From information to hand from
Bradford, we gather that spinners and manufacturers
are well employed, with orders on their books that will
take some months to execute, although new business is
rather quiet. Our next sale will take place on Thursday
(20th) when we expect to bring forward a catalogue of
about 1000 bales, consisting chiefly of the new clip from
Queensland.*
The island trader, who gave to the S.M.
Herald particulars of employment in the New
Hebrides in rebuttal of certain House of Commons
statements about the oppression of the natives by
long hours of work, might have effectually hit
back at horrified Britain with the story of its own
oppression of its own workers. Two wrongs
don’t make a right, but when the British
Government affects horror at a blaclrboy working
from daylight till dark, there is a great tempta-
tion to remark that one wrong doesn’t make a
right either, even when it is Bull’s wrong.
Only a few months before the Commons stood
up for the blackboy of the New Hebrides, one
Miss Irwin gave evidence before a committee
of the Lords, and showed that girls were em-
ployed in bakers’ shops in England from 6.45 a.m.
to 8 p.m., and to 11.30 p.m. on Saturdays. The
black boys in the New Hebrides are allowed a
couple of hours’ siesta in the middle of the day,
but the bakers’ white girls are given no time for
rest, and even while eating their meals, they have
to run backward and forward to the shop. And
their case isn’t the worst; from 90 to 96 hours a
week are set down as the English restaurant girl’s
record of labor. Some day John Bull will have
his belltopper and prayer-book taken from him,
and the sentence of civilisation will be that he
shall not be allowed to appear in public with them
any more till he treats his own girls half as well
as he professes to think a distant male nigger
should be treated.
The Bulletin receives a card from Mr.
Frank Lock, a State candidate for Waverley
(N.S.W.). It reads as follows :
Vote for Honest Politics, Progressive naws, Liberty of
Action, Liberty of Conscience, Freedom of Speech, Anti-
Sectarianism, Local Option, No Confiscation, Anti-
Monopoly, Fair Labor Conditions, Land for the People,
A White Australia, Humane Laws, Help for the Little
Ones, Immigration, Sound Finance, The Development of
Our Resources, True Reform, etc., etc., etc.|
N.S.W. has local option and freedom of speech
already. The State Parliament has no voice in
the White Australia matter, so that point is mere
frill. And the rest are platitudes. Everybody
claims that his politics are “honest,” his laws’
“humane” and “progressive,” his finance
“sound,” his reform “true,” and his idea of
labor conditions “fair.” Everybody believes in
“ land for the people,” but doesn’t say how they
are to get it. Everybody believes in “help for
the little ones,” but doesn’t say how they are to
be helped; and “the development of our re-
sources” is as old a platitude as the hills. Mr.
Lock is in the anti-Carruthers interest. That
cause wants a much more definite candidate.
Melbourne Age is deeply shocked about
the great leasehold estates in the Territory. And
people are saying that if the paper could see its
way to be half as much shocked over the great
freehold monopolies in the Western District of
Victoria, that dreadful and chronic story about
the Farmers’ Exodus might not be such a weary
disfigurement to the aforesaid paper.

Suffered for a Long Time Without
Relief—Had Three Doctors and
Derived No Benefit —One Doctor
Was Afraid to Touch Them
Soreness Disappeared and Hands
Now Smooth After Application of
CUTICURA SOAP AND
CUTICURA OINTMENT
“For a long time I suffered with
Bores on the hands which were itching,
painful, and disagreeable. I had three
doctors and derived no benefit from
any of them. One doctor said he was
afraid to touch my hands, so you
must know how bad they were; an-
other said I never could be cured; and
the third said the sores were caused
by the dipping of my hands in water
in the dye-house where I work. I
saw in the papers about the wonderful
cures of the Cuticura Remedies and
procured some of the Cuticura Soap
and Cuticura Ointment. In three
days after the application of the
Cuticura Ointment my hands began
to peel and were better. The sore-
ness disappeared, and they are now
smooth and clean, and I am still
working in the dye-house.
“I strongly recommend Cuticura
Soap and Cuticura Ointment to any
one with sore hands, and I hope that
this letter will be the means of help-
ing other sufferers. Mrs. A. E. Maurer,
Chicago, 111., July 1, 1905.”

office of the Potter Drug & Chemical Corporation.

The popular credulity which fills the
pockets of the spiritualistic “meejum” with
good shining coin is beginning to develop other
aspects, not quite so pleasant to the ghostly im-
postor. He now finds it rather difficult to get a
decent house to live in and nail his shingle to.
Landlords discover that it is bad business to let a
dwelling to a raiser of the dead. Quite a multi-
tude of people believe in spirits, and a lot more
half-believe, and an additional number don’t
exactly half-believe, but don’t like to hear
strange knockings in the dead of night. There is
a feeling that the spirits may be real, and that
when they have acquired the habit of dropping in
at a house they may keep on doing it, and even
if they don’t there are plenty more houses, and it
is no use taking a risk. One Sydney house—a
well-appointed place in a decent street —has had
three different tenants during the two months
since the spookist flitted. The first tenant
stayed a week, the second four days, and the
third three days. They simply “didn’t like the
place ” as soon as they heard that ‘ ‘ spirits ” used
to drop in there o’ nights, and so they left. Now
the youngsters thereabouts call it “the haunted
house,” and that’s the first step on the downward
road which may lead to it being a faded, shutter-
less, broken-windowed tenement, avoided by every-
body as if it were an evil spirit, and only fit to be
sold for firewood.
“ Stony ” writes :
A pallid, scared, feminine face, shabbily straw-hatted
above, and shabbily bodiued below, peered round the
open swing-door of the bar, and withdrew. Later, I went
out, on my way to the street, and in the vestibule found
the owner of the face and hat, with a frightened expres-
sion, supporting at intervals the head of an utterly
paralytic victim of beer. Seven men, all eminently
stupid, were attempting to drag the drunk to his feet.
The Public always does try to stand a drunk on
his feet. When the drunk once arrives at an
upright position he immediately falls down again,
and cuts his nose and bleeds like a pig, and a large
crowd collects, and he is taken in charge for being screwed
and disorderly. This bunch of men was trying to stand
the drunk up. Then I came on the scene, calm and
collected, and asked her of the heavy eye if she W'anted
to get her husband home. Which seems a silly enough
question when one comes to think of it. However, at
that stage a blunderer dashed out from the bar and said,
bluntly and excitedly, “ What ? What’s this ? What’s
the matter ? What —is he drunk 1” The faded Mrs. re-
plied, indignantly, “ No, Vs not. ’E’s sick ! ” and then
answered me,“ Yes.” “Well, you want a cab?” I
demanded. “0, they’ve just sent fer one,” she said. The
drunk had reclined in the vestibule about half-an-hour by
this time, and they had just sent fora cab. A lean-faced
person at that moment put his chin over the crow'd and
said : “ ’Ere ’e is, missus—an’ it’ll cost yer two bob.”
She of the hopeless hair murmured, defiantly, “ Well,
it’s no good ; I’ve on’y got one-an’-nine,” and we of the
public had to take up a collection to find the remaining
threepence. Then we had the work of dumping the em-
beered one in among the straw at the bottom of the cab.
What I want to know is : Why isn’t a seeing-home fund
provided by publicans for the comfortable disposal of
their disabled patrons, instead of leaving them to the
public ? The thing should be done on the Monte Carlo
principle, by which the stony-broke punter is allowed
money to take him out of town. The publican is anti-
Sosh in theory ; but when it comes to State or public
care of his principal product, the beer-cadaver, he lets the
State and the public carry the corpse readily enough.
“D.” : He came out of Exhibition-street,
Melbourne, and yelled after the green tram head-
ing down Collins-street. The conductor did not
hear, or did not want to hear, and the man started
in pursuit at quite a great pace for a short-legged,
rather meaty person overgrown with country
whiskers. He was certainly from the country-
one of the simple bush characters to whom bad
men present wads of bank-notes left by philan-
thropic relations in Fiji. He carried a bundle
loosely wrapped in brown paper, and every time
he tried to brandish it as a signal to the tram-
conductor some article of wearing apparel fell out.
At the top of the hill he nearly reached us, but by
this time "he was almost breathless. I refrained
from enlightening the officer in charge, as I hate
to spoil a good sporting event, and so the tram
started across Russell-street without him. He
came down the incline past George and George’s
at a great bat, still shedding goods out of his
bundle, and in front of the Town Hall, while the
tram was waiting signals, 'he arrived, lurched
into a corner, and sat gasping. The conductor
took his thrum and nipped a ticket. “Pu-pu-put
me down at Swanston-street, please,” said the
stranger from Far Out. “Certainly,” said the
conductor, “this is Swanston-street.”
“ Koala ” has had an experience :
One wet night, a fortnight or so back, a motor-car
stopped in front of our door. The motorist, muffled up
in a big loosely-fitting leather coat, after seeing his car
housed, came in, and in a voice of the purest “ culehah,”
started out on what was almost a monologue. He began
on the boy (whom he called “ Harray ”) accompanying
him. “Ai say, Harray, what rippin’ luck ! My cigarettes
are still dwy. Ai thought they would be sodden. May
we have tea? Thanks, thanks awfully. How far are we
from tone? Only 30 miles, eh ? Ai would have gone on
only Ai was afwaid my cahbide would give ote, and Ai
mate be run in if Ai had no late when Ai got to tone. It
is absolutely impehative that Ai weach tone by nine in the
mohning, so Ai shall need to be up at six. Ai am afwade
Ai shall sleep in. Eh, you’ll call me 1 You will, for
suah ? Thanks, awfullay. Ai am a bit fagged, and
the boy is knocked out. If Ai get an early staht, and
there are no twavellers, Ai shall go lake—lake— blazes.
Ha, heah’s the tea. So good of you.” Then to me:
“ Keep on smoking, my deah f’lah. Ai am a seasoned
vessel.” After his meal, and when the second cigarette
was under way, he went on : “Ai have been touring for
pleashah, and about 10 miles back, some bally thing or
othah went wrong. Bay good luck we got going again,
and then had a beastly punctchah—took us two solid
hahs to repair it. Othahwise Ai would have been home
befoah this. Heigh ho ! what d’ye chink of ouahEedewal
legislatahs? Wotton lot, ain’t they, the whole box and dice
of’em? And then think of that man Bent going home
as Pw'emiah of Victorya ! What will they think of us in
England? Well A’me for bed. Youah suah you’ll call
me? Thanks so much; good nate. Come, Harray.”
Called him at six, by rapping on his wall. He broke off
a very high-class snore, and yapped out, “ What the
devil’s the matter? Eh, what, 6 o’clock alweady? Ai
have been dweaming. Thanks, thanks, awfly. Harray !
Harray 1” “Or-ri.” A few seconds later—“Harray!
what is the use of youah saying ‘All rate’ and not
moving? You must turn ote.” Half an hour later, after
making a din with the “cah” like the upsetting of a
well-stocked travelling tinker’s waggon, they got away,
and it is to be hoped reached home safely.
Thus a Pig Islander :
Ic is not probable that the “ tip ” curse will ever leave
us. Like the politician's promises of roads and bridges,
its immediate advantages are too many and too far-reach-
ing. I was one of a number of unfortunate Exhibition
patrons who rushed a departing boat at Lyttelton on the
bare prospect of a shake-down. The chief steward said
“No ! ” with decision. Even the masthead was occupied.
I lied glibly to the effect that the company’s office had
assured me of a shake-down, at least. “ The company’s
office would tell you anything,” he said. “ We’ve turned
hundreds of ’em away. Why, some of ’em have even
offered us money," he remarked fiercely, as he eyed the
crowd of importunate persons who blocked the gangway.
“ Yes, money ! ” he hissed significantly, turning to me. ' I
retired, abashed, but fully determined not to leave the
steamer. “ What’s your berth, sir? See to your luggage,
sir ? ” and a steward clutched my belongings. “ Ain’t got
none,” I replied ungrammatically and with bitterness.
My hand wandered unconsciously to my hip pocket.
“ That’s all right, sir, I’ll fix you up. Ah, thank you,
sir.” The other victims, who knew not the value of a
coin on an emergency, imagined I had some influence
with the company or something. The gratuities go into a
common pool, which is subsequently divided amongst the
crowd. The tippers are certainly marked out for especial
attention, and the knowledge that they have tipped
spreads with incredible rapidity.

DIRECTORS’ REPORT.
Presented at the Ordinary General Meeting of the Proprietors, held at Winchester House, Old Broad Street,
(Hall No. 174), at 12 o’clock noon, on Monday, 13 th May, 1907.
! The Directors present their Report, accompanied as usual by audited Accounts, for the year ended 31st
December last.
The Profits as shown by the Annual Statement of Profit and Loss, after deducting Management
Charges and Taxes amount to .. .. .. .. .. .. .. £83,553 2 9
To which is added the amount brought forward .. .. .. ~ 1 o'lo6 13 4

year 1906, amounting to .. .. .. .. .. .. .. 47,098 7 9

Which the Directors recommend be appropriated as follows : *

To Balance carried forward to next account.. .. .. .. .. £23,907 17 10
During the year Branches have been opened at Alvie and Beaufort in Victoria, and at Macksville, Nabiae and
Smithtown in New South Wales. The Branch at Townsville, Queensland, has been closed.
It is with much regret the Directors have to announce the death of their late colleagues, Sir James F. Garrick,
K.C.M.G., K.C., and Mr. George Miller.
Under the provisions of the Bank’s Articles of Association the vacancies thus caused on the Board have been
filled by the appointment of Mr. George Slade and Mr. Gordon Hdntly Campbell.
The Director retiring under the provisions of the Bank’s Articles of Association is Mr. David Finlayson, who,
being eligible, offers himself for re-election.
The Bank’s Auditors, Messrs. Price, Waterhouse & Co., also retire, and again offer themselves for re-election.
Following the Meeting of Shareholders, a Meeting- of Depositors will be held at 12.45 p.m., at which Mr. Andrew
Cunningham and Mr. Gordon Huntly Campbell, the retiring Depositors’ Directors, will offer themselves for
re-election.
Warrants for Dividends, less Incrme Tax, will be issued payable on and after the 16th May, to those Share-
holders whose names appeared on the Register on the 29th ultimo.
F. J. CURTIS, Secretary.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the seiise of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.
RAMEY'S MEDICATOR cures Catarrh,

“ Ailral” : Part of a political address de-
livered in my town
There’s Denmark and Norway and Sweden and Por-
tugal, all making their living at agriculture alone. Us in
Australia get 17 million a yer from butter, 20 million a
yer from wool, 15 million a yer from mutton, six million
a yer from timber. We have a population of four million
a yer—er-er —a million in four yers, er—er—I mean
four million altogether. That works out at over £4 a yer
for butter per head, £5 a yer for wool, £3 15s. a yer for
mutton, and £1 10s. a yer for timber. In spite of
droughts we have recovered marvellously from our de-
pression, and have paid every penny of our debts.
And I believe the animal was elected.
Westralia’s Newton Moore Ministry, which
has drawn a year’s pay for uselessly wagging its
chin, threatens, in the course of one session, to
pass a land-tax, legalise preferential voting,
abolish the Public Service Commissioner, appoint
a new railway manager, reform the electoral law,
put through an Infant Life Protection Bill,
authorise half-a-dozen railways, and knock the
present licensing law endways, in addition to rub-
bing the House of Fat the wrong way if it gives
any trouble. And that’s only half the programme.
In fact, the policy speech hasn’t been delivered
yet. The things mentioned are mere trifles to go
on (talking) with.

Considering that the following letter
arrived nearly 30 years before it was written, it
seems to The Bulletin that some of the asper-
sions cast on the Australian postal department
must be groundless : —
Federal City, May 6, 1937.
Dear Bulletin,— A few of us had an argument last
night, at the Federal City Hotel, and we want you to
settle it. I said that, about 30 years ago, N.S.W. had a
Premier named Karruthers or Crothers —I am not very
sure of the spelling. The others said that there never
was such a Premier, and that the Premiers about the
period mentioned were Parkes, Gibbs, Barton and Crick
(or Click).
Now, I distinctly remember, as a boy, hearing my
father talk about “ old Bill Crothers ” (they used to goto
school together, I believe). Dad said that Bill Crothers
had two great schemes. One was for altering the slant
of the earth, so that the sun would shine only onN.S.W.,
leaving the rest of Australia with merely a sort of twi-
light. But just when this scheme was ready for use,
Kruthers trod on a banana-peel, and slipped off the end
of Circular Quay, and we heard no more of him. I fancy
he was drowned. Bill’s other scheme was to build the
Federal city on wheels ; and every night he intended to
shift it quietly a few yards nearer to Sydney. I remem-
ber Dad reckoning that this was a great scheme ; and I
was disgusted that none of the people here seemed to
have ever heard of it. Yours, etc.,
State F. Bight.
During 1906 there was an increase of 128
factories in N.S. Wales, and 5583 extra hands
found employment in the industries fostered by
the Protection which came with Federation.
“ H.” on the matter of compulsory vot-
ing :—
If a man is so apathetic that he will not trouble to go
to the polling booth, or secure a postal ballot paper,
the less say he has in the government of his country the
better. So far from forcing the apathetic to vote, the
proper course would be to discourage the apathetic vote
altogether. It is the vote of the people who don’t know
and don’t care which is the real danger to the com-
munity.

What is health ? To this the answer is : A perfect structure of all the organs of
digestion and elimination, with a perfect performance of all their functions. Health
and happ.ness are possessions we all desire to obtain and retain. You cannot be
healthy with a sluggish liver or inactive kidneys; sometimes these organs get thrown
out of action on account of improper food or drink, sometimes from a faulty
metabolism. Nature then requires assistance, and it is of paramount importance
that the right aid should be employed in dealing with these delicate pieces of
mechanism. It has been known from time immemorial that certain European spas
which have acquired world-renowned fame on account of their possessing remedial
and health-giving properties, form a natural stimulant for the liver and act as a
cleansing agent for the kidneys. The health-giving and remedial properties of these
famous spas are now incorporated in Kutnow’s Powder, which is universally
acknowledged by the most eminent physicians to be a most perfect remedy for all
liver, kidney, stomach and bowel disorders.
TAKE KUTNOW’S POWDER
The INFALLIBLE REMEDY.
The principal organs concerned in effecting the separation of deleterious substances from the blood are the
liver, kidneys and bowels. So long as these organs work efficiently so long may the owner count on health and
strength. But we know that a large percentage of mankind find it very difficult to keep the digestive and excretory
organs in perfect working order. Stripped of these health-producing faculties, they are left miserable, anxious mortals,
simply worrying through a bald and barren existence. All fear of dyspepsia, liver disease, kidney trouble, and intestinal blood-
poisoning can be avoided by simply adopting the remedy which Prof. Lawson Tait, M.D., so highly recommended—“ KUTNOW’S POWDER.”

Business, Robbery, etc.
A very large firm of Sydney drapers always
keeps the odd halfpenny. It does an immense business,
and that odd bawbee must amount to a good deal in the
course of the year.
■***•*
“Tito”: My nervous system received a rude
shock a day or two age. Whilst looking over some old in-
voices, I came across one from an English firm, and
amongst other matter printed at the head of it was
“Manufacturer and Exporter." I rubbed my mas-
sive brow for a few moments and thought deeply.
Exporter 1 I had never noticed that word on
any of our warehouses or shops—surely it musD
be a mistake of the German clerk when he made out
the invoice for the printer. Still my craving for know-
ledge was not satisfied, and all night I sat up and
wrestled with the mighty subject. With morning light
it gradually dawned upon me. “Exporters!” They
must be the people who supply our “ Direct importers.”
I had never seen the word b.efore, so my ignorance may
be excused. I only see local invoices, and they all read
“ Direct importers.”
Bendigo, not being able to get much excite-
ment at “The Beehive ” just now (share-dealing is as
dead as Julius Caesar), is tearing itself in halves in a wild
discussion whether pigs are cattle. It all arose out of a
license to sell cattle granted to “Jimmy” Curnow, ex-
postman, and ex-Mayor. Bendigo’s produce markets are
mostly in Hargreaves-street, whither everybody with
weeds in his whiskers and strange smells about his
clothes naturally drifts, and Curnow set up his saleyards
there. He specialised in pig's. Now', these yards are in
the very heart of Bendigo, and, though there was
one pig-selling yard within a few chains, a large
part of Bendigo reckoned that there shouldn’t
be any more ; and Curnow, Who had meantime been
emptied out of the Bendigo City Council—-chiefly on
the pig or no-pig issue—was instructed to sell no more
swine. But his lawyer told him that he was licensed to
sell cattle, and swine W'ere cattle ; therefore he wouldn’t
stop. Then the Council consulted its lawyer, and other
people consulted other lawyers, and among them they
make out that sw'ine, are cattle sometimes, but not
always, but nobody knows for sure when they are and
when they are not, no time-table having been issued on
the subject. So Curnow risks it, and sells on, and the
lawyers have hopes that the controversy will carry them
through the winter.
“ Murray Hicks” : I went to the Sweated In-
dustries show, taken from the Christchurch Exhibition,
and now' being used as an object lesson throughout
Maoriland, and I shuddered. Things that I bad spent
years trying to forget came hammering back, and I feel
them ail over again. This lady’s costume, of elegant
design and neat workmanship, the task of about 30 hours,
made for 13d. ; and here, in Maoriland, we should pay at
least 20s. for the labor. A child’s suit and shirt made for
sd. the lot! Though I came into the world under what
looked like pretty favorable circumstances, by one of
the queer turns of Fortune’s wheel I spent seven years
of my miserable childhood amid scenes of squalor and
starvation —“in poverty, hunger, and dirt,” on the bed-
rock of existence. Maxim Gorky’s recent description of
the poor children of New' York as “human wolves,”
prow'ling in the gutters, turning over the dust bins and
refuse-heaps in search of scraps of anything eatable, irre-
spective of filth, is literally true. I know—l have been a
wolf. Looking at thesesweated garments, I see a recurring
scene of my boyhood in a garret— sans food, sans fire,
sans everything but a little hope. We frequently listened
with expectancy to footfalls on the creaking stair, to be
confronted by "some visitor or other, who generally
enjoined us to put our trust in God, assured us that He
would provide, and left us a tract. One of them, I re-
member, was entitled, “Are you a Sheep or a Goat?” I
used to puzzle over it then, and have often done so since
w'ithout a satisfactory solution of the problem. It would
.be a good thing if a similar exhibit to that which has set
me thinking and writing could be showrn throughout
Australia. It should arrest the attention and enlist the
sympathies of every Australian worker, and make him
think tpo.

Part of a Johnstone River (Q.) sugar planter’s
wail, t’other day, was for children of the hoe, or, as he
put it, “ apprentices who would be paid a merely nominal
wage until they were conversant with the industry.”
This awful complexity of a hoe never struck us before.
By the way, if a boy has to serve an apprenticeship before
he can weed sugar cane, what is unskilled labor ?

They start early in Melbourne. Some boys
went out last holiday shooting with a pea-rifle. As it
didn’t W'ork well, the elder of two brothers looked down
the barrel, and the usual thing happened. Then—so it
was recorded at the inquest—the victim’s brother, aged
11, after vainly trying to get the other to speak, wailed
“ Geordie, Geordie, are you gone! Who do you leave
your pigeons to?” But Geordie was already past caring
about pigeons.
The latest roving party of Australian miners to
be heard from are manning the Neaqueen Proprietary
Gold Mines, in the foothills of the Andes (S. America),
and they were expecting to hear from a gang of bandits,
40 stron°' as soon as the battery got to work. This
opens up an alluring vista. The big fish which was just
missed by the angler will he as nothing to the size of the
dividend which would have been announced if the bandits
had not looted the battery, or massacred the staff, or re-
moved the mine. It must be a blessed relief for an apolo-
g'ising chairman of directors to. have a g*ang* of bandits to
fall back on. All the other excuses are so frayed.
* * * ,
Bent’s Closer Settlement Board appears to be
making some wonderfully bad bargains. One that it has
now on offer to the public—Tindara—is in blocks that
are costing the settler from £4 2s. fid. to £7 per acre,
unfenced and unimproved. Yet in May a well-improved
farm of 320 acres, with house and outbuildings, in Tin-
dara district, was sold for £5 10s. an acre, and it is three
miles nearer the station than the Closer Settlement
blocks. Another farm, slightly improved and but a mile
further from the line, brought only £3 per acre. Without
a land tax, the more the Board buys the more it has to
pay • and then it can only let the settler in by passing
the steadily increasing bundle on to him.

JJrom the offices Oldest Inhabitant: Mr,
J. S. Butters resurrects himself in Melbourne Argus re
Johnson’s Reef, Bendigo. The co. was floated, he says,
bv Melbourne’s first firm of mining brokers, Baillie and
Blitters. The proposition was brought to B. and B.’s
office bv J. H. Knipe, then of Bendigo, whose heiress
grand-daughter is being fought over and pursued in the
law courts. The early owners of the adjoining claims
were Thorp , Earlv, Chappie, Abbott, and Murdock,
whose ground had produced £90,000 worth of gold in IS
months. From these claim-holders Knipe obtained 200
specimens of milk-white quartz, blazing with nuggets,
“ the finest exhibit ever made in Melbourne.” They were
placed in the showcases in the Collins-street windows
on the day the prospectus was issued, with the
result that, by 3 o’clock in the afternoon, the
office door had to be shut, to prevent any more
applications coming in. Still, applications were put into
the letter box and under the door by scores, and over
£200,000 in cash was received. The hon. sec. was
G. h’. Knowles ; but subsequently Howard Spensley, who
had recently arrived in the State, was appointed legal
manager. This Howard Spensley, who had studied for
the Bar, entered Parliament and became Gay an Duffy s
Solicitor-General; he married Simon Staughton s daughter,
with a pile of money, built the Bourke-street Arcade
therewith, and afterwards went to London and entered
the House of Commons. J. S. Butters himself was a
notable character. He was in the Legislative Assembly,
and left it along with C. E. Jones. But both got
back to the Assembly again. Butters was Maypr of
Melbourne in the Duke of Edinburgh’s year. In 1870 he
and his partner Baillie went to Fiji, and when Thakambau
started a Parliament and Executive Council, Butters
became Speaker and drew up the Standing Orders The
Parliament o Thakambau was abolished when Fiji was
annexed. Baillie was murdered in Fiji-a murder mystery
still. Butters returned permanently to Victoria in 1874
and again entered Parliament, this time in the House of
Rich Landlords. The warrior is now in his 76th year. He
arrived in Melbourne in 1853, and founded the stock-
broking business in 1806. #

Quan : When the Judders have fastened
prohibition on Australia, I’m going to set up as a vendor of
American patent medicines. And 111 boom. .ruck by
the enormous increase in the sale of patent medicines in
Massachusetts (a prohibition State), the local Board of
Health had 61 well-known and widely-patronised tonics,
bitters, and other proprietary medicines analysed. All
were found to contain alcohol, the proportion ranging
from 6 to 47J per cent., while the average was over 20 per
cent. Nine of the 61 were described in terms indicating
that they contained no alcohol. Yet analysis of these
revealed that (1), “not a rum drink,” contained 13'2
per cent.; (2), “purely vegetable, recommended for
inebriates,” 41'6 ; (3), “ entirely harmless, 19'5 ; (4), “ not
an alcoholic beverage,” 6 ; (5), “ entirely vegetable and
free from alcoholic stimulant,” 25'6 ; (6), “contains no
alcohol,” 20'5 ; (7), “ contains no spirit,” 6'l ; (8), “a
non-intoxicating stimulant,” whisky without its sting,”
28*2 ; and (9), “ recommended for treatment of alcoholio
habit,” 26 - 5.
The rascalities of the short-weight wood-and-
coal dealer are troubling Melbourne again. Instances
are given of 601 b. being short out of 3cwt., 151 b. in lewt.,
751 b. in 2cwt., and so on. Citizens who complain to the
negligent councils are invited to act as inspectors and
prosecutors themselves. Bent’s Parliament passed a law
strong enough to absolutely stop this species of petty
larceny and then left it to the councils to carry out.
Three central municipalities—where Laborites hold a few
seats—have joined together to pay an inspector ; the
rest wholly ignore the matter. • It becomes evident that
if such laws are to be left to the average councillor to
carry out, he will have to be instructed to do the work
thorou = hly ’ and be fin , ed if he doe # snx

O.K.Y. : The SUNDAY Slimes is giving pic-
tures of the beautitul homes of N.S.W. lor the
most part, they are fearsome monstrosities in bricks
an( i mortar. The typical house of the Calico
Jimmy in Sydney suburbs is apparently a quarter-
acre roof of French tiles, supported by brick railway
arches, under the gloom of which lurk little windows fit
for prison cells, and a doorway large enough for a fair-
sized dog. It is into dungeons of this brand that Jat
retires after the beat and burden of the day, and within
he lies low until next morning. Our true home, for
climatic reasons, should be a dwelling surrounded by
wide verandahs, each room with windows extending
from floor to ceiling*. Queen Anne is dead, and her style
o{ R enne i should be dead, too.

. Rubber-growing enterprises are being boomed
in London and Paris on the strength of some pioneer cos.
figures. On paper the profits are too rich to be taken
without apple sauce. Cost ot planting (with tropical
colored labor, ot course) is set down at about £lO per
acre, and subsequent attention at £2 per acre. In the
seventh year returns start to come in, and run from any-
thing, to begin with, up to £2OO per acre per annum when
the trees are mature. If the figures are within miles of
the truth, there ought to be something in it for Queens-
land, and especially for 1 apua. At Kamerunga, the State
nursery back from Cairns, nothing seems to thrive better

Mercator : Now that Australian goods are
getting their name up, shoddy stuffs are seeking sale
under the masquerade of Australian. Was passing a
shop when I was attracted by a pile of blankets, sur-
mounted by an aggressive notice— These fane quality
colonial blankets are being absolutely thrown away,
Being interested in the manufacture of Australian
blankets, a cursory glance told me that the goods were of
the poorest English make, being very largely cotton, and
calculated to make the lowest quality Australian blanket
blush for shame. Until the States extend the JJederal
Commerce Act provisions against such dishonesty there
seems no remedy,
THE MAIDS OF THE LANE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Melbourne softgoods firm has made an innovation in
pushing its wares by engaging a couple of lady com-
mercial travellers. It is anticipated that other firms will
follow, and the lady traveller will shortly be a common
'eature of the road.—News item.
The poet, with figments and fancies,
Is fading away ;
For Commerce the greatest romances
Develops to-day.
Where the bustle and push of the trade is
The youthful gods reign,
And have for their Hebes, the ladies
With lines from the Lane.
With trains full of damsels who travel,
Just purely for biz,
The joj r that the bushies now have’ll
Be twice what it is.
They’ll gather on platform and siding
To hail in glad terms
The cars where, in beauty, are riding
The girls from the firms.
Do they understand what the lore is
Attached to the craft?
Do they know all those smoking-room stories
At which we have laughed ?
Will each try to stay up the later,
Love’s fictions to tell,
While cuddling the prettiest waiter
At every hotel ?
The youth who knows not her existence,
When counting his cash,
Will watch her approach from a distance
And hope for a mash.
But e’er his words come, she will start hers
And give him a shock,
By offering lovelier garters
Than he has in stock.
Before he’s come out of his faint, he
Will suddenly know
That something in lingerie dainty
She’s anxious to show.
All thoughts in a whirl will be flying,
Confused in his mind,
Till the trucks come from Melbourne supplying
The orders ;he’s signed.
With books bulging stoutly, they’ll tarry
Not long where they go,
Until they retire and they marry
The head of the co.
No time for coquetry or dalliance,
O’er peak and o’er plain,
They sweep in their fairy battalions—
The maids of the Lane.
N.S.W.
A.B.


MICK. SIMMONS
HaymarKct, Sydney, N.,S\W.

INDIGESTION,
The torments which are suffered when the digestive organs fail to do their work thoroughly are, ;
unfortunately, so well known to most of us that it is not necessary for the various symptoms ot indi-
gestion or dyspepsia to be given in detail here. ■ ’
Indigestion has several sources of origin, the principal being Gastric Catarrh, in which the food
becomes covered with mucous, and consequently resists the action of the digestive piices , Gastuc
Flatulency, the development of wind or gas; Gastric Diarrhoea, caused by the food,
through the digestive organs unassimilated, and Constipation. Constipation is by far ■the mo§|
frequent and obstinate cause of indigestion, and it must be rectified or digestion cannot be otherwis.
than imperfect. , A
Food must be eaten in sufficient quantity, and must be digested, and be convertMl into l.loodj
Nature makes this one of her most imperative laws of life. During the process of digestionl food 1S
entirely changed in composition by the action of the juices of the internal organ, t g ,
passes. In the mouth it is thoroughly mixed with the saliva. Then it is swallowed, and enjerstbe
stomach, where it is acted upon by the gastric juices, and becomes partly liquefied. *
it passes to the smaller intestine-which is about 18 feet in length—and there certain tg
food are liquefied by the bile and other juices. The food thus made fluid is in a condition to be
absorbed into and becomes a constituent part of the blood. -The indigestible portion of the f
discharged into the larger intestine, whence it is m turn expelled from the body together with other
refuse matter. _ . >
Just as certainly as that it is necessary to life that food must be absorbed, so, likewise, lt-is
essential that the blood must be in a condition to absorb the food, lorpidity of the liver is the c
cause of nearly every case of indigestion, and when the liver is torpid the kidneys are al y.
sympathetically affected to a greater or lesser extent. The blood, which should be transformed,
cleansed and filtered by the kidneys and liver, then contains urinary and biliary poisons, and is there-
fore a feeble absorbent of nutriment. This condition of the blood reacts upon the nervous- system qf
the digestive organs, and prevents the flow and alters the quality of the digestive juices. yJ
The entire nerve energy of a person suffering from indigestion is weakened, owing to the con-
taminated condition of the blood, and the general feeling of mental and physical depression, which is
experienced during an attack of dyspepsia, is due to this cause.
The blood must be continuously purified by the action of the liver and kidneys, Or good digestion
cannot be expected to occur.
Many sufferers from indigestion obtain temporary relief by eating predigested foods or taking
medicines, such as pepsin, which act as digestives in the intestines. A course of such treatment merely
encourages a slothful action of the digestive organs, and causes them to become gradually weaker; atia
less capable of performing their duty, just in the same way that a person who takes jittlepr
becomes incapable of responding to any demand for exertion. Other sufferers irritate the digestive
■organs into temporary and abnormal activity by taking purgative medicines so frequently tnat
presently the stomach and intestines refuse to act except under such irritating stimulation. Xhe w - 7
rational and permanent cure for indigestion is to create such a condition of the blood that eacn
corpuscle becomes hungry for food, and ready and eager to absorb it. The digestive secretions will
then respond to the demands of the blood, and the stomach and intestines will perform their work as
a matter of course. When the blood is laden with uric acid and other urinary and biliary poisons it
■cannot adequately absorb food, and makes but a feeble attempt to do so.
Warner’s Safe Cure J
Is not a purgative medicine. It permanently cures indigestion and dyspepsia, simply because it
restores the liver and kidneys to health and activity, so that the blood naturally; becomes tree trem
urinary and biliary poisons and ravenous to absorb nutriment freely. Nutriment is then conveyed by
the blood to the nerves throughout the body. The nerves of the digestive organs being; properly
nourished, the organs are in a condition to do their work efficiently. Nature is merely aided in mer
efforts to preserve a balance in the manifold and complex processes of waste and renewal by wnic 1
life is maintained.
Remember, constipation or irregularity of the bowels must be overcome. W arner s Safe Rills
should, therefore, also be taken in sufficient quantities to ensure one free movement daily.
Do not overload the stomach. Avoid tea, coffee, alcholic stimulants and rich or fat foods. Do
not eat generously of any solid food.

Flood.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MAN set a narrow bound for me,
With ordered willows, row on row,
And garden-slopes and lawns below ;
And marked me moving to the sea,
My placid wave untroubled, slow ;
Nor guessed beneath my even flow
The fettered spirit—fierce and free.
By glimmering beach and bay I wound
My listless length, a laggard tide,
So still my dreaming wavelets died
In soundless shallows ere they found
The rushes bn the river side.
My naked strength was all untried,
My strength that laughed at bar and bound.
Voice to a thousand things I gave—
The rustle in the water-weeds,
The ripple lisping in the reeds,
The plash of wild things in the wave,
The myriad sounds that silence breeds ;
Man knew not, buried in his needs,
My smiling mirror masked a grave.
Storm darkened on the solemn hills,
The brooding skies were black with rain,
That swept across the cowering plain,
And drowned the flowers and gorged the rills,
And trampled down the ripening grain,
As sudden as the hurricane,
Whose breath is vital while it kills.
It swept me thick as swarming bees,
My dwindling currents drank and drank,
Until I brimmed from bank to bank,
And lapped the willow’s gnarled knees;
And, as I rose, 1 leneath me sank
My bordering rushes, rank on rank ;
My marshes widened into seas.
Full-fed and foaming, swift as flame,
Oame vassal waters from the hills,
And every creek that flows and fills.
And trickling threads without a name,
The melancholy marsh distills
And slowly brims and slowly spills,
To me, its lord, with tribute came.
Then rose I roaring, wave on wave,
Maddened, exulting, ’neath the lash,
And swifter than the lightning flash
1 burst my bonds, no more a slave.
Man built his barriers ; vain and rash !
They fell before me crash on crash ;
I whelmed them in a seething grave.
The dams they deemed so stout and staunch,
I leaped them all and wrenched away
With roars of triumph stone and stay ;
1 snatched the trailing willow branch,
And tossed it in Titanic play,
Till foam-sweat mingled with my spray,
Resistless as the avalanche.
My tawny torrents tossed in glee
Their foaming crests, and laughed to feel
The stately'arches'rock""and" reel,
And roared a thunderous litany
Thro’ blocks of stone and stays of steel,
Deep as a swelling organ peal,
The chant of triumphs of the free.
My fretted, fettered soul had found
That freedom lay beyond the span
Of bridge and bar, and brooked no ban.
I flooded field and fen, and drowned
'The shores as far as eye could scan; ■
Fierce as a ravening wolf I ran,
A watery waste without a bound.
Storm lifted from the hills afar
Her trailing mantle touched with light,
And forth in splendor came the night,
The heavens opened star on star.
The high moon sailed serene and white
Over the valley and the height,
The waves fell hushed upon the bar.
But far and wide, a shoreless sea,
My lawless waves unbridled flowed,
O’er lane and field and winding road ;
And that wild soul the floods set free,
When I rose, maddened, to the goad
. Of roaring rains, and seaward strode,
Shall nevermore be quelled in me.
• MAOKILAND.
MAUD PEACOCKE.
The Shepherd Kings of
Canterbury.
THE LADY OF “OCEAN CAVES.”
[For Tub Bulletin.]
I WAS laid up with rheumatism, and the
long day dragged wearily. I had cast the
illustrated papers aside, and had at last
settled down to seeing pictures in the fire. My
mother, looking out of the window, remarked
that Mr. Holmby had a very wet day for his
funeral, and it occurred to me that Mr. Holmby
might, after all, be quite indifferent to the fact.
It was to have taken place at three p.m., and
the hour hand of the big cuckoo clock was
approaching the figure six. I was glad it had
rained, for the wet would assuredly drive Edward
Forrester in ; and once Forrester had two whiskies
nothing on earth would stop him telling a yarn.
And I liked Forrester’s yarns.
At last I heard his voice in the hall. He poked
his nose in at the door, and assured me that it was
worth having rheumatism to escape helping
to bury Squire Holmby.
“Not that the old squire didn’t make a very
picturesque exit. Few people have gone to
Valhalla with such pomp and frill as he did to-
day,’’ continued Forrester, stirring the whisky
punch cautiously. “It was not the stereotyped
pomp, but a new brand all his own, and almost
worth the inconvenience of watching it. We
left Deepwater at nine o’clock. We were to have
got away before eight, but there was such a crowd
that we had to breakfast in relays, and we started
neai’ly an hour late. It was a perfect morning,
and as we trotted along, up out of the l’olling
tussock came groups and knots of horsemen, and
dropped into the cortege. The majority were in
the dark blue uniform of the Canterbury Yeo-
manry, and very fine they looked. The squatter
was coffined in red rimu, covered with a Union
Jack, and rode on a gun carriage.
“ When we-were about 15 miles from Geraldine
I saw a small torpedo-shaped cloud rush suddenly
out from behind Four Peaks, and then whole
squadrons came sailing from between the peaks of
the Southern Alps, and in a few minutes the sky
was overcast. Muttering thunder ran round the
hills, red gleams showed momentarily, till with
a blinding flash and a deafening boom of thunder,
the rain fell in a vast sheet. We trotted on
through it, the bugles wailing spasmodically,
and what a long dreary two hours it was before
we caught a glimpse of the church spire of
Geraldine showing over the trees !
“ The bugles were answered, and when we came
down to the long, white bridge there were two
lines of men, all in the Yeomanry uniform, from
the bridge to the gate of the cemetery. Well,
just as the Squire crossed the bridge that infernal
downpour ceased, and the sun pierced a shaft
through the rolling inky mass, and shone down
full on the squatter’s coffin. Very slowly the
gun carriage climbed the hill on which the ceme-
tery is, and that strange unearthly sunlight
streamed right down upon it. It was a most
weird effect ! The massed bands of all Central
and Southern Canterbury were wailing the Dead
March from 4 Saul.’ The grave was waiting, and
the deluge had filled it with water, so that the
coffin floated. The Freemasons were gathered
round the grave, and they cast little sprigs of
green in on the squatter. The Shepherd Kings
heaped him over with white and purple lilies ;
old Brown, the sexton, poured clods upon him,
and then over the yellow mound they heaped
more lilies in the weird sunlight from the break
in the inky clouds. The Yeomanry fired three
farewell volleys. Then the clouds closed sud-
denly, and a fresh downpour sent them scurrying
to the pubs.
“You know Squatter Holmby didn’t deserve
all this frill. He was the incarnation of uncon-
scious selfishness, and with a lot of people that
stands for real greatness. He was apparently
devoid of all those affections and emotions that
go to make up normal humanity. An insatiable
desire for wealth, for which he had no use except
as a means to acquire still more wealth, and a
fearful devouring regard for what he called his
4 honox - ,’ were his chief characteristics. He made
much wealth, and founded many stations—and he
drove his wife to another man to find affection
and companionship.
* * * *
“ The reason why we were all taught to do him
extra reverence was because of his smashing of Te
Rouparaha. The Maori Napoleon, or Ghengis
Khan, or whatever his admirers like to call the
bloodthirsty brute, came raging down through
Marlborough after the Devil, his father, had
assisted him across Cook Strait, having a regular
carnival of murder and cannibalism. When he
came within sight of Kaiapoi Pah, the greatest
fortified post in all Maoriland, he was aghast at
the strength of the place. Moreover, old Tai-
aroa, with all the southern Maoris, lay on the
edge of the swamp ready to attack his flank the
moment he attacked the Pah. Te Roup, hauled
off and began to temporise. Taiaroa and
he got negotiating ; and Taiaroa got the worst of
the deal, for Te Roup, humbugged him, and sud-
denly assaulted and carried Kaiapoi before the
bamboozled Taiaroa could get through the swamp
to assist it. The slaughter was awful, and Te
Roup, ’s friends dined on roast baby that night.
Next morning they fell on Taiaroa with the fury
of tigers, and the southerner was crumpled all up.
As the two armies went thundering down the
Plains, killing and being killed, the northern
Maoris came across flocks of strange animals
which were almost as good eating as the people of
Otago. They were Holmby’s specially imported
merinos and crossbreds.
44 Three days later Te Roup, was astounded to-
find himself attacked in the rear by a small band
of mounted white men, led by a youngish man
with a beard the color of a tussock. This was
Edward Holmby, and such men as he could
raise, and they didn’t number more than. 70, all
told. But they had caught Te Roup, in a bad
spot; his crowd was struggling across the shingle
reaches of the bed of the lower Rangitata when
the white men rode down on them and opened fire
at 50yds. All day long the roar of battle went up
between the shingle cliffs, and drowned even the
crashing of the boulders. Hour after hour the
white men rode down on the rushing Maoris and
shot them like dogs, wheeled, galloped away, re-
loaded, and came again. And between every
shingle drift were brown corpses. At last the
Maoris fled; 'but there was no shelter. Behind
them thundered the terrible horsemen, and they
withered away under the deadly rifle fire till they
were glad to beg mercy on their knees. What
did the great Chief of the Golden Beard desire of
his brown children ?
“The Great Chief of the Golden Beard was
brief and emphatic :
44 4 You can kill and eat one another till you’re
damned well tired, but our sheep are sacred, and
tapu are their lives and wool; neither may they be
disturbed. Get out! ’
“And they got out, quickly. And here endeth
the greatness of Edward Holmby.
• * *
“For the next 16 years Holmby went on accu-
mulating wealth and piling station upon station,
till, like the old Scotch county of Cromarty, his
territory was all over the country. . Finally, he
settled down at Deepwater, and, of all things,
married. The unfortunate lady was Miss Doyle,
daughter of Esmeralda station. She went to live
at Deepwater, where, faint and far, on stormy,
nights, could be heard the ci’ash and boom of the
South Pacific on the shingle bow of Ninety Mile
Beach. Fora pastime she turned the place into a
wilderness of roses, for her husband was never at
home ; he spent his life roaming round his various
properties, auditing books, inspecting works and
sheep, and supervising expei’iments to determine
the relationship between soil and wool. Usually
his tall, gray-eyed wife, who, by the way, had
beautiful brown hair, saw him about every half-
year. Deepstream was the only station he didn’t
audit and inspect carefully; and he gave as a
reason for it that Ambrose Mort, the manager,
was a fool, who hadn’t brains enough to do any-
thing but what he was told.
“Ten miles from Deepstream homestead the
shingle beach gives way to an irruption of rocks,
honeycombed with a series of curious caves.
Through some of them at high tide the waves roll
thunderously, but Others are above even the
spring tides, and remarkably dry. There are also
patches of singing Muds, and the whole region is
wild and romantic. Stretching from this rocky
shore a tract of yr'onderfully fertile soil runs
inland. Holmby had for years tried to buy it,
but someone in the Lands Office always baulked
him. He could get the leasehold, but not even
bribery secured the freehold.
44 When Mrs. Holmby heard that there were
singing sands at Ocean Oaves she becam greeatly
interested, and frequently drove out there in the
mornings, returning in the evenings just in time
for dinner, at which meal Ambrose Mort always
joined her.
“ One day Holmby returned to Deepwater, to
find that the lease of that wonderfully fertile
strip had been cancelled, and the land sold to the
Ocean Caves Syndicate, Ltd. At first he was
very angry, but suddenly he remarked :
“ ‘That's good ; we will buy that syndicate out,
later on, Mort ! ’
“ ‘ Have you got all of ours off, and have the
others been put on ? ’
“ ‘ I got all ours off immediately I got notice of
the sale, but I am unable to say if the others
have been put on.’
“ Mr. Holmby blew his whistle, and when the
groom appeared he was sent to ascertain if the
new owners’ stock had been put on the strip. He
was back inside two hours with the information
that two men with guns had ordered him off,
threatening him with arrest as a trespasser if he
came again. He had threatened them with Mr.
Holmby’s displeasure and then the groom
stopped.
“ ‘ Well, what happened then ? You were quite
right. I sent you. Of course they apologised ! ’
“ ‘ No, sir, they didn’t; they said they’d bundle
you off too, if you came.’
“ ‘ Had they been drinking?’
“ 4 Well, sir, I suppose they must have been,
though they didn’t show any signs that way.’
“ ‘Very well, you-can go now. I’ll deal with
them later on.’
* ■* * *
“Next day the great Edward Holmby himself
was ordered off the Ocean Caves Station, under
pain of being instantly arrested. He dared them
to arrest him. But the man coarsely assured
him they wouldn’t take the trouble, and there-
upon smote the squatter’s horse over the hind
quarters with his hunting crop, and Mr. Holmby
had to go, willy nilly. When he did manage to
pull the brute up, he considered the question, and
saw that Ocean Caves had as much right to keep
him off its property as he had to keep them from,
inspecting the new pigsties he had just put up.
“A few nights later he came home to dinner
and found it served in his library, where he
always retired immediately after the meal to read.
He ate his dinner, rang for the maid to clear, and
settled down with book and pipe. As was his
wont, he smoked and read till far into the morn-
ing, and then he rang the bell fiercely till the
cook, who knew his habit, served grilled kidneys.
“ ‘ Ask Mrs. Holmby if she would care to join
me ! ’ he said to the sleepy servant.
“ 4 Mrs. Holmby did not return home last night.
She went away early, and has not come back.’
“Mr. Holmby looked at her with a pained
expression.
44 4 Why did you not let me know? Tell Mr.
Mort I wish to see him ! ’
“‘Mr. Mort’s away too—he was driving, and
they’ve neither of them come back. As Mrs.
Holmby took so much luggage, I thought she had
gone to Christchurch, sir ! ’
“‘O, very well,’ and Mr. Holmby went on
with his reading.
“The cook went, and, no doubt, made many
bright and original remarks in the kitchen. Mr.
Holmby went on reading for a while, and then
he went down to the office, and started to over-
haul Mort’s boolcs, and daylight found him still
examining them.
“At the end of three days’ careful study of
Ambrose Mort’s books, Mr. Holmby suddenly
gave orders that the Whole of the employees were
to assemble at the office. When they arrived
there he swore them in as special constables,
armed them, and started for Ocean Caves home-
stead. As usual, they were met by the two arnled
shepherds, who discreetly retired in the face of
superior force, after sending up a cloud of smoke.
Mr. Holmby pushed on to the station. The
homestead was no better than a cottage, but
round it, in the shape of a great mass of newly-
planted rose cuttings, he recognised his wife’s
handiwork.
“ Leaving his men at the bottom of the garden,
Holmby rode up to the verandah, and tapped the
post with his hunting-crop. He was answered by
a maid in cap and streamers, who said 1 that Mr.
and Mrs. Mort lived there, and when the gushing
young woman brought out a photo of Mrs. Mort,
Holmby recognised his wife, and knew that what
he suspected was true. Fortunately for them, the
maid did not know in which direction Mr. and
Mrs. Mort were to be found that day, and before
they returned Holmby was back at Deepstream.
“On hearing that her husband had called on her,
‘Mrs. Mort ’ became terrified. She knew that Mort
was outnumbered, and in any conflict must be
beaten. He was not even a Justice of the Peace,
and that was a heavy handicap. What she
feared was that Holmby would take her back to
Deepwater by main force and practically make a
prisoner of her. Having once called and ascer-
tained where she was, she felt certain Holmby
would come again, though in the beginning she
had hoped he would wash his hands of her, and
devote himself more strenuously to the making of
money and determining of the relationship be-
tween soil and wool.
“As a matter of fact, it was not Mrs. Holmby,
nor his outraged honor, that the great Chief of the
Golden Beard was worrying about. It was that
wonderfully fertile strip of ground that Mort had
jockeyed him out of. That was his trouble.
With cultivation it would run 15 sheep to the
acre, and the loss of it broke Holmby’s heart, and
al l his letters to his f riends at that time were filled
with references to 1 this terrible blow, the loss of
this splendid land !’ His wife was hardly men-
tioned.
‘ ‘ Mort decided that the homestead was no longer
a safe place for the lady, and she flatly refused to
allow him to stay in it unless she was with him.
She feared violence at the hands of the hero of
Rangitata Reach, and at last persuaded Mort to
take some furniture and provisions down to one
of the caves. He took them secretly in a Yankee
waggon, under cover of the night, and‘Mrs. Mort’
selected the driest cave, and made it into a rather
comfortable chamber. Every evening, after
dinner, they used to ride down to the caves and
sleep there, riding back to breakfast in the morn-
ing. The employees were warned that there was
danger in the air, and on no account to run risks
by trying to defend the house should it be
attacked.
“The fury of a woman scorned was gentle raillery
compared to the rage of an old-time squatter done
out of choice land, and ‘Mrs. Mort’s’ intuition was
correct. Mr. Holmby did sweep down one wild
night when a nor’-wester was starting to blow,
firing tussock as he came. The servants at Ocean-
Caves faithfully obeyed orders and made for
safety, and from a distance watched the dark,!

KIDNEY TROUBLE affects PEOPLE DIFFERENTLY
Though no two persons alike are
affected in precisely the same way by
kidney disorder, the danger is always
equally great. One man may suffer
from pains in the back and loins, or
from rheumatism —another may have
urinary troubles, and his eyes may be
puffy and swollen; one woman may
notice heart irregularity, she may be
sleepless, nervous, and despondent —
her neighbour may have all the
symptoms of dropsy or Bright’s disease.
It depends on the sufferer’s constitution
and the nature of the impurities kept
back in the body by the blood filters —
the kidneys.
Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills
Should be taken at the FIRST
sign of anything wrong. ■ .
They are the reliable medicine for
every disorder caused directly or in-

PHIS.
•
gliding figures in the glare of the burning tussock
as they burned the homestead. They saw
squatter Holmby sitting on his horse, watching it
go up in smoke and sparks, and then saw him
ride off again towards Deepwater.
“The sanctuary at Ocean Caves was a dead
secret. By midnight the howling nor’-wester
suddenly ceased. A curious calm fell over the
land, and a horrible feeling of something about to
happen, as the medical advertisement says, fell
on the houseless servants of Ocean Caves. They
longed for the dawn and the reappearance of their
master, who would start to reorganise things.
“Far away from the North came a rumbling
sound, and the earth heaved gently. It was not
a shock, just a gentle heave, a feeling that a wave
had swept under the solid earth and gently forced
it up, and all over Canterbury the shingle ter-
races rustled musically.
“Down at Ocean Caves the sleepers felt the
gentle heave, and were awakened by the crash of
falling rock. Then the stillness lulled them
again.
“ When they arose next morning, Mort was hor-
rified to find that exit was cut off by a mass of
rock that had crashed down across the entrance.
Being of a philosophical mind, he straightway
began to wonder whether it was fortunate or
otherwise that the supply of air was uninter-
rupted. If they were discovered in time, it would
be good ; if no one came along that way before
starvation did its work, it would be a misfortune.
At the station, the people waited vainly, and,
when midday came without any sign of the squat-
ter, his people concluded that he had fallen into
the hands of Holmby and been murdered. They
erected shelters for the maids, and, while some of
the men killed mutton, which was all they had
to eat, others started off to Esmeralda station to
acquaint ‘Mrs. Mort’s’ people with what had hap-
pened.
“ The Doyles were shocked at their sister’s con-
duct, but, all the same, they weren’t going to
stand by tamely while her roof was burned over
her head and her lover shot. For the hands had
represented that Mort had been shot and the lady
carried off prisoner.
“Next morning, Squatter Holmby, who was
beginning to wonder if he had not overdone it,
received an armed deputation. The Doyles
arrived in full force and demanded an account of
their sister. Holmby assured them she was with
Mort. ‘ But where is Mort?’ they queried.
‘ He’s not been seen since you burned him out.
Neither has she. We’re going to rake the ruins,
and if we find anything, you’ll swing, as sure as
your name is Edward Holmby.’
“ Holmby sat back and breathed hard. Was it
possible that Mort and Emily had been burned in
their home. Once the hands knew that, they
would probably turn on him. In fact, he knew
they would—the obedience of even Canterbury
hands of the feudal period didn’t run to burning
women alive. Squatter Holmby waited till the
Doyles had disappeared beyond the home pad-
docks, and then he mounted a horse, the fastest
in the stable, and galloped wildly away towards
Ocean Beach. His saddle bristled with fire-
arms. He did not believe his wife and Mort were
dead, but the danger was that the Doyles might
find something in the ashes that would lead them
to believe she was—and Holmby felt his throat
tenderly.
“ Well, it seems that the cats had not escaped,
or some of the pets had been burned, for some
tiny fragments of charred bones were discovered.
They didn’t bear the least resemblance to human
bones, but the Doyles were excitable young men.
Ingram Doyle burst into tears and hoarsely
called on his brothers to follow him and avenge
thpir murdered sister.
" In a few minutes the whole of them were gal-
loping back to Deepwater. As they approached
the station they expected to find preparations for a
fight; but there were none, and the people seemed
considerably scared at their appearance.
“ They rushed into the house and demanded
Holmby, dead or alive. He could not be found.
One of the men told how he had ridden away
bristling with firearms. He opined that the
squatter had taken refuge in one of the caves
down where the singing sands were. There one
man, while he remained awake, could defy an
army. . .
“‘Take us to these caves immediately, said
Ingram Doyle. ‘ Saddle up the best horse in his
stable, and show the way. You can’t sympathise
with a ruffian who burns a woman in her bed,
surely ! ’
“ The man, who had probably borne a hand m
the firing, shuddered as he reprobated any con-
nection with such horrors. In five minutes he
was leading the way to Ocean Caves. It was
afternoon when they arrived there, and searching
around for tracks Ingram Doyle heard someone
cooee. At first some of the men who knew the
romantic reputation of the place said it was a
good locality to get out of, but the Doyles went
on searching. Again that faint cooee. They
tried to locate it, but it was an hour before they
did, and then it took the whole party, using gun-
barrels and driftwood from the beach below as
levers, to roll the mass away and rescue the couple
within.
* * * *
“Squatter Holmby escaped safely to the North
Island, after a terrible journey. He made his
way on horseback along the sea-coast, right
up through Canterbury, across Banks’ Peninsula
and through Marlborough, under the impression
that he was being hotly pursued, and that detec-
tives would be waiting for him at Christchurch.
It was only when he was preparing to make a
dash for New Caledonia, or Chili, or somewhere
in a small yacht that he heard that his wife was
alive and well, and that his terrific journey was
wasted effort, owing to the fact that he was never
pursued. He eventually made peace by obtaining
a divorce from his wife, and rebuilding the home-
stead at Ocean Caves. Old Mort was down at
Geraldine to-day. He has buried the hatchet.”
C. A. JEFFRIES.
The Trap.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE trap and I were chums—that is, we
used to fish together on Sundays off the
rocks, and used to confide in each other as
far as we thought it advisable.
There was an old fellow named Jerry over the
mountain, and he made a drop of grog. There
were, I believe, many stills over that way, but I
was only sure of Jerry’s. The stuff was made
mostly of bran and sugar. White it was, and
strong as sheep wash —would burn a hole in almost
anything. I took a small schnapps bottle of it
into a cowyard one morning, and the smell of it
caused a riot.
The trap was always watching the mountain
for the smoke of illicit stills, but I don’t fancy he
often spotted them. The stills mostly operated
on foggy days. He didn’t care too much about
poking around after the mountain distillers in
person, because a gun, pointed by an unseen
hand, might go off any time ; or a lot of other
things might happen in the gullies.
Old Jerry had an agent in the town. A lame
man the agent was, and always got about on
crutches. I don’t know rightly how he got rid of
the stuff, but I had a notion that a lot of it
reached Sydney in milk-cans, same as it does to
this day. When I felt like it, I bought all I re-
quired from the agent at eighteen pence per
square face, and he did a pretty considerable
trade locally. Nearly all the J’s.P. were cus-
tomers of his. People used to be surprised at
the number of loony-looking men who mooched
around town—and men who were never seen in-
side a pub, at that. When you see a big per-
centage of loony characters in any community,
vou can swear there is a still handy. The J’s.P.
were all loony ; but that didn’t signify.
As I said before, the trap and I were friends.
One day, over a couple of beers, he told .me, in
confidence, how it had been reported to him that
Jerry was expected down the mountain that
night with a load of stuff. He was going to ride
out about 11 o’clock, and land him, and he wanted
me to accompany him; but I didn’t happen to
have time. I had important business in town,
and told him so. People, the trap explained, had
been complaining a lot about Jeremiah and his
cart.
The old hoppy agent, who went round on
crutches, couldn’t ride for sour apples, but he
always had a fast horse stabled, and something
had to be done in a hurry—a real fast hurry. I
didn’t trust the agent much, and told him no
details. But, at 9 o’clock that night, I was riding
for the mountain as fast as Hoppy’s horse could
move under list. I met Jerry right enough about
a quarter of a mile from the top, with some 50
gallons of fire-water, and warned him. Jerry’s
language was beautiful.
Ten minutes later, I was bound for town, fear-
ful that I might meet the trap on the road. I
reckoned lie must be on the road somewhere.
There was no getting across country there, so I
rode hard and hoped for all I was worth, for I had
a character to maintain and a billet to keep. But
my luck was out. * Long before I got to the cross-
roads, where I meant to diverge and get out of the
way. of trouble, I heard the clatter of a horse
coming my way. It made a row that any sensible
trap, out looking for a malefactor, should have
been ashamed of.
There was neither bush nor gutter anywhere
handy that I could dodge into until he passed,
and I was reckless. When I got within 50 yards
of him, I spread myself down along my mount,
bushranger fashion, and made a dash for it.
I could never just remember passing him, but
I have dim recollections of being called upon to
stand in the Queen’s name, and of hearing shots
and curses, and sounds of pursuit. But l rode
like the wind, and presently the enemy was hull
down on the horizon.
When the trap met old Jerry, he went through
his cart, but found nothing under the load of
sorghum. Jerry afterwards said that the trap’s
language was disgraceful.
I don’t mind telling you now that I had an in-
terest in that grog-manufacturing concern ; and
that’s mostly why I rode so hard.
I often wondered why the trap let me into the
secret; but I discovered all about it some years
a ter, after he had retired from the force. He
was also one of the firm. And I might have
broken my neck saving his grog and helping him
to work up a reputation for vigilance.
YAERUM.
Rue.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN Night her own world make 9
apart,
And hushes noisy street and mart,
And stills the City’s throbbing heart—
When sleep walks through the city, free,
With shoes of silence silently—
My bitter Self comes home to me.
He will not be by force gainsaid ;
He has no fear of me, no dread
He lies beside me in my bed.
He will not let me rest; his eyes
Burn through my brain ; he pants and sighs
And evermore his poniard plies.
Between the hours of dark and day
Most people die, the wise folk say
I know the reason ; so do they.
Ah me ! The tender Moon that shone
Long, long ago my bed upon —
Where is she now ? Where has she gone ?
She used to come to me at Night,
And stroke my face with fingers white,
And whisper stories of delight.
“ Beyond the world, beyond the day,
There is a bright world far away !
Come, little stranger ! ” she would say.
“ This glad, green earth that now you see,
Which seems, by day, so full of glee,
Is not my world of wizardry.”
That tender golden moon of yore
Is dead ; I see her face no more,
Da Luan haivn ! Asthore! Asthore!
The lamplight through my chamber gleams;
I watch the clock, as one in dreams ;
The long hand like a dagger seems.
In vain I sigh, and turn, and start;
In vain are all the drugs of art —
That dagger strikes into my heart.
Good friends I had in days gone by—
No man had more beneath the sky—
Good friends I have—and what am I ?
I gave to them, for follies wild,
The small excuses of a child,
And they, with fond forgiveness, smiled.
When comes the last dread Reckoning Day,
And all are judgi d—l humbly pray—
Dear God, be Thou as kind as they !
VICTOR J. DALEY.
